Rational Judgments
intermediate
7 min
The Desert Hostel and the Refutation of Analogy
Nasafi refutes anthropomorphic analogy using the hostel example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between human and divine acts fails in the case of foolishness, using Nasafi's desert hostel example.

Core Idea
What counts as foolish for humans does not apply to Allah.

Nasafi uses a concrete example of building a hostel in the desert to test the boundaries of what counts as foolishness. He shows that human actions are judged by personal benefit (praise/reward), but since Allah cannot benefit, the same standard cannot apply. This lesson unpacks that argument, showing why analogy between the witnessed and unseen must be carefully limited.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the example of the hostel and the reasoning about foolishness.
The passage begins with a reference to a hungry naked person; this is likely a continuation from a prior discussion, but the main focus is the hostel example.
2
Identify why the builder is not foolish
The builder is not considered foolish even though he does not live there, because he gains praise and reward. Find this reasoning in the text.
3
Examine the counterfactual scenario
The text imagines a scenario where the builder knows he will get no praise or reward, yet still builds. Then he is considered foolish. Understand why this changes the judgment.
This shows that personal benefit is crucial for humans.
4
Grasp the divine exception and conclusion
Allah does not benefit from His acts, yet His acts are not foolish. Therefore, the analogy from human to divine is invalid. See how the passage concludes about command, prohibition, and obligation.
The same principle applies to all divine attributes and actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجائع العاري، وكذا من بنى في مفازة من المفاوز رباطاً لا يعد سفيها، وإن كان من قصده أن لا يسكن هو لما أن غيره من المحتاجين ينتفعون به. قيل: إنما لم يعد هذا سفهًا لا لمكان انتفاع غيره بل لمكان انتفاع نفسه فإنه ينال به الثناء الحسن في الدنيا والثواب الموعود في الآخرة، حتى إنه لو بنى مع علمه أن أحدًا من الناس لا يعلم أنه هو الباني فلا يحصل له الثناء، وأخبره نبي من الأنبياء أنه لا ينال الثواب بذلك، ثم بنى مع هذا وأنفق فيه الأموال مع علمه أنه لا حمد له ولا أجر بذلك فإنه يعد سفيها، وإن كان يعلم أن غيره ينتفع به. والله تعالى أنه لا ينتفع بفعله لما مر من استحالة الانتفاع عليه لا يكون سفها، فدل أن اعتبار الغائب في هذا بالشاهد فاسد . فكذا في الأمر والنهي والتكليف.
The hungry naked person, and likewise one who builds a hostel in one of the deserts is not considered foolish, even if his intention is that he himself will not dwell in it, because others of the needy benefit from it. It was said: He is not considered foolish due to the benefit to himself, for he attains good praise in this world and the promised reward in the hereafter. So much so that if he built while knowing that none of the people know that he is the builder, so he does not attain praise, and a prophet of the prophets informed him that he would not attain the reward for that, then he built despite that and spent wealth therein while knowing that he would have no praise nor reward for that, then he would be considered foolish, even if he knew that others benefit from it. And God the Most High—He does not benefit from His act, due to what has passed of the impossibility of benefiting Him, so it is not foolishness. Thus it is shown that considering the unseen by analogy with the witnessed in this matter is invalid. Likewise in commanding, prohibiting, and obligation.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage begins with an example and then refutes an analogy between human and divine acts regarding foolishness (sufah). Nasafi presents the case of a person who builds a hostel in the desert (ribat) for the benefit of others; such a person is not considered foolish (safih) even if he himself does not intend to live there, because he gains praise and reward. The text then considers a scenario where he builds secretly and knows from a prophet that he will get no reward or praise—then he would be considered foolish, even though others benefit. From this, Nasafi argues that the human case hinges on personal benefit (even if indirect), but God cannot benefit from His acts. Therefore, what is considered foolish for humans (lack of personal benefit) cannot be applied to God. The conclusion is that the analogy between the witnessed (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib) in this context is false, and the same applies to command, prohibition, and obligation.

Key Terms
سفه
رباط
شاهد
غائب
حمد
ثواب
منفعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's acts must benefit Him to be considered wise; otherwise they would be foolish.
Correction: The passage shows that the criterion for foolishness in humans depends on personal benefit, but since Allah cannot benefit, that standard cannot be applied to Him. His acts are beyond such judgments.

Checkpoint
Why is a person who builds a hostel in the desert not considered foolish according to the initial example?
A) Because he personally benefits from praise and reward.
B) Because he does not spend his own money.
C) Because the building is for his own dwelling.
D) Because the act is commanded by a prophet.

Key Takeaway
The standard for foolishness in human acts involves personal benefit, but since Allah cannot benefit, that standard cannot be transferred to divine acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a trial in your life as meaningless because you couldn't see how it could possibly benefit you? How does this lesson's distinction between human and divine benefit challenge that reaction?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's actions are beyond human measures of foolishness, what would change about how you respond when life seems unfair? Will you commit to pausing before judging His decree?
?
Consider a situation where you sought praise or reward from people for a good deed. Does your motive rest more on human recognition or on the awareness that Allah does not need your act yet still commands it? What is one step you will take today to shift your intention solely towards Him?

Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
Commanding Benefit and Prohibiting Harm Are Not Foolish
Nasafi argues via analogy that commanding benefit and prohibiting harm is wise even if the commander does not benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's commands and prohibitions are considered wise even though He does not personally benefit from them.

Core Idea
Wisdom is about benefit to the commanded, not the commander.

Nasafi strengthens his case by drawing a parallel with ordinary human reasoning. If we consider a person who warns a blind man of a well to be wise, how can we deny the same wisdom to God? This lesson unpacks that analogy and its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Nasafi builds his argument: a rhetorical question, an analogy, a forced concession, then an application.
The passage uses the phrase 'فلا بد من بلى' which means 'there is no choice but to say yes'.
2
Understand the analogy
The analogy involves a blind man and a dangerous road. Why is the person who warns him not foolish? Identify the key point: the benefit goes to the blind man, not the warner.
This sets up the principle that an action can be wise even if the doer gains nothing.
3
Connect to God’s commands
Nasafi applies the analogy to God. Even though God does not benefit or suffer harm, His commands and prohibitions are for the benefit of servants, so they are wise, not foolish.
This refutes the opponent's claim that such acts would be pointless.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider the misconception: Some think an act without benefit to the agent is futile. The passage shows that benefit to others can justify the act as wise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول (۱) : أليس أن من أمر غيره بفعل ينتفع به المأمور أو نهاه عن فعل يندفع (۲) الضرر عن المنهي بأن نهى أعمى عن سلوك طريق فيه بئر لئلا يقع (۳) فيه، أو دخول بيت فيه أسد ضار، أو حية عظيمة لئلا يفترسه الأسد وتنهشه الحية لم يكن سفيها، فلا بد من بلى لما أنه لو أجاب بغيره لنادى على نفسه بالعناد والمكابرة. فقيل له: ما أنكرت أن (٤) أمر الله تعالى بما فيه للعباد منفعة ونهيه عما لهم (٥) فيه مضرة حكمة (٦) وليس بسفه وإن كان لا ينتفع هو به ولا تنتفي عنه المضرة (٦).
Then we say (1): Is it not that whoever commands another to do an action by which the commanded benefits, or prohibits him from an action by which harm is repelled from the one prohibited—such as prohibiting a blind person from taking a road in which there is a well, lest he fall into it, or from entering a house in which there is a dangerous lion or a large snake, lest the lion devour him and the snake bite him—he is not foolish. So there is no choice but to say yes, because if he answered otherwise he would call himself stubborn and obstinate. So it was said to him: What prevents you from acknowledging that God's command regarding what contains benefit for the servants and His prohibition regarding what contains harm for them is wisdom and is not foolishness, even if He does not benefit from it nor is harm repelled from Him?
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument in the form of a rhetorical question (أليس). Nasafi asks the opponent: if a person commands something beneficial to the commanded or prohibits something harmful to the prohibited (e.g., telling a blind man to avoid a well), that commander is not considered foolish. The opponent must agree (فلا بد من بلى), otherwise he would be stubborn. Then the conclusion is drawn: similarly, God's commands and prohibitions are for the benefit of the servants and are wise, not foolish, even though God does not benefit personally. The footnotes provide manuscript variant readings; the core argument remains clear.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
منفعة
مضرة
حكمة
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An action is only wise if the doer personally gains benefit or avoids harm.
Correction: The passage shows that an action can be wise if it benefits or prevents harm to someone else, as in the blind man example. Thus God's commands are wise for the benefit of servants.

Checkpoint
In the example of the blind man, why is the person who prohibits him from a dangerous road not considered foolish?
A) Because the prohibitor benefits from the prohibition.
B) Because the prohibition prevents harm to the blind man.
C) Because the blind man asked for the prohibition.
D) Because the prohibitor is a prophet.

Key Takeaway
An act is not necessarily foolish if the agent gains nothing; it can be wise if it benefits or prevents harm to others, as with God's commands to humanity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every command of Allah is for your own good, even when you cannot see the benefit? What would change in your obedience if you internalized this truth?
?
When you find yourself reluctant to obey a divine command or avoid a prohibition, do you pause to consider that it is protecting you from harm? How can you train your heart to welcome Allah's commands as acts of mercy?
?
If you knew that a command from Allah would bring you immense benefit in this life and the next, would you rush to obey it with joy? What is holding you back from that certainty right now?

Acts Are Foolish Only If Prohibited by an Authority
Nasafi defines foolishness using shahid and then applies to gha'ib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that an act is only considered foolish if it violates a prohibition from a superior authority, and that God cannot be subject to such authority.

Core Idea
Foolishness requires a prohibition; God has no superior to prohibit Him.

Nasafi continues his refutation by challenging the premise that sending messengers could be foolish. He argues that foolishness is defined by violating a prohibition from a higher authority. Since no authority exists over God, His act of sending messengers cannot be foolish. This lesson unpacks that logic from the witnessed world to the Unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting the key terms like سفه and نهي. Let the flow of the argument settle before analyzing.
2
Identify the claim
Locate where Nasafi states his position: that an act is foolish only if prohibited by an authority with power over the doer.
This appears in the first sentence after 'ثم نقول'.
3
Analyze the witnessed world (shahid)
Notice how Nasafi explains that in human experience, an act is foolish because it violates the command of the Creator. There is no other standard.
He gives examples of a ruler who commands something with no benefit.
4
Transfer to the Unseen (gha'ib)
Understand that this cause (prohibition by a sovereign) cannot apply to God because no one has authority over Him.
Thus, His acts cannot be foolish.
5
Review the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that the opponent's argument fails because it wrongly applies a human standard to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: إن من يذهب إلى أن إرسال الرسل في حيز الممكنات ولا يترقى عن ذلك إلى القول بكونها واجبة الوجود لا محالة، لا نسلم لكم أن فعلاً ما يكون سفها ما لم يكن منهيًا عنه من قبل من له ولاية الأمر والنهي، وكل تصرف أو فعل في الشاهد ما كان سفهًا إلا لكونه منهيًا عنه، وما كان فاعله سفيها إلا لارتكابه نهي خالقه، ولا نهي لأحد على الله تعالى لما يستحيل دخوله تحت نهي غيره، إذ لا ولاية لغيره عليه ولا قدرة، فلا يتصور كون فعله سفها، فكل من أمر غيره بما لا منفعة له فيه أو نهى عما لا مضرة له فيه أو أرسل رسولاً إلى من يكذب رسوله أو يقصده بمكروه أو فعل فعلاً لا منفعة له فيه كان سفيهًا، لأن صانعه نهاه عن هذه الأفعال فكان سبب كون الفعل سفهًا هذا هو، ويستحيل تعديته إلى الغائب فلم يكن فعلاً ما من الصانع سفها فلم يستقم منكم هذا الكلام.
Then we say: Whoever holds that sending messengers is among possibles and does not go beyond that to saying that they are necessarily existent, we do not concede to you that any act is foolishness unless it is prohibited by one who has the authority to command and prohibit. And every action or deed in the witnessed was foolish only because it was prohibited, and its doer was foolish only because he committed the prohibition of his Creator. And there is no prohibition for anyone against God the Most High, because it is impossible for Him to fall under the prohibition of another, since there is no authority over Him nor power. So it is impossible that His act be foolishness. So whoever commands another to something in which there is no benefit for him, or prohibits something in which there is no harm for him, or sends a messenger to someone who will lie about his messenger or intend harm to him, or does an act in which there is no benefit for him—he is foolish, because his Sovereign prohibited him from these acts. So the cause of the act being foolishness is this, and it is impossible to transfer it to the Unseen. So no act of the Sovereign is foolish, so this speech of yours is not sound.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument, introduced by 'ثم نقول'. Nasafi declares that he does not accept the opponent's claim about sending messengers being merely possible; rather, he argues that an act is only foolish if it is prohibited by a higher authority. In human terms (shahid), acts are foolish because they violate the command of the Sovereign (khaliq). Since no one has authority over God, His acts cannot be foolish. Nasafi then explains the human case: a person is foolish if he does something with no personal benefit or sends a messenger to one who will harm him—but this is because his Sovereign has prohibited such actions. That cause cannot be transferred to God (gha'ib). Therefore, the opponent's argument that God's sending messengers could be foolish is invalid.

Key Terms
سفه
نهي
ولاية
شاهد
غائب
إرسال الرسل
ممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if an act appears pointless (like sending messengers to people who reject them), it is automatically foolish, regardless of who does it.
Correction: Nasafi argues that foolishness is defined only in relation to a higher authority's prohibition. Since no authority exists over God, His acts cannot be judged by human standards of foolishness.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the sole cause of an act being considered foolish in the human context?
A) Lack of benefit to the doer.
B) Prohibition by the doer's Creator.
C) Harm caused to others.
D) Waste of resources.

Key Takeaway
An act is foolish only when it violates a prohibition from a superior; since God has no superior, His acts cannot be considered foolish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label an action as 'foolish' in your daily life, do you ever stop to ask: 'Foolish relative to whose command?' How does this lesson force you to reassess your own judgments of others' actions?
?
Every sin you commit is a direct violation of a prohibition from your Creator. Does that reality strike you as a heavy warning, or have you become numb to it? What would it take for you to feel the weight of that transgression?
?
This lesson shows that God can never be subject to any authority—but you are under His authority every moment. Are you living as if you are accountable to a Sovereign who has the right to command and prohibit? If not, what specific change can you make today to realign?

The Rational Status of Prophethood: Possible or Necessary?
Al-Nasafi defines the dispute and clarifies the meaning of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central dispute about the rational status of prophethood and what the term 'necessary' actually means in this context.

Core Idea
'Necessary' does not mean forced upon God, but assured by His wisdom.

Al-Nasafi begins by framing a key theological question: is prophethood rationally possible or necessary? He presents two camps and then clarifies a critical ambiguity. Understanding this distinction is essential for following the rest of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening question and the two positions
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the first group (most Ahl al-Hadith mutakallimun) says prophethood is possible, while the second group (those who affirm rational obligation) says it is necessary.
The second group primarily refers to the Mu'tazila.
2
Grasp the clarification of 'wajib'
The text explicitly states that 'necessary' does not mean an obligation upon God, but rather 'assured of existence' (متأكدة الوجود). This is a crucial nuance.
The analogy with God's knowledge helps: what God knows will exist is inevitably existent.
3
Understand the basis for assurance
Prophethood is assured because it is a requirement (مقتضى) of God's wisdom. Its non-existence would entail foolishness (سفه), which is impossible for God.
This reasoning connects divine attributes (wisdom) to the necessity of prophethood.
4
See the methodological shift
The author then says he will not repeat detailed proofs but will argue against deniers by using their own premises—that reason can discern good and evil. This prepares for the next lesson.
This marks a transition from definition to argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل الرسالة من قبل الممكنات في العقل أم من جملة الواجبات؟ فذهب جميع متكلمي أهل الحديث سوى أبي العباس القلانسي إلى أنها من جملة الممكنات، وذهب القائلون بأن العقل آلة معرفة الحسن والقبيح ووجوب شكر المنعم إلى القول بوجوبها، ولا يعنون بكونها واجبة أنها وجبت على الله بإيجاب أحد أو بإيجابه على نفسه بل يريدون أنها متحققة الوجود، ويقولون: إن الوجوب الحقيقة لفظة بها عن فضل تأكد لوجود المذكور، كما أن الامتناع لفظة يعبر بها عن تأكد لا وجوده، وهما في الحقيقة متقابلان تقابل الأضداد، وإنما يقولون: إنها متأكدة الوجود لأنها من مقتضيات حكمة القديم جل وعلا، ويستحيل أن لا يوجد ما كان وجوده من مقتضيات حكمته لما أن انعدامه يكون من باب السفه وهو يستحيل على القديم عز وجل، هذا كما أن ما علم الله وجوده يتحقق وجوده لا محالة ويكون واجب الوجود أي متأكد الوجود، لا أن وجوبه بإيجاب أحد على الله تعالى بل لأن وجوده يتحقق لا محالة لما أن انعدامه يوجب الجهل به، وهو مما يستحيل على القديم عز وجل وإلى هذا القول ذهب جميع من يقول بوجوب شكر المنعم. ولا معنى للاشتغال ببيان دلائل هذه المسألة وإني قد بينت ذلك على استقصاء في تصنيف لي في تلك المسألة، وإنما الحاجة هاهنا إلى بيان القول بوجوبها على منكريها، ولا شك أن من أنكر الرسالة وعدها في حيز الممتنعات فعل ذلك بالعقل دون السمع، إذ السمع بلا ثبوت الرسالة محال، فثبت من نفاة الرسالة المساعدة على القول بأن العقل مما يوقف به على الحكمة والسفه والواجب والممتنع والحسن والقبيح. فنتكلم معهم على هذه الطريقة بناء للأمر على مساعدتهم دون الاشتغال بإثبات معرفة ذلك بمجرد العقول بالدليل فنقول وبالله التوفيق إن من المعلوم الذي لا خفاء به على من اعترف بالصانع أن الصانع جل جلالة خلق الخلق ولم يجعل على ما عليه العادة بقاءهم إلا بالأغذية وحبب إليهم البقاء، وجعل في جنس ما خلق ما بالخلق إليه بطباعهم رغبة لما فيه من اللذة والشهوة، ثم إنه تعالى كما خلق ما لهم به البقاء من الأغذية، وما يحفظ عليهم الصحة التامة ويزيل عنهم العلل العارضة من الأدوية، خلق من جنس تلك الجواهر السموم القاتلة والجواهر المتلفة، وليس في قوة الحواس ولا في جبلة العقل الوقوف
Is prophethood from the category of possible things in the intellect or from the category of necessary things? All the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith except Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi went to that it is from the category of possible things. And those who say that the intellect is a tool for knowing good and evil and the obligation to thank the Benefactor went to the position that it is necessary. They do not mean by its being necessary that it became obligatory upon God by the obligation of anyone or by His obligating it upon Himself, but rather they mean that it is assured of existence. And they say: 'Indeed, necessity in reality is an expression for the extreme assurance of the existence of the mentioned thing, just as impossibility is an expression for the extreme assurance of its non-existence. And they are in reality opposites, like the opposition of contraries. And they only say that it is assured of existence because it is from the requirements of the wisdom of the Eternal, Exalted and Majestic, and it is impossible that what exists as a requirement of His wisdom should not exist, because its non-existence would be from the category of foolishness, and that is impossible for the Eternal, Mighty and Majestic. This is just as what God knows will exist becomes necessarily existent inevitably, and it is necessary of existence, i.e., assured of existence, not that its necessity is by the obligation of anyone upon God, but rather because its existence becomes realized inevitably, because its non-existence would necessitate ignorance of it, and that is impossible for the Eternal, Mighty and Majestic.' And to this position went all who say the obligation to thank the Benefactor. And there is no point in elaborating the proofs of this issue, and I have already explained it exhaustively in a work of mine on that issue. The need here is only to explain the position of its necessity to those who deny it. There is no doubt that whoever denies prophethood and counts it among impossibilities does so by reason, not by revelation, since revelation without the establishment of prophethood is impossible. So from the deniers of prophethood is established agreement with the statement that reason is a means by which one knows wisdom and foolishness, the necessary and the impossible, good and evil. So we will discuss with them in this way, building the matter on their agreement, without engaging in establishing knowledge of that by mere reason through proof. So we say, and with God is success: It is among the known matters, about which there is no concealment for whoever acknowledges the Maker, that the Maker, Majestic is His glory, created creation and did not make their survival according to custom except through nourishment, and He made them love survival, and He placed in the kind of what He created an inclination by their natures towards desire for what contains pleasure and appetite. Then, He, Exalted, as He created the nourishments by which they survive, and what preserves perfect health for them and removes from them accidental ailments from medicines, He created from the same kind of substances deadly poisons and destructive substances. And it is not within the power of the senses nor in the innate constitution of reason to know...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines a key theological dispute regarding the rational status of prophethood (رسالة) and then begins a proof for its necessity. The author first presents two opposing views: most mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith (except al-Qalanisi) hold that prophethood is rationally possible (ممكن), while those who affirm rational obligation (mainly Mu'tazila) hold it is necessary (واجب). A crucial clarification follows: 'necessary' here does not mean obligatory upon God (which would imply constraint), but rather 'assured of existence' (متأكدة الوجود). This assuredness stems from prophethood being a requirement (مقتضى) of God's wisdom; it is impossible for what wisdom requires not to exist, as that would entail foolishness (سفه) in God. The author compares this to God's knowledge: whatever God knows will exist is necessarily existent (واجب الوجود) in the sense of being inevitable, not because anyone obligates God. The passage then shifts to methodology: the author states he will not repeat detailed proofs (having done so elsewhere) but will instead argue against deniers of prophethood by using a premise they already accept—that reason can discern wisdom, folly, necessity, possibility, good, and evil. The author begins the argument by noting an observable fact: God created nourishments and medicines for survival and health, but also created deadly poisons and destructive substances from the same categories. Human senses and innate reason are not capable of distinguishing [the beneficial from the harmful] without guidance—the text cuts off at this point, but the intended conclusion is that this demonstrates the need for prophetic revelation. The argument is incomplete in the OCR, but the premises are laid out. The passage thus combines a definition of the theological dispute, a clarification of key terms, and the opening of a practical proof for the necessity of prophethood.

Correction: The text clarifies that it means logically inevitable (متأكدة الوجود) based on God's wisdom, not an external obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Do you live with the same conviction that every word of revelation is a necessary expression of God's wisdom, not an optional extra? When you neglect the Qur'an or the sunnah, are you not treating them as if they are merely possible rather than necessarily existent? What will you change today to treat prophethood as truly 'assured of existence' in your daily life?
?

Do you truly feel that gratitude to God obligates you to follow His messengers, or have you reduced thankfulness to a mere feeling? If you are not actively seeking to know the prophetic guidance, is your gratitude genuine? What specific step will you take this week to thank God by submitting to His message?

Proof for Prophethood: The Argument from Wisdom and Creation
Al-Nasafi begins his proof for prophethood using observable creation and the limits of reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's argumentative strategy and the initial premises of his proof for the necessity of prophethood.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot distinguish poisons from medicines without divine guidance.

Having clarified the terms, Al-Nasafi now turns to argue against those who deny prophethood. He adopts a method of building on their own premises—that reason can discern good and evil. He then presents an observable pattern in creation that points to the need for revelation.

Guided Path
1
Note the methodological shift
The author states he will not repeat detailed proofs but will argue using the deniers' own acceptance that reason can know wisdom, folly, necessary, impossible, good, and evil.
This is a dialectical approach: using the opponent's premises.
2
Read the observation about creation
Read the description: God created nourishments and medicines for survival and health, but also created similar poisons and destructive substances. Senses and reason cannot fully distinguish them.
The text cuts off, but the intended conclusion is clear.
3
Consider the implied conclusion
The argument implies that since humans cannot reliably tell beneficial from harmful substances, they need prophetic guidance. This supports the necessity of prophethood from God's wisdom.
This completes the proof: reason's limitation necessitates revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل الرسالة من قبل الممكنات في العقل أم من جملة الواجبات؟ فذهب جميع متكلمي أهل الحديث سوى أبي العباس القلانسي إلى أنها من جملة الممكنات، وذهب القائلون بأن العقل آلة معرفة الحسن والقبيح ووجوب شكر المنعم إلى القول بوجوبها، ولا يعنون بكونها واجبة أنها وجبت على الله بإيجاب أحد أو بإيجابه على نفسه بل يريدون أنها متحققة الوجود، ويقولون: إن الوجوب الحقيقة لفظة بها عن فضل تأكد لوجود المذكور، كما أن الامتناع لفظة يعبر بها عن تأكد لا وجوده، وهما في الحقيقة متقابلان تقابل الأضداد، وإنما يقولون: إنها متأكدة الوجود لأنها من مقتضيات حكمة القديم جل وعلا، ويستحيل أن لا يوجد ما كان وجوده من مقتضيات حكمته لما أن انعدامه يكون من باب السفه وهو يستحيل على القديم عز وجل، هذا كما أن ما علم الله وجوده يتحقق وجوده لا محالة ويكون واجب الوجود أي متأكد الوجود، لا أن وجوبه بإيجاب أحد على الله تعالى بل لأن وجوده يتحقق لا محالة لما أن انعدامه يوجب الجهل به، وهو مما يستحيل على القديم عز وجل وإلى هذا القول ذهب جميع من يقول بوجوب شكر المنعم. ولا معنى للاشتغال ببيان دلائل هذه المسألة وإني قد بينت ذلك على استقصاء في تصنيف لي في تلك المسألة، وإنما الحاجة هاهنا إلى بيان القول بوجوبها على منكريها، ولا شك أن من أنكر الرسالة وعدها في حيز الممتنعات فعل ذلك بالعقل دون السمع، إذ السمع بلا ثبوت الرسالة محال، فثبت من نفاة الرسالة المساعدة على القول بأن العقل مما يوقف به على الحكمة والسفه والواجب والممتنع والحسن والقبيح. فنتكلم معهم على هذه الطريقة بناء للأمر على مساعدتهم دون الاشتغال بإثبات معرفة ذلك بمجرد العقول بالدليل فنقول وبالله التوفيق إن من المعلوم الذي لا خفاء به على من اعترف بالصانع أن الصانع جل جلالة خلق الخلق ولم يجعل على ما عليه العادة بقاءهم إلا بالأغذية وحبب إليهم البقاء، وجعل في جنس ما خلق ما بالخلق إليه بطباعهم رغبة لما فيه من اللذة والشهوة، ثم إنه تعالى كما خلق ما لهم به البقاء من الأغذية، وما يحفظ عليهم الصحة التامة ويزيل عنهم العلل العارضة من الأدوية، خلق من جنس تلك الجواهر السموم القاتلة والجواهر المتلفة، وليس في قوة الحواس ولا في جبلة العقل الوقوف
Is prophethood from the category of possible things in the intellect or from the category of necessary things? All the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith except Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi went to that it is from the category of possible things. And those who say that the intellect is a tool for knowing good and evil and the obligation to thank the Benefactor went to the position that it is necessary. They do not mean by its being necessary that it became obligatory upon God by the obligation of anyone or by His obligating it upon Himself, but rather they mean that it is assured of existence. And they say: 'Indeed, necessity in reality is an expression for the extreme assurance of the existence of the mentioned thing, just as impossibility is an expression for the extreme assurance of its non-existence. And they are in reality opposites, like the opposition of contraries. And they only say that it is assured of existence because it is from the requirements of the wisdom of the Eternal, Exalted and Majestic, and it is impossible that what exists as a requirement of His wisdom should not exist, because its non-existence would be from the category of foolishness, and that is impossible for the Eternal, Mighty and Majestic. This is just as what God knows will exist becomes necessarily existent inevitably, and it is necessary of existence, i.e., assured of existence, not that its necessity is by the obligation of anyone upon God, but rather because its existence becomes realized inevitably, because its non-existence would necessitate ignorance of it, and that is impossible for the Eternal, Mighty and Majestic.' And to this position went all who say the obligation to thank the Benefactor. And there is no point in elaborating the proofs of this issue, and I have already explained it exhaustively in a work of mine on that issue. The need here is only to explain the position of its necessity to those who deny it. There is no doubt that whoever denies prophethood and counts it among impossibilities does so by reason, not by revelation, since revelation without the establishment of prophethood is impossible. So from the deniers of prophethood is established agreement with the statement that reason is a means by which one knows wisdom and foolishness, the necessary and the impossible, good and evil. So we will discuss with them in this way, building the matter on their agreement, without engaging in establishing knowledge of that by mere reason through proof. So we say, and with God is success: It is among the known matters, about which there is no concealment for whoever acknowledges the Maker, that the Maker, Majestic is His glory, created creation and did not make their survival according to custom except through nourishment, and He made them love survival, and He placed in the kind of what He created an inclination by their natures towards desire for what contains pleasure and appetite. Then, He, Exalted, as He created the nourishments by which they survive, and what preserves perfect health for them and removes from them accidental ailments from medicines, He created from the same kind of substances deadly poisons and destructive substances. And it is not within the power of the senses nor in the innate constitution of reason to know...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines a key theological dispute regarding the rational status of prophethood (رسالة) and then begins a proof for its necessity. The author first presents two opposing views: most mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith (except al-Qalanisi) hold that prophethood is rationally possible (ممكن), while those who affirm rational obligation (mainly Mu'tazila) hold it is necessary (واجب). A crucial clarification follows: 'necessary' here does not mean obligatory upon God (which would imply constraint), but rather 'assured of existence' (متأكدة الوجود). This assuredness stems from prophethood being a requirement (مقتضى) of God's wisdom; it is impossible for what wisdom requires not to exist, as that would entail foolishness (سفه) in God. The author compares this to God's knowledge: whatever God knows will exist is necessarily existent (واجب الوجود) in the sense of being inevitable, not because anyone obligates God. The passage then shifts to methodology: the author states he will not repeat detailed proofs (having done so elsewhere) but will instead argue against deniers of prophethood by using a premise they already accept—that reason can discern wisdom, folly, necessity, possibility, good, and evil. The author begins the argument by noting an observable fact: God created nourishments and medicines for survival and health, but also created deadly poisons and destructive substances from the same categories. Human senses and innate reason are not capable of distinguishing [the beneficial from the harmful] without guidance—the text cuts off at this point, but the intended conclusion is that this demonstrates the need for prophetic revelation. The argument is incomplete in the OCR, but the premises are laid out. The passage thus combines a definition of the theological dispute, a clarification of key terms, and the opening of a practical proof for the necessity of prophethood.

الحكمة

الجواهر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reason alone can always distinguish beneficial from harmful without divine guidance.
Correction: The argument shows that similar substances (medicines and poisons) cannot be reliably distinguished by senses or reason alone, necessitating revelation.

The Necessity of Prophetic Revelation for Moral Specification
Nasafi argues that reason alone cannot specify all good and evil, necessitating prophetic revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi argues that reason alone cannot provide the detailed moral knowledge needed for obligation, necessitating prophetic revelation.

Core Idea
Reason shows which direction to go, but only prophets reveal each step.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a sophisticated argument that while the intellect naturally inclines toward good and away from evil, it lacks the detailed knowledge of each act's moral category. This gap means that without revelation, the intellect's general commands and prohibitions would remain ambiguous and unworkable. The passage then demonstrates why divine wisdom requires sending messengers to clarify the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the translation. Notice the author's careful reasoning.
The argument builds gradually.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that the intellect's inclination creates a kind of command/prohibition from God, but it cannot specify each act's category.
3
Trace the logical consequence
The author shows that without revelation, the general command/prohibition remains like an ambiguous statement that cannot be followed.
This is the heart of the argument.
4
Appreciate the necessity of prophets
The conclusion is that revelation must come through a chosen envoy to fulfill divine wisdom.
This is the proof for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن العاقل لو ترك ومجرد عقله مع أنه يميل إلى المحاسن وينفر عن القبائح ولا وقوف له على أفرادها بحقائقها من الحسن والقبح، وهو بميلانه إلى الحسن يدعو صاحبه إلى اكتسابه، وبنفاره ينهي صاحبه عن الإقدام عليه، وذلك هو معنى الأمر والنهي، إذ الأمر والنهي ليس إلا الدعاء إلى المباشرة والامتناع، والله تعالى هو الذي خلقه مائلاً إلى ما يميل إليه، نافرًا عما ينفر عنه، فيكون العقل دليل أمر الله ونهيه كالخطاب دليل أمره الذي هو صفته القائمة بذاته لما فيه من الدعاء إلى مباشرة فعل ودليل نهيه لما فيه من الدعاء إلى الامتناع عن مباشرة فعل العقل، ثم هو لما لم يقف على حقيقة كل فرد من أفراد العقل أنه من قبيل المحاسن أو من قبيل القبائح لم يعلم أنه مأمور به أو منهي عنه، فلو لم يرد الشرع ببيان محل كل فرد من أفراد الأفعال أنه من قبيل المحاسن أو من قبيل القبائح لبقى الأمر والنهي الحاصلان بالعقل على وجه لا يطاوع العمل بهما بمنزلة الأمر والنهي الواردين بالخطاب على سبيل الإجمال ولو لم يتصل البيان بالمجمل لما اهتدى المكلف إلى [معرفة؟] ما أمر به ونهى عنه، ولا يتوصل إلى الائتمار والانتهاء، وذلك خارج عن حد الحكمة، فلابد حينئذ من ورود بيان سمعي على لسان من ارتضاه الصانع سفيراً بينه وبين عباده لتتحقق الحكمة في ذلك.
The second: that the rational person if left with his mere intellect, while he inclines to good deeds and recoils from bad deeds, and has no full knowledge of their particulars with their realities of good and evil, and by his inclination to good he calls his possessor to acquire it, and by his recoiling he forbids his possessor from undertaking it, and that is the meaning of command and prohibition, for command and prohibition are nothing but the call to action and refusal. And Allah Most High is the One who created him inclining to what he inclines to, recoiling from what he recoils from. Thus the intellect becomes a proof of Allah's command and His prohibition, like the revealed speech is a proof of His command which is His attribute subsisting in His essence, because of what it contains of calling to the direct performance of an act, and a proof of His prohibition because of what it contains of calling to refraining from the direct act of the intellect. Then, since it does not have full knowledge of the true nature of every individual of the acts of the intellect, whether it is of the category of good deeds or of the category of bad deeds, it does not know whether it is commanded or prohibited. So if the revealed law did not come to clarify the position of every individual of the acts, whether it is of the category of good deeds or of the category of bad deeds, the command and prohibition obtained through the intellect would remain in a manner not yielding compliance with them, like the command and prohibition coming through speech in a general way. And if the clarification had not been connected to the general, the accountable person would not be guided to the knowledge of what he was commanded and prohibited, nor would he attain to obedience and refraining, and that is outside the bounds of wisdom. Hence it is necessary then that a revealed clarification should come upon the tongue of one whom the Maker chose as an envoy between Him and His servants so that wisdom may be realized in that.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of prophetic revelation. The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, presents a second argument (the first presumably being on a previous page) that reason alone, despite its innate inclination towards good and aversion to evil, cannot provide the detailed knowledge required for moral obligation. He explains that the intellect's natural pull towards the good and away from evil constitutes a kind of command and prohibition from God, because God created it that way. However, the intellect cannot discern the true nature of each individual act—whether it falls under good or evil. Therefore, without divine revelation specifying each act's category, the intellect's general commands and prohibitions remain akin to ambiguous general statements that cannot be acted upon. This would render the moral law ineffective, which is incompatible with divine wisdom. Hence, God must send a messenger (a prophetic envoy) to clarify the details, so that wisdom is fulfilled.

الأمر والنهي (al-amr wa al-nahy)
البيان السمعي (al-bayan al-sam'i)
المحاسن والقبائح (al-mahasin wa al-masawi')
المكلّف (al-mukallaf)
الحكمة (al-hikmah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect is fully sufficient for moral guidance without revelation.
Correction: While the intellect provides general inclinations, it cannot specify the moral status of every possible act. Revelation is needed to detail which acts are commanded and which are prohibited.

Checkpoint

A) The intellect cannot know any good or evil at all.
B) The intellect does not provide the detailed specification of which acts are good and which are evil.
C) The intellect always errs in moral judgments.
D) Revelation is needed only for acts that are neutral.

Key Takeaway
Reason provides general moral inclinations, but only prophetic revelation can specify the details of what is commanded and prohibited, fulfilling divine wisdom.

The Rational Obligation of Gratitude
Establishes gratitude as a rational obligation and God's blessings as originary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why gratitude to God is a rational obligation and how God's blessings are purely originary, not a reward for prior deserving.

Core Idea
Gratitude to God is a rational duty, not just a religious recommendation.

The author begins by establishing gratitude as a moral imperative rooted in reason itself. Before discussing revelation, he argues that the intellect independently recognizes that thanking a benefactor is good and necessary, while ingratitude is evil and forbidden. This foundation is crucial for understanding the subsequent need for divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the first sentence where the author states that gratitude is good and obligatory in the intellects, and ingratitude is evil and forbidden. Notice that he grounds this in reason, not revelation.
2
Identify the divine wisdom argument
The author argues that it is incompatible with divine wisdom to disregard gratitude and permit ingratitude. Reflect on why this must be the case.
3
Understand the originary nature of blessings
The author points out that God created humans with blessings without any prior action on their part. This means the blessings are purely a gift, not payment for a debt. This strengthens the obligation of gratitude.
Contrast this with rewards earned by service.
4
Conclude the obligation
The author concludes that gratitude is obligatory. Make sure you see how each premise leads to this conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق هذا أن شكر المنعم حسن واجب في العقول وكفران المنعم قبيح محرم فيها، لا يليق بحكمة الحكيم إسقاط الشكر وإباحة الكفران. ثم إن الله تعالى خلق البشر في أحسن تقويم وسخر لهم جميع ما على وجه الأرض من غير أن سبق منهم ما كانت هذه النعم خارجة مخرج المكافأة أو قضاء حق مستحق فكانت نعماؤه مبتدأه، ولا وجه إلى إسقاط شكرها وإباحة كفرانها لما فيه من تضييع النعم وظلمها فيجب الشكر ، ثم ينبغي أن يكون الشكر كفاً للنعمة موازيًا لها في ذاتها مساويًا لها في قدرها، ولو لم يكن كذا لم يكن معتدا به ولا مقابلاً لها أن يرضى المنعم بكرمه وجوده بأدون من حقه ثم إن النعم تتفاوت أقدارها وتتباين منازلها بتفاوت أقدار المنعمين وتباين منازلهم ودرجاتهم، فمن كان منهم أعظم قدراً أو أعلا درجة ومنزلة كانت النعم منه أجل قدرًا وأكبر منزلة، فإن واحدًا من طبقات الرعايا وأصناف السوقة لو أهدى إلى رئيس من الرؤساء أو عظيم من العظماء فرسًا جوادًا أو برذونًا فارها أو جارية حسنة أو كسوة فاخرة لم تكن درجته عند ذلك الرئيس أو العظيم ولا في أعين من نظر إليها من العقلاء، كدرجة ما لو أهدى مثل ذلك أو أدون منه من جل قدره من الملوك وعظم خطره في القلوب ممن اتسع نطاق ملكه واشتدت شوكته وملأت الصدور جلالته وهيبته. فإذا كانت هذه المقدمة صادقة جارية لدى العقلاء مجرى البداية، ثم لا نهاية لجلال الله وكبريائه وعظمته وسلطانه، ولم يكن في عقل أحد من البشر الوقوف على كنه أدنى نعمة أسداها إلى أحد من خلقه وكيف، وقد توالت نعمه وترادفت آلاؤه على عباده، بحيث لو أراد أحد من الخلق وإن كان في الفطنة والذكاء وفي وفور العقل ونفاذ البصيرة آية أن يقف على ما الله تعالى عليه من النعم التي أسداها إليه ونعمة دفع البلايا عنه في لحظة يعجز عن الوقوف على ذلك؟! فكيف ما تواتر
And that which verifies this is that gratitude to the bestower of blessings is good and obligatory in the intellects, and ingratitude to the bestower of blessings is evil and forbidden in them. It is not befitting the wisdom of the Wise to disregard gratitude and permit ingratitude. Then indeed Allah Most High created mankind in the best of forms and subjected to them all that is on the face of the earth, without there having preceded from them anything that would make these blessings come as recompense or the fulfillment of a due right, so His blessings were originary. And there is no justification for disregarding their gratitude and permitting ingratitude to them because of what that involves of wasting the blessings and wronging them. Thus gratitude is obligatory. Then it is fitting that gratitude be a match for the blessings, parallel to them in their essence and equal to them in their capacity. And if it were not so, it would not be considered valid nor a counterpart for them, so that the bestower would be satisfied with less than his right due to his generosity and bounty. Then indeed blessings vary in their capacities and differ in their stations according to the varying capacities of the bestowers and the difference of their stations and degrees. Whoever among them is greater in capacity or higher in degree and station, the blessings from him are more magnificent in capacity and greater in station. For if a person from the ranks of subjects and classes of commoners were to present to a chief of the chiefs or a great one of the great ones a swift horse or a excellent draft horse or a beautiful slave girl or a fine garment, his rank with that chief or great one, nor in the eyes of those who look at it among the rational people, would be like the rank of what would be presented similarly or even less by one of great capacity among kings, whose standing in hearts is great, whose dominion has expanded, whose power is strong, and whose majesty and awe fill hearts. So when this premise is true, current among the rational people as a given, and then there is no limit to the majesty of Allah, His pride, His greatness, and His sovereignty, and it is not within the intellect of any human to comprehend the essence of the least blessing He has bestowed upon any of His creatures—how then, when His blessings have followed one after another and His favors have been successive upon His servants, such that if any of the creatures, even if he were a sign in intelligence and sharpness and abundance of intellect and piercing insight, wanted to know what Allah Most High has of blessings which He has granted him and the blessing of repelling calamities from him in a moment, he would be incapable of knowing that?! How then (with) what has come successively?
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a supporting argument for the necessity of revelation, specifically focusing on the obligation of gratitude (shukr) to God. The author states that reason itself deems gratitude obligatory and ingratitude forbidden, and it is inconsistent with divine wisdom to abrogate this obligation. He then explains that God created humans with many blessings without prior deserving, making His gifts purely originary (mubtada'a). Gratitude must be proportionate to the blessing, which humans cannot fully achieve because the value of a blessing increases with the status of the benefactor. Using the analogy of a gift from a commoner versus a king, he shows that a greater benefactor deserves greater gratitude. Since God's majesty is infinite, even the smallest of His blessings surpasses human comprehension. Therefore, humans cannot fulfill the obligation of gratitude through reason alone, further demonstrating the need for revelation to guide and assist.

Key Terms
شكر المنعم
كفران المنعم
الحكمة
النعم المبتدأة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Gratitude to God is merely a recommended act of piety, not a rational necessity.
Correction: The author argues that reason itself deems gratitude obligatory and ingratitude forbidden, independent of revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is gratitude to God obligatory?
A) Because God commands it in revelation.
B) Because reason deems it good and ingratitude evil, and God's wisdom cannot ignore that.
C) Because humans fear punishment.
D) Because gratitude increases blessings.

Key Takeaway
Gratitude to God is a rational obligation rooted in the intellect's recognition of good and evil and the originary nature of God's blessings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the countless blessings you enjoy right now—sight, breath, faith. The passage says they are 'originary' (mubtada'ah), given without any prior deserving from you. When was the last time you truly paused to acknowledge that you never earned any of this? What if you wake up tomorrow and realize you have been wronging these blessings by taking them for granted?
?
The text calls ingratitude 'wasting the blessings and wronging them' (تضييع النعم وظلمها). Have you ever used one of God's blessings—like your tongue, your wealth, or your strength—in a way that was itself an act of ingratitude? What would it look like to use that same blessing today in a way that gives it its due right of gratitude?
?
If gratitude is a rational duty, not a suggestion, then how can you justify the moments when you withhold thanks? When you feel ungrateful, are you not acting against your own intellect? Challenge yourself: pick one blessing you have been unthankful for, and commit to a tangible act of gratitude for it before you sleep tonight.

Gratitude Must Match the Benefactor's Greatness
Introduces the principle that gratitude is proportional to the benefactor's status, illustrated by a social analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the magnitude of gratitude owed is proportional to the status of the benefactor, and how this principle applies to God's infinite majesty.

Core Idea
Greater benefactors deserve greater gratitude—an eternal truth for the Creator.

After establishing the obligation of gratitude, the author now addresses its proper measure. He introduces a key principle: the value of a blessing and the gratitude it demands are tied to the rank of the giver. Using a vivid analogy from social life, he shows that a gift from a king commands far more gratitude than the same gift from a commoner—an insight that prepares us to grasp why human reason alone cannot fulfill the gratitude due to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the core principle
Read the sentence where the author states that blessings vary according to the status of the bestower. This is the central idea.
2
Study the analogy
The author gives an example: a commoner giving a horse to a chief versus a king giving the same or even less. Notice how the response differs. This illustrates the principle clearly.
Focus on the contrast in social standing.
3
Apply the principle to God
Now think: if this principle holds among humans, how much more does it apply to Allah, whose majesty is infinite? This is the implied conclusion.
4
Reflect on the implication
Since God's status is infinitely greater than any king, the gratitude due to Him must be infinitely great—beyond human capacity. This ties into the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق هذا أن شكر المنعم حسن واجب في العقول وكفران المنعم قبيح محرم فيها، لا يليق بحكمة الحكيم إسقاط الشكر وإباحة الكفران. ثم إن الله تعالى خلق البشر في أحسن تقويم وسخر لهم جميع ما على وجه الأرض من غير أن سبق منهم ما كانت هذه النعم خارجة مخرج المكافأة أو قضاء حق مستحق فكانت نعماؤه مبتدأه، ولا وجه إلى إسقاط شكرها وإباحة كفرانها لما فيه من تضييع النعم وظلمها فيجب الشكر ، ثم ينبغي أن يكون الشكر كفاً للنعمة موازيًا لها في ذاتها مساويًا لها في قدرها، ولو لم يكن كذا لم يكن معتدا به ولا مقابلاً لها أن يرضى المنعم بكرمه وجوده بأدون من حقه ثم إن النعم تتفاوت أقدارها وتتباين منازلها بتفاوت أقدار المنعمين وتباين منازلهم ودرجاتهم، فمن كان منهم أعظم قدراً أو أعلا درجة ومنزلة كانت النعم منه أجل قدرًا وأكبر منزلة، فإن واحدًا من طبقات الرعايا وأصناف السوقة لو أهدى إلى رئيس من الرؤساء أو عظيم من العظماء فرسًا جوادًا أو برذونًا فارها أو جارية حسنة أو كسوة فاخرة لم تكن درجته عند ذلك الرئيس أو العظيم ولا في أعين من نظر إليها من العقلاء، كدرجة ما لو أهدى مثل ذلك أو أدون منه من جل قدره من الملوك وعظم خطره في القلوب ممن اتسع نطاق ملكه واشتدت شوكته وملأت الصدور جلالته وهيبته. فإذا كانت هذه المقدمة صادقة جارية لدى العقلاء مجرى البداية، ثم لا نهاية لجلال الله وكبريائه وعظمته وسلطانه، ولم يكن في عقل أحد من البشر الوقوف على كنه أدنى نعمة أسداها إلى أحد من خلقه وكيف، وقد توالت نعمه وترادفت آلاؤه على عباده، بحيث لو أراد أحد من الخلق وإن كان في الفطنة والذكاء وفي وفور العقل ونفاذ البصيرة آية أن يقف على ما الله تعالى عليه من النعم التي أسداها إليه ونعمة دفع البلايا عنه في لحظة يعجز عن الوقوف على ذلك؟! فكيف ما تواتر
And that which verifies this is that gratitude to the bestower of blessings is good and obligatory in the intellects, and ingratitude to the bestower of blessings is evil and forbidden in them. It is not befitting the wisdom of the Wise to disregard gratitude and permit ingratitude. Then indeed Allah Most High created mankind in the best of forms and subjected to them all that is on the face of the earth, without there having preceded from them anything that would make these blessings come as recompense or the fulfillment of a due right, so His blessings were originary. And there is no justification for disregarding their gratitude and permitting ingratitude to them because of what that involves of wasting the blessings and wronging them. Thus gratitude is obligatory. Then it is fitting that gratitude be a match for the blessings, parallel to them in their essence and equal to them in their capacity. And if it were not so, it would not be considered valid nor a counterpart for them, so that the bestower would be satisfied with less than his right due to his generosity and bounty. Then indeed blessings vary in their capacities and differ in their stations according to the varying capacities of the bestowers and the difference of their stations and degrees. Whoever among them is greater in capacity or higher in degree and station, the blessings from him are more magnificent in capacity and greater in station. For if a person from the ranks of subjects and classes of commoners were to present to a chief of the chiefs or a great one of the great ones a swift horse or a excellent draft horse or a beautiful slave girl or a fine garment, his rank with that chief or great one, nor in the eyes of those who look at it among the rational people, would be like the rank of what would be presented similarly or even less by one of great capacity among kings, whose standing in hearts is great, whose dominion has expanded, whose power is strong, and whose majesty and awe fill hearts. ?

This night, before sleeping, list three blessings you normally take for granted and thank Allah specifically for each.
?
You may have never thought that the worth of a blessing depends on the giver. Does this lesson expose a hidden ingratitude in your heart? Will you now begin to internalize that every good thing you have is from the Most Great, and thus deserves the utmost thanks? Take a moment now to sincerely repent for any past ingratitude and resolve to be more mindful.

Human Reason Cannot Grasp God's Infinite Blessings
Argues that human reason cannot fathom God's blessings, proving the need for revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that human reason cannot fully comprehend even the least of God's blessings, highlighting the need for revelation to guide gratitude.

Core Idea
God's infinite greatness makes His blessings unfathomable to human reason.

The author now draws the ultimate conclusion from the preceding principle. If gratitude must match the benefactor's status, and God's majesty is infinite, then the gratitude due to Him is beyond any human capacity. Moreover, even a single blessing from God is so immense that no intellect can fully grasp it. This argument demonstrates the insufficiency of reason alone and the need for divine assistance and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise
The author begins by saying that the premise about gratitude and status is accepted by rational people. Then he notes that God's majesty is without limit.
2
Consider the inability to comprehend
He states that no human intellect can comprehend the essence of even the least blessing from God. Reflect on why this follows from God's infinity.
3
Imagine the impossibility
The author then says that if someone with perfect intelligence tried to understand the blessings God has given him in just a moment, he would fail. This reinforces the point.
4
Understand the implication
Since humans cannot adequately thank God through reason alone, revelation is necessary to teach us how to be grateful and to assist us in fulfilling this duty.
This connects to the broader lesson of the necessity of prophethood.

Rational Judgments

The Rational Division of Actions and the Need for Revelation
Nasafi classifies actions rationally and explains reason's limitation for possible actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational categories of actions and why possible actions require divine guidance.

Core Idea
Reason can know the necessary and impossible, but only revelation can guide possible actions.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by establishing a foundational principle in Islamic theology: actions are divided by reason into three categories. This classification is not merely philosophical but has direct implications for the role of revelation. The passage explains that while reason can grasp the first two categories, it cannot determine the outcomes of possible actions—only revelation can. This sets the stage for the necessity of prophethood and divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of the author's style. Then read the English translation.
Notice the concise, logical flow.
2
Identify the three categories
List the three categories: obligatory, impossible, and the intermediate (possible). Understand how each is defined.
The intermediate is called 'wasit' and is identified with 'mumkin'.
3
Understand reason's limitation
The passage states that reason can know the obligatory and impossible, but not the possible. Why? Because the possible can have a praiseworthy or blameworthy consequence which is hidden from reason.
This limitation leads to the need for revelation.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Ask yourself: What does this classification imply about the necessity of prophets and scriptures? The passage implicitly argues that without revelation, humans cannot reliably choose actions leading to good outcomes.
This connects theology (kalam) to the proof of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن الأفعال في العقول منقسمة إلى أقسام ثلاثة : واجب وممتنع وواسط وهو الممكن. الأفعال تنقسم إلى واجب وممتنع وممكن ثم بالعقل إن كان يمكن الوقوف على الواجب والممتنع ، فلا يمكن الوقوف به على الواسطة، إذ قد يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة، وقد يتعلق به عاقبة ذميمة وخيمة، وتقصر العقول عن الوقوف على حميدة العافية وذميمها لخفائها في نفسها، فتقع
He confirms it: that actions, in the intellects, are divided into three divisions: obligatory (wājib), impossible (mumtaniʿ), and the intermediate, which is possible (mumkin). Actions are divided into obligatory, impossible, and possible. Then, by the intellect, if it is possible to know the obligatory and the impossible, it is not possible to know by it the intermediate, for there may attach to it a praiseworthy consequence, and there may attach to it a blameworthy and evil consequence. And intellects fall short of knowing what is praiseworthy of the outcome and what is blameworthy of it due to their hiddenness in themselves, so it falls...
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof of the need for revelation. Imam al-Nasafi states that actions are rationally divided into three categories: obligatory (wājib), impossible (mumtaniʿ), and possible (mumkin). Reason can (in principle) know the first two categories, but it cannot determine the consequences of possible actions—whether they lead to a good or bad outcome—because these outcomes are hidden. Therefore, humans need divine revelation (samʿ) to guide them in choosing possible actions that lead to beneficial consequences and avoiding those that lead to harmful ones. The final word 'فتقع' likely begins an explanation of what results from this deficiency, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
واجب
ممتنع
ممكن
واسط
عاقبة حميدة
عاقبة ذميمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone can determine the moral value of all actions.
Correction: According to Imam al-Nasafi, reason can only know the obligatory and impossible. Possible actions have hidden consequences that reason cannot evaluate, requiring revelation.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Nasafi, which category of actions cannot be fully known by reason alone?
A) Obligatory actions.
B) Impossible actions.
C) Possible actions.
D) All actions.

Key Takeaway
Actions are divided into three by reason, but only revelation can guide the possible actions whose outcomes are hidden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you made recently that was 'possible' — neither clearly obligatory nor forbidden by reason. Did you seek guidance from revelation before acting? If not, what was the consequence? Will you change that pattern starting today?
?
Your intellect cannot see the hidden outcomes of possible actions. Are there areas of your life — your finances, relationships, or daily habits — where you trust your own reasoning more than the Quran and Sunnah? What would it cost you to ignore revelation there?
?
The hiddenness of consequences ('خفائها في نفسها') means you could be one step away from a blameworthy outcome without realizing it. How often do you pause and ask: 'What does Allah command here?' before moving forward? Commit to a specific action: choose one possible matter today and consult a scholar or a verse before deciding.

intermediate
6 min
Classification of Actions and Need for Revelation
Nasafi classifies actions into three rational categories and argues for revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi classifies actions into three rational categories and why revelation is required for possible acts.

Core Idea
Reason knows the necessary and impossible, but not the possible.

In this passage, Nasafi lays the foundation for the necessity of prophethood by showing that human reason has limits. While reason can distinguish between obligatory and impossible actions, it cannot determine the moral status of possible actions because their outcomes are hidden. This gap is filled by revelation through prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully and its translation. Notice the three categories of actions and the argument that follows.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three divisions
Find the three categories: obligatory, impossible, and possible. Understand what each means in the context of rational judgment.
The middle category is also called 'possible'.
3
Understand reason's limitation
Nasafi states that reason can know the first two categories but not the possible. Why? Because the outcomes of possible actions are hidden.
4
See the need for revelation
The hidden outcomes create a need for someone who knows them—a messenger. This leads to a proof for the necessity of prophethood.
This is the key argument of the passage.
5
Reflect on divine wisdom
Nasafi concludes that without revelation, abandoning obligations and committing prohibitions would be permissible, which is contrary to wisdom.
Thus, God always sends revealed guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن الأفعال في العقول منقسمة إلى أقسام ثلاثة: واجب وممتنع وواسط وهو الممكن. [الأفعال تنقسم إلى واجب وممتنع وممكن] ثم بالعقل إن كان يمكن الوقوف على الواجب والممتنع، فلا يمكن الوقوف به على الواسطة، إذ قد يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة، وقد يتعلق به عاقبة ذميمة وخيمة، وتقصر العقول عن الوقوف على حميدة العافية وذميمها لخفائها في نفسها، فتقع الحاجة إلى بيان من له الاطلاع على عواقب الأمور على لسان من ارتضاه من الرسل ليتمكن من أداء ما وجب منها والامتناع عما حرم، وهذه مقدمة لا خفاء بها على من له أدنى لبس. وقد يمكن أن يجمل عين هذا الكلام دليلاً للقول بوجوب الرسالة، إذ الأمر لما كان على ما بينا فلو لم يرد الوصول ببيان القسم الواسطة وقد يجب منه البعض لتعلق الحميد من العواقب به، ويحرم البعض التعلق الذميم من العواقب به لكان فيه إباحة ترك الواجب وإباحة مباشرة المحظور، وهو خارج عن الحكمة، فلابد من بيان ذلك ممن له العلم بذلك على لسان الرسول ولهذا ما أخلى الله تعالى العالم منذ خلق الممتحنين عن الحجة السمعية. إما على ألسنة الرسل وإما على ألسنة خلفائهم، والله الموفق.
He verifies that actions in the intellects are divided into three divisions: obligatory, impossible, and middle, which is the possible. [Actions are divided into obligatory, impossible, and possible.] Then by the intellect, if it is possible to know the obligatory and the impossible, it is not possible to know thereby the middle, for a praiseworthy outcome may attach to it, and a blameworthy, harmful outcome may attach to it, and intellects fall short of knowing its praiseworthy outcome and its blameworthy one due to their hiddenness in themselves, so the need arises for an explanation from one who has knowledge of the outcomes of affairs on the tongue of whom He has approved from the messengers, so that one may perform what is obligatory from them and abstain from what is forbidden. This is a premise not hidden from anyone possessing the least intelligence. And it is possible to make this very statement a proof for the necessity of prophethood, for when the matter is as we have explained, if the arrival of an explanation of the middle division were not intended—while some of it may be obligatory due to praiseworthy outcomes attaching to it, and some may be forbidden due to blameworthy outcomes attaching to it—then that would involve allowing the abandonment of the obligatory and allowing the commission of the prohibited, which is contrary to wisdom. Therefore, it is necessary to have an explanation from one who has knowledge of that on the tongue of the messenger. For this reason, God Almighty has not left the world, since He created those who are tested, without a revealed proof—either on the tongues of messengers or on the tongues of their successors. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of actions and a proof of the need for revelation regarding the middle category. Nasafi begins by stating that actions, as perceived by reason, are divided into three categories: wajib (obligatory), mumtani' (impossible), and the middle category, which is mumkin (possible). He then argues that while reason can know the obligatory and the impossible (e.g., it is impossible for a thing to be and not be simultaneously), it cannot know the possible acts because their outcomes may be praiseworthy or blameworthy, and these outcomes are hidden. Consequently, people need an explanation from someone who knows the outcomes—a messenger—so they can perform what is obligatory and avoid what is forbidden. Nasafi asserts that this premise is clear to anyone with minimal intelligence. He then formalizes this as a proof for the necessity of prophethood: if there were no revelation about possible acts, people would be allowed to abandon what is actually obligatory and commit what is actually forbidden due to hidden consequences, which would be contrary to divine wisdom. Hence, God has always provided revealed proof through prophets or their successors.

ممتنع
ممكن
عاقبة حميدة
عاقبة ذميمة
حكمة
رسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone can determine the moral status of all actions.
Correction: Nasafi argues that reason can only know the obligatory and impossible; possible actions require revelation because their outcomes are hidden.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why can reason not determine the ruling for possible actions?
A) Because possible actions are always harmful.
B) Because the outcomes of possible actions are hidden and reason cannot grasp them.
C) Because reason is only for necessary truths.
D) Because possible actions are not real.

Key Takeaway
Revelation is necessary because reason cannot assess the outcomes of possible actions, and without it, divine wisdom would be undermined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life today have you been relying on your own reasoning to decide an action, ignoring what revelations says about its hidden consequences?
?
Think of a possible action you are currently hesitant about. Have you sought a clear ruling from the Quran and Sunnah, or have you justified it based on your own estimation of its outcome?
?
If you truly believed that your intellect cannot grasp the ultimate outcomes of even small actions, how would your daily decision-making change in terms of seeking divine guidance?

The Dialectical Refutation of Denying Divine Command
A dialectical argument that any denial of divine command leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dialectical argument forces an opponent to acknowledge the necessity of divine command by showing that any denial leads to contradiction.

Core Idea
Denying divine command is self-refuting: every alternative leads to an absurdity.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla presents a powerful dialectical refutation against someone who denies that God commands or prohibits. By posing a series of logical questions, the argument shows that any possible answer the opponent gives inevitably acknowledges some form of obligation, thus undermining their initial denial. Let's work through the steps of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial question
The author asks whether commanding useless or prohibiting harmless actions is foolish in human experience. This sets up a comparison between the witnessed realm (shāhid) and the unseen (ghā'ib).
The opponent must choose an answer.
2
Follow the first branch: if he says it is not foolish
If the opponent denies that such commands are foolish, then he contradicts himself because he already applied the same foolishness to God. This branch leads to immediate refutation.
3
Follow the second branch: if he says it is foolish
Then the author asks whether this foolishness is good or evil. If the opponent says it is good, he is irrational; if evil, then the argument proceeds to classify the action as permissible, obligatory, or prohibited.
Each classification further traps the opponent.
4
Analyze the final options
If he says obligatory or permissible, the question becomes who imposed it. If he says God, then God permits evil; if another, that other has authority over obligation. If he says prohibited, that implies a prohibition, which is a form of obligation. Every path forces acknowledgment of divine command.
The conclusion is that denial is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : هل الأمر بما لا نفع للآمر بفعله والنهي عما لا ضرر على الناهي بفعله سفه في الشاهد أم لا؟ فإن قال : لا ، أبطل كلامه لأن جعل ذلك في الغائب سفة، لدلالة كون مثله سفها في الشاهد. وإن قال نعم هو سفه . قيل: أحسن هو أم قبيح؟ فإن قال: هو حسن عرف العقلاء مبلغ عقله حيث زعم أن السفه حسن وإن قال: هو قبيح. قيل: أمباح أم محظور أم واجب؟ فإن قال بالوجوب أو الإباحة. فقيل: من الذي أوجبه عليه أو أباحه له؟ فإن قال : هو الله تعالى، فقد أعظم القول، حيث زعم أن الله تعالى يبيح القبيح أو يوجبه، على أنه مع بطلانه ترك لمذهبه لأن الإيجاب أو الإباحة شرع وتكليف، وإن قال : أوجب ذلك عليه أو أباحه له غير الله تعالى فقد هذى، حيث جعل لغير الله تعالى ولاية التكليف ولم يجعل ذلك الله تعالى فإن قال: هو محظور وليس بواجب ولا مباح فقد أقر أيضا بالتكليف إذ الحظر لن يثبت إلا بالنهي، وهذا يوجب إبطال كلامه، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then we say: Is commanding what is of no benefit to the commander by doing it and prohibiting what is no harm to the prohibiter by doing it foolish in the witnessed realm or not? If he says: No, he refutes his own statement, because making that apply to the unseen would be foolish, by the indication that the like of it is foolish in the witnessed. If he says: Yes, it is foolish. It is said: Is it good or evil? If he says: It is good, the rational people would recognize the extent of his intellect, since he claims that foolishness is good. If he says: It is evil. It is said: Is it permissible, prohibited, or obligatory? If he says it is obligatory or permissible. It is said: Who made it obligatory or permissible for him? If he says: It is God Almighty, he has made a grave statement, since he claims that God Almighty permits evil or obliges it, while besides its falsehood he has abandoned his school, because obligation or permission is legislation and obligation. If he says: Someone other than God Almighty made it obligatory or permissible for him, he has raved, since he made someone other than God Almighty have authority over obligation and did not make that for God Almighty. If he says: It is prohibited and not obligatory nor permissible, he has also acknowledged obligation, because prohibition cannot be established except by a prohibition, and this necessitates the invalidation of his statement. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation aimed at anyone who denies the necessity of divine command. It begins by asking whether commanding useless actions or prohibiting harmless ones is foolish in human experience. If the opponent denies it, he contradicts himself because applying the same to God would be foolish. If he admits it is foolish, the author asks whether it is good or evil. If he says it is good, he is irrational; if evil, then is it permissible, prohibited, or obligatory? Each answer leads to a contradiction: if obligatory or permissible, the opponent must say who imposed it—if God, then God permits evil; if another, that other has authority over obligation. If he says it is prohibited, that implies a prohibition, which is a form of obligation. Thus, any stance forces acknowledgment of divine command.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
سفه
إباحة
حظر
وجوب
نهي
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that denying obligation avoids obligation altogether.
Correction: The argument shows that even saying the action is prohibited acknowledges obligation through the prohibition itself.

Checkpoint
What happens if the opponent says the command without benefit is prohibited?
A) This contradicts him.
B) This acknowledges obligation through prohibition.
C) This shows it is permissible.
D) This proves it is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The denial of divine command is self-refuting because every possible stance either contradicts itself or implicitly acknowledges obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered yourself free from God's commands while still feeling bound by your own moral code? This argument forces you to ask: where does that moral authority come from? Can you honestly claim it doesn't ultimately rest on God?
?
When you judge a policy or action as 'good' or 'evil', are you implicitly appealing to a standard? This dialectic shows that any attempt to avoid divine command leads to contradiction. What does that reveal about your own heart—do you want to be independent from God?
?
Imagine you are the interlocutor being refuted. Which branch of the argument do you find hardest to escape? Why does your mind resist the conclusion that God's command is inescapable? Let that resistance be your teacher.
