Implication of Prohibition and the Maturidi Method
Al-Nasafi argues that prohibition implies a divine prohibitor, affirming Maturidi rationalism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi argues that claiming an act is prohibited logically implies a divine prohibitor, and how this supports the Maturidi commitment to rational ethical discernment.

Core Idea
Prohibition implies a prohibitor; denial of divine command still entails obligation.

Al-Nasafi presents a subtle argument: even someone who denies divine obligation implicitly acknowledges it when they claim an act is prohibited. Since prohibition requires a command, it must come from a commander. This reasoning aligns with the Maturidi view that good and evil can be known by reason, as the very concept of prohibition is rationally linked to a lawgiver.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the three alternatives presented: obligatory, permitted, prohibited.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two hypothetical scenarios
The passage examines two claims: (1) that an act is obligatory or permitted by other than God, and (2) that an act is prohibited (but not obligatory or permitted). See how each leads to an affirmation of divine obligation.
3
Understand the key inference
Focus on the statement: 'الحظر لن يثبت إلا بالنهي' — prohibition is only established by a prohibition command. This shows that calling something prohibited necessarily implies a commander.
This is the logical core.
4
Connect to the Maturidi method
The passage concludes by linking this reasoning to Imam al-Maturidi and his school, who affirm that good and evil can be discerned by reason. How does the argument above depend on rational discernment?
Reflect on why reason is needed to recognize the implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرع وتكليف، وإن قال : أوجب ذلك عليه أو أباحه له غير الله تعالى فقد هذى، حيث جعل لغير الله تعالى ولاية التكليف ولم يجعل ذلك الله تعالى فإن قال: هو محظور وليس بواجب ولا مباح فقد أقر أيضا بالتكليف إذ الحظر لن يثبت إلا بالنهي، وهذا يوجب إبطال كلامه، ولا قوة إلا بالله
على مثل هذه الطريقة يتكلم الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي، وكل من يقول من أصحابنا بإمكان استدراك الحسن والقبيح بالعقل.
Legislation and obligation. If he says: 'God obligated or permitted it for him,' but if he attributes that to other than God, he has erred, because he assigns the authority of obligation to other than God, while God did not assign that. If he says: 'It is prohibited and not obligatory nor permitted,' he also affirms obligation, because prohibition is only established by a prohibition [command]. This necessitates the nullification of his statement. There is no power except with God. In such a manner does Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi speak, and everyone among our companions who affirms the possibility of discerning good and evil by reason.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the logical consequences of claiming an act is prohibited. The argument begins with a hypothetical: if someone says that an act is obligatory or permitted by a being other than God, he has erred because he assigns the authority of obligation to that being, which rightly belongs only to God. Alternatively, if he says the act is prohibited (محظور) but not obligatory or permitted, he still implicitly affirms obligation, because prohibition (حظر) can only be established by a prohibition command (نهي). Since the command comes from a commander, this implies the existence of a lawgiver. Thus, even the denier's claim of prohibition entails an admission of divine obligation. The author then states that this is the method of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and all Maturidi scholars who hold that good and evil can be known by reason (العقل). This aligns the argument with the broader Maturidi commitment to rational ethical evaluation.

Key Terms

تكليف
الحسن والقبيح بالعقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying an act is prohibited without divine command is possible.
Correction: The author shows that prohibition logically requires a prohibitor (command), which implies a commander.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone says an act is prohibited (محظور) but not obligatory or permitted, what does this necessarily imply?
A) The act is indifferent (مباح)
B) The act is obligatory
C) The existence of a prohibitor (نهي)
D) That the act is rationally evil

Key Takeaway
Even the claim of prohibition entails acknowledgment of a divine lawgiver, supporting the Maturidi view that rational discernment of good and evil is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say something is 'wrong' or 'forbidden,' do you genuinely acknowledge that you are invoking a divine standard, or do you pretend your judgment is purely your own? How does this affect your sense of accountability to God?
?
The passage shows that even the attempt to escape divine command by saying 'it's just prohibited' still logically affirms a Lawgiver. Is there an area of your life where you resist submitting your moral judgments to God? What would it mean to fully surrender that area to His authority?
?
If reason alone can discern good and evil, why do you sometimes ignore what you know to be right? What does that inner conflict reveal about your heart's relationship with the One who commands?

Refuting the Whole-from-Parts Argument with the Weight Analogy
Nasafi refutes the whole-from-parts argument with the weight analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of each part does not make the whole possible, using the weight analogy from Imam Nasafi.

Core Idea
The whole can be impossible even when every part is possible.

Imam Nasafi confronts a common objection to the miraculous nature of the Quran: if each word is possible for humans to produce, why can't they produce the whole Quran? He exposes the logical flaw in this reasoning by drawing a simple yet powerful analogy about carrying pounds. Let's see how he dismantles the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow and the analogy used.
Pay attention to the key terms: جملة (whole), فرد (part/individual), مقدور (possible/power over), مكابرة (stubbornness).
2
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that because each individual word of the Quran can be spoken by humans, the entire Quran (the whole) should also be producible. Nasafi denies this as a false premise.
3
Analyze the weight analogy
Nasafi asks: can a man carry a thousand pounds? No, but each individual pound is carryable. This shows that the whole (thousand pounds) is impossible even though every part (a pound) is possible.
The analogy directly parallels the Quran: each word is like a pound, the whole Quran is like a thousand pounds.
4
Recognize the dilemma
The opponent is caught in a dilemma. If he admits that a whole can be impossible despite its parts being possible, his argument collapses. If he denies either part of the analogy, he is being stubborn (mukabir) and unreasonable.
Nasafi uses logical pressure to force the opponent into self-contradiction.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Reflect on the broader principle: the possibility of individual components does not guarantee the possibility of the whole composition. This is a key rational principle in kalam.
This principle is crucial for defending miracles and understanding divine acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول ما ذكرت من اعتبار الجمل بالأفراد مقدمة كاذبة وقضية مجحودة، دلالة كذبها ما آنفًا مر من قدرة كل من الناس على كل فرد من أفراد كلمات القصائد الفصيحة والخطب البليغة، وعجزهم عن جمل القصائد والخطب. ثم نقول: أليس أن الرجل يعجز عن حمل ألف رطل، وكل رطل من ذلك مقدور الحمل؟ فإن أقر بذلك أبطل دليله وإن جحد أحد شطري هذا الكلام بأن قال: حمل ألف رطل وعشرة آلاف رطل وأزيد من ذلك مقدور لمن كان له حمل كل رطل منه مقدوراً ، أو قال : لما لم يكن حمل ألف رطل مقدورًا لأحد لا يكون حمل رطل مقدوراً له، فقد كابر وعاند.
Then we say: What you mentioned about considering the wholes as composed of individuals is a false premise and a denied proposition; the proof of its falsehood is what has just passed, namely the ability of every person to have power over every individual word of eloquent poems and orations, and their inability to produce the whole poems and orations. Then we say: Is it not that a man is unable to carry a thousand pounds, while every pound of that is capable of being carried? If he acknowledges that, he invalidates his argument. If he denies one of the two parts of this statement by saying that carrying a thousand pounds, ten thousand pounds, or more is possible for someone who can carry every pound of it, or says: since carrying a thousand pounds is not possible for anyone, then carrying one pound is not possible for him, then he has stubbornly resisted and opposed.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's claim that since each individual word of the Quran can be spoken, the entire Quran can be composed by anyone. Nasafi calls this premise false, giving the example of a man who cannot carry a thousand pounds even though each pound is carryable. The opponent is forced into a dilemma: if he admits the analogy, his argument fails; if he denies either part—either claiming that a sum of carryable pounds is always carryable, or denying that a single pound is carryable if the sum is not—he is simply being obstinate (mukabir wa ‘anid). Thus the argument is invalid.

Key Terms
جملة
فرد
مقدور
مكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the analogy of carrying a thousand pounds illustrate?
A) That a whole is always easier than its parts.
B) That the impossibility of a whole does not imply the impossibility of its parts.
C) That the Quran is like a heavy load that no one can carry.
D) That the opponent's argument is sound.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of individual parts does not guarantee the possibility of the whole; this principle is essential for understanding miracles like the inimitability of the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to achieve a significant spiritual goal (like consistent night prayer), do you minimize the value of the small acts you do (like a single prayer)? How does the weight analogy expose the error of thinking that either the whole is possible or the parts are useless?
?
Have you ever assumed that because you can control your tongue in one conversation, you can easily avoid all sins of speech? What does this lesson teach you about the difference between individual ease and cumulative difficulty?
?
The opponent stubbornly denies the obvious when cornered. Where in your life do you resist a clear truth because it would require you to change your worldview or admit a deeper limitation? Will you face that stubbornness today?

The Logical Analogy of Carrying a Thousand Pounds
Refutes an opponent's inability argument using a logical analogy about parts and whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a logical analogy about parts and whole refutes an opponent's argument in kalam.

Core Idea
Inability to carry the whole does not imply inability to carry its parts.

In theological debates, opponents sometimes argue that since producing the entire Qur'an is impossible, producing even a single surah is impossible. Imam Nasafi refutes this by drawing a simple analogy: a man may be unable to carry a thousand pounds at once, yet he can carry each pound individually. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice how the author structures the refutation as a conditional argument.
The passage uses a rhetorical question to engage the opponent.
2
Identify the two possible responses
The author presents two ways the opponent could respond: either admit the analogy (which invalidates his argument) or deny one side of it (which leads to obstinacy). Identify each response in the text.
Look for the phrases 'أقر بذلك' and 'جحد أحد شطري هذا الكلام'.
3
Understand the logical principle
Reflect on why the analogy works: inability to perform a composite act does not entail inability to perform its components. This is a key logical principle in kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أليس أن الرجل يعجز عن حمل ألف رطل، وكل رطل من ذلك مقدور الحمل؟ فإن أقر بذلك أبطل دليله وإن جحد أحد شطري هذا الكلام بأن قال: حمل ألف رطل وعشرة آلاف رطل وأزيد من ذلك مقدور (١) لمن كان له حمل كل رطل منه مقدوراً ، أو قال : لما لم يكن حمل ألف رطل مقدورًا لأحد لا يكون حمل رطل مقدوراً له، فقد كابر وعاند.
Then we say: Is it not that the man is incapable of carrying a thousand pounds, while every pound of that is possible to carry? If he acknowledges that, he invalidates his argument; but if he denies one of the two parts of this statement by saying: 'Carrying a thousand pounds, ten thousand pounds, and more than that is possible for one for whom carrying every pound of it is possible,' or if he says: 'Since carrying a thousand pounds is not possible for anyone, carrying a pound is not possible for him,' then he has acted in obstinacy and stubbornness.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of an opponent's argument. The author uses an analogy: a man cannot carry a thousand pounds at once, but he can carry each individual pound. The opponent's claim likely is that since the whole Quran cannot be produced, then even a single surah cannot be produced. The author points out that if the opponent admits that the whole is impossible but a part is possible, his argument collapses. If he denies one side—either that the whole is impossible or that a part is possible—he is being obstinate. The analogy demonstrates that inability to perform a compound act does not entail inability to perform its components.

Key Terms
عجز
مقدور
كابر
عاند

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that inability to produce the whole Quran implies inability to produce any part of it.
Correction: The analogy shows that inability to carry a thousand pounds does not mean inability to carry each pound; similarly, inability to produce the whole Quran does not negate ability to produce a single surah.

Checkpoint
What point does the analogy of carrying a thousand pounds argue against?
A) The opponent's claim that the Quran cannot be divided into parts.
B) The opponent's argument that inability to produce the entire Quran means inability to produce a single surah.
C) The idea that miracles can be rationally analyzed.
D) The doctrine of sarfa (turning away).

Key Takeaway
The logical principle that inability to perform a composite action does not entail inability to perform its components.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponent denied an obvious fact out of obstinacy. Is there a truth about your own weakness or limitation that you have been denying, holding you back from taking the small steps that are within your power? What would it cost you to admit it?
?
This parable shows that the whole may be impossible while the parts are possible. Are you using the impossibility of perfection as an excuse to avoid doing anything at all? What is one 'pound' of worship or character that you can lift today?

The Five Forms of Impossibility in Denying Occurrence
Al-Nasafi enumerates five categories of impossibility for denying an event.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five logical categories that exhaust all possible grounds for denying the occurrence of any event, as outlined by Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
All denials of occurrence fit into one of five unavoidable logical forms.

This passage presents a key logical tool from Al-Nasafi: a classification of the ways opponents might claim something is impossible. By understanding these five forms, you can recognize and evaluate any objection to a claim of occurrence. The author shows that any denial must fit one of these categories, making the framework exhaustive and essential for theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall structure. Notice that it opens with a statement that denial of occurrence relies on five aspects, then lists them one by one.
The passage uses ordinal markers (أحدها, والثاني, etc.) to organize the forms.
2
Examine each form
Go through each of the five forms carefully. For each, note the type of impossibility and the example given by Al-Nasafi. This will help you understand the logical category.
The first form is inherent impossibility, the second is prevention by opposites, etc.
3
Consider the exhaustive nature
Reflect on why Al-Nasafi says these five forms 'do not go beyond them'. This means any objection to a claimed event must fall into one of these categories. Think of a potential objection and see if it fits.
This classification is a tool for analyzing arguments in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا لأن إنكار حدوث ما يدعي أحد حدوثه وإدعاء استحالته يعتمد وجوهاً خمسة لا يعدوها. صور المستحيلات: أحدها: أن ذلك الأمر الذي أخطر بالبال وجوده يكون مستحيل الثبوت ممتنع الوجود كوجود الشريك والصاحبة والولد للصانع. والثاني: أن يكون شيء ما مانعا من وجوده وثبوته كوجود البياض في محل قام به السواد وكذا غيرهما من الأضداد المتعاقبة. والثالث: أن يكون ذلك الأمر عظيمًا في نفسه لفوت قدرة من يدعي إيجاده كالإحياء والإماتة من جهة العباد، والإتيان بالشمس من المغرب على ما حاج إبراهيم عليه السلام اللعين وبهته وأفحمه. والرابع: أن يكون الخلل في جهة الفاعل بأن كان ممن يتعذر عليه نوع ذلك الفعل. والخامس: أن يكون ذلك الأمر أمرًا محكمًا متقنا في نفسه فلا يتصور حصوله ممن لا علم لربه.
And this is because denial of the occurrence of what someone claims to have occurred and the claim of its impossibility relies on five aspects that do not go beyond them. The forms of impossibilities: First: that the matter which is brought to mind for its existence is impossible of realization, precluded from existence, like the existence of a partner, a consort, and a child for the Creator. Second: that there is something preventing its existence and realization, like the existence of whiteness in a locus where blackness has occurred, and likewise other successive opposites. Third: that the matter is great in itself due to the impossibility of power of the one who claims to produce it, like giving life and causing death on the part of servants, and bringing the sun from the west, as Ibrahim (peace be upon him) argued with the accursed one, baffling and silencing him. Fourth: that the defect is in the aspect of the agent, such that the type of that act is difficult for him. Fifth: that the matter is firmly established and perfected in itself, so it cannot be imagined to occur from one who has no knowledge of his Lord.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of five categories of impossibility that an opponent might invoke to deny the occurrence of something. The author states that any denial of a claimed event or assertion of its impossibility must fall into one of these five faces. First, the thing may be inherently impossible, like attributing a partner, spouse, or child to God. Second, a preventing factor may exist, such as the coexistence of opposites like whiteness and blackness in the same locus. Third, the thing may be too great for the power of the one who claims to do it, as with giving life and death by servants, or bringing the sun from the west—a reference to Abraham's debate with Nimrod. Fourth, the defect may lie in the agent, i.e., the agent is incapable of that type of action. Fifth, the thing may be so perfectly and firmly established that it cannot be produced by someone lacking knowledge of it, as in the case of the Quran's inimitability. These five covering all possibilities, any objection to a claim of occurrence must fit into one of these categories.

Key Terms
مستحيل الثبوت
ممتنع الوجود
الأضداد المتعاقبة
فوت القدرة
الخلل في جهة الفاعل
محكم متقن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These five forms are only specific objections and not an exhaustive classification.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explicitly states that denial of occurrence relies on these five aspects and does not go beyond them, making the classification exhaustive.

Checkpoint
Which face of impossibility involves a preventing factor that blocks the existence of something, like whiteness not coexisting with blackness in the same place?
A) The first face: inherent impossibility in the thing itself.
B) The second face: prevention by successive opposites.
C) The third face: greatness beyond the agent's power.
D) The fifth face: perfection unachievable by the ignorant.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's five forms of impossibility provide a comprehensive logical framework for analyzing any claim that something cannot occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I can't do this,' which of Al-Nasafi's five forms are you using? Is it really impossible, or are you hiding behind your own fear of failure? Commit today to distinguish true impossibility from mere difficulty.
?
Think of a time you dismissed a truth because it seemed too perfect to be from God—like a verse of the Quran that challenged you. Was that the fifth form? What would it mean to surrender your pride and accept that perfect message?
?
Have you ever denied a miracle or a sign from Allah because you thought it was 'too great for anyone to do'? How does the third form expose the arrogance behind that denial? Will you now ask Allah to give you the ability to believe in His power?

The Five Categories of Impossibility in Kalam
Imam Nasafi enumerates five types of impossible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five distinct ways something can be considered impossible in Islamic theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Impossibility is not just logical contradiction—it includes incapacity, prevention, and ignorance.

Imam Nasafi provides a systematic classification of impossibility, showing that not all impossibilities are the same. The five categories range from essential impossibility to impossibility due to a defect in the agent or the perfection of the act. Let's explore each one with its instructive examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to get an overview of the five categories.
Pay attention to how each category is introduced with 'that' (أن).
2
Identify the five categories
The passage lists five types of impossibility. Write them down: essential impossibility, prevention by contrary, incapacity of the claimant, defect in the agent, and perfection requiring knowledge.
Each category has a distinct cause for impossibility.
3
Analyze the examples
For each category, Imam Nasafi gives an example. Reflect on how each example illustrates the specific cause of impossibility.
The example of Ibrahim (AS) with the sun from the west is a powerful illustration of incapacity.
4
Connect to kalam reasoning
Understanding these categories is crucial for theological arguments, especially regarding divine attributes and the impossibility of partners for God.
This classification is foundational for later proofs of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صور المستحيلات
أحدها: أن ذلك الأمر الذي أخطر بالبال وجوده يكون مستحيل الثبوت ممتنع الوجود كوجود الشريك والصاحبة والولد للصانع.
والثاني: أن يكون شيء ما مانعا من وجوده وثبوته كوجود البياض في محل قام به السواد وكذا غيرهما من الأضداد المتعاقبة.
والثالث: أن يكون ذلك الأمر عظيمًا في نفسه لفوت قدرة من يدعي إيجاده كالإحياء والإماتة من جهة العباد، والإتيان بالشمس من المغرب على ما حاج إبراهيم عليه السلام اللعين وبهته وأفحمه.
والرابع: أن يكون الخلل في جهة الفاعل بأن كان ممن يتعذر عليه نوع ذلك الفعل.
والخامس: أن يكون ذلك الأمر أمرًا محكما متقنا في نفسه فلا يتصور حصوله ممن لا علم لربه.
Forms of impossibilities: First: that the matter which is brought to mind as existent is impossible to establish and impossible in existence, like the existence of a partner, consort, or child for the Maker. Second: that something prevents its existence and establishment, like the existence of whiteness in a place where blackness has arisen, and likewise other successive opposites. Third: that the matter is great in itself due to the incapacity of the one who claims to bring it about, like giving life and causing death by servants, and bringing the sun from the west, as when Ibrahim, peace be upon him, argued with the accursed one and confounded him. Fourth: that the defect is in the agent, in that he is one for whom that type of action is impossible. Fifth: that the matter is precise and perfect in itself, so its occurrence from one who has no knowledge is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a classification of impossibilities into five categories. The author begins with the heading 'Forms of impossibilities' and then lists each type. The first is essential impossibility: something that is impossible in itself, like a partner for God. The second is impossibility due to a preventing contrary, like whiteness cannot coexist with blackness in the same spot. The third is impossibility due to the claimant's lack of power, e.g., raising the dead by humans or bringing the sun from the west, as in the argument of Ibrahim. The fourth is impossibility due to a defect in the agent, i.e., the agent lacks the capacity for that kind of act. The fifth is impossibility due to the perfection of the act requiring knowledge, so it cannot come from an ignorant being. Each category is supported by an example, demonstrating a comprehensive view of what makes something impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impossibility only means logical contradiction.
Correction: Imam Nasafi shows that impossibility can also arise from lack of power, prevention by opposites, and lack of knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Essential impossibility (first category)
B) Impossibility due to a preventing contrary (second category)
C) Impossibility due to the claimant's lack of power (third category)
D) Impossibility due to defect in the agent (fourth category)

Key Takeaway
Impossibility in kalam is multi-faceted, encompassing essential, preventive, incapacitative, agent-related, and perfection-related types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What will you surrender to Him today?
?
The fifth category says that a perfect act cannot come from an ignorant one. Where in your worship are you content with ignorance—repeating rituals without seeking to know the One you worship? Will you commit to learning one thing about Allah's names and attributes this week that you have been neglecting?
?
The second category shows that opposites cannot coexist, like belief and disbelief. Is there any area in your heart where you are trying to mix love for this world with love for Allah? What concrete step will you take to remove that contradiction and purify your intention?

The Rational Basis and Wisdom of Charity
Al-Nasafi explains the rational wisdom behind charity and the obligation of zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational requirements for charity as derived by the intellect and confirmed by revelation, and appreciate the profound wisdom in the detailed rules of zakat.

Core Idea
Charity is not just a command; it aligns with reason, dignity, and sincere devotion to Allah.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi explains why charity is not an arbitrary obligation but a requirement that reason itself demands. By examining human nature and the intellect, the author shows that true charity involves giving the best of what we have, doing so willingly, and expecting reward only from Allah. The Quran then confirms these principles, and the intricate rules of zakat point to a wisdom beyond full human comprehension.

Guided Path
1
Understand the human condition
The author begins by noting that the soul is naturally stingy, yet human honor is tied to generosity. This sets the stage for why charity is needed.
2
Identify the three intellectual requirements
Read the passage to find the three requirements that the intellect ('aql) imposes on charity: giving good things, giving willingly, and expecting reward only from Allah.
3
See the Quranic confirmation
The author cites verses that confirm each of the three requirements. Notice how each verse addresses one aspect.
4
Reflect on the depth of zakat's wisdom
The concluding lines stress that the detailed regulations of zakat (types of wealth, recipients) contain a wisdom so deep that even a lifetime of contemplation cannot exhaust it.
This shows that Islamic law is not arbitrary but rooted in profound divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المصالح، إذا النفس مجبولة على الضن بالمال وشرف الإنسان مقرون بإفاضة الجود على وجوه الخيرات، ثم العقل يقتضي أن يتقرب العبد إلى خالقه بأداء ما هو الطيب دون الخبيث، وأن يتسارع إلى أداء ذلك بطيب نفس منه دون الكراهية (1) والكسل، وأن لا يتوقع به الجزاء إلا من الله تعالى إذ هو يتقرب إليه ولا يمن على المعطي بما فعل، فورد ما بلغه محمد عليه السلام من الكتاب بذلك كله قال في الأول: يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا أَنفِقُوا مِن طَيِّبَتِ مَا كَسَبْتُمْ (٢). (د) وقال في الثاني: الَّذِينَ يُنفِقُونَ أَمْوَالَهُمُ ابْتِغاءَ مَرْضَاتِ اللَّهِ وَتَلْبِينَا مِنْ أَنفُسِهِمْ (۳) وقال في ذم من يضاد هذه الحالة (٤): ﴿ وَلَا يَأْتُونَ الصَّلَوَة إِلَّا وَهُمْ كُسَالَى وَلَا يُنفِقُونَ إِلَّا ٥٤ (0) وَهُمْ كَرِهُونَ ) . ثم وقال في الثالث: لَا تُبْطِلُوا صَدَقَتِكُم بِالْمَنْ وَالْأَذَى ) (۱) وقال : يُؤْتِي مَالَهُ يَتَزَكَى ()() يبين أصناف الأموال وما يجب في كل صنف، وأصناف من يجب الصرف إليهم على وجه لو (۸) استغرق الحكيم عمره بالتأمل ليقف (۹) على كنه ما في ذلك من الحكمة لما وقف عليه، والله الموفق.
Benefits: whereas the soul is naturally disposed to stinginess with wealth, and the honor of a person is linked to the pouring forth of generosity in ways of good things. Then the intellect requires that the servant draw near to his Creator by performing that which is good, not that which is evil; and that he hasten to perform that with a willing soul without dislike and laziness; and that he not expect recompense except from God Almighty, since he is drawing near to Him, and not reproach the giver for what he has done. Then what Muhammad, peace be upon him, conveyed from the Book came with all of that. He said concerning the first: 'O you who have believed, spend from the good things you have earned' (2). He said concerning the second: 'Those who spend their wealth seeking the pleasure of God and confirming [their] selves' (3). And He said in condemnation of the one who opposes this state (4): 'And they do not come to prayer except while they are lazy, and they do not spend except while they are unwilling' (54). Then He said concerning the third: 'Do not invalidate your charities with reproach and injury' (1), and He said: 'He gives his wealth to purify himself' (?). It explains the categories of wealth and what is due in each category, and the categories of those to whom it should be given, in a manner that if a wise man devoted his whole life to contemplating the inner reality of the wisdom in that, he would not attain it. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a rational and scriptural proof for the obligation and wisdom of charity. The author begins by describing a human psychological reality: the soul is naturally stingy, yet nobility lies in generosity. The intellect ('aql) then imposes three requirements: (1) giving only good things, not bad; (2) giving willingly, not reluctantly; (3) expecting reward only from God, not from people. Then the author brings Quranic verses as confirmations: the first verse commands spending from good earnings; the second praises those who spend seeking God's pleasure with certainty of heart; the third condemns those who pray lazily and spend unwillingly; the fourth prohibits invalidating charity by reproach and injury; and the fifth describes the one who gives to purify himself. The passage concludes that the detailed regulations of zakat—specifying types of wealth and recipients—are so profound that even a lifelong contemplation could not exhaust their wisdom. The author affirms reliance on God.

Two Divisions of Istita'a
Nasafi enumerates two types of istita'a and defines each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi distinction between the two types of istita'a (capability): the soundness of means as a precondition and the accidental power as the direct cause of action.

Core Idea
Istita'a is not one thing: it is both readiness and the actual power given at the moment of action.

Nasafi opens this section by clarifying that what we call 'qudra' and 'istita'a' actually refers to two distinct realities. Before we can understand how human action relates to divine power, we must first grasp this foundational distinction. In this passage, he defines each type, explains its relationship to action, and notes the points of agreement and disagreement with the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the passage begins with 'ثم الأصل أن المسمى باسم القدرة والاستطاعة عندنا قسمان'. This signals a classification.
The passage enumerates two distinct kinds of capacity.
2
Understand the first division: soundness of means
Focus on the definition: 'سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات'. Note that it is a precondition but not a cause; actions cannot exist without it, but it does not cause them.
The first type is defined as readiness to execute when the free agent wills.
3
Understand the second division: accidental power
The second type is an accident (عرض) created by Allah in the living being at the moment of action. It is the actual cause (علة) of the voluntary action.
This is a direct cause, unlike the first type.
4
Note the historical context
Nasafi mentions that the Baghdad school of Mu'tazila agreed with this view, while the Basrans disagreed, claiming it is merely a cause (سبب) not the direct cause.
This shows the intra-Mu'tazili debate on the nature of capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن المسمى باسم القدرة والاستطاعة عندنا قسمان: أحدهما سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات وهي قد تتقدم الأفعال، وحقيقتها ليست بمحصولة عللاً للأفعال وإن كانت الأفعال لا تقوم إلا بها لكنها نعم من الله تعالى يكرم بها من يشاء ثم ( ) يستأديهم شكرها عند احتمالهم العلم بالنعم وبلوغ عقولهم الوقوف عليها، وهذا النوع من الاستطاعة يحد بأنها : التهيؤ لتنفيذ الفعل عند إرادة المختار . والقسم الثاني : معنى لا يمكن تبين حده بمعنى يشار إليه سوى أنه ليس إلا للفعل، وهو عرض يخلقه الله تعالى في الحيوان يفعل به أفعاله الاختيارية وهو علة للفعل عندنا، وساعدنا عليه البغدادية من المعتزلة، وأنكرت البصرية ذلك وزعمت أنه سبب، وفي الجملة يحصل المحدث فاعلاً به.
Then the principle is that what is named by the name qudra (ability) and istita'a (capability) with us is of two kinds: One of them is the soundness of means (salamat al-asbab) and the health of instruments (sihhat al-alat), and it may precede actions. Its reality is not attained as causes for actions, even though actions are only established through it. Rather, it is a blessing from Allah, the Exalted, with which He honors whomever He wills, then He requires from them gratitude for it when they are capable of knowing blessings and their minds reach to grasp them. This type of istita'a is defined as: the readiness to execute the action when the free agent wills. The second kind: a meaning that cannot be made clear in its definition by a meaning pointed to, except that it is only for the action. It is an accident (arad) that Allah, the Exalted, creates in the living being, by which it performs its voluntary actions, and it is the cause (‘illa) of the action with us. The Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila agreed with us on this, while the Basrans denied that and claimed that it is a cause (sabab), and in sum, the originated thing (muhdath) becomes an agent by it.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition. Nasafi states that according to the Maturidi school (and likely the Ahl al-Sunna in general), what is called 'qudra' and 'istita'a' is divided into two kinds. The first kind is called 'soundness of means' and 'health of instruments'—it precedes actions and is a precondition, but it is not the cause of the action. Rather, it is a divine blessing for which gratitude is required once the person is capable of recognizing it. This type is defined as 'the readiness to execute the action when the free agent wills.' The second kind is a meaning that cannot be defined by pointing to something else; it is an accident created by Allah in the living being at the moment of action, enabling the performance of voluntary acts. For Nasafi, this second kind is the actual cause (‘illa) of the action. He notes that the Baghdad school of Mu'tazila agreed with this position, while the Basran Mu'tazila rejected it, claiming that this capacity is merely a 'cause' (sabab) and not the direct cause. The passage ends by stating that, in summary, the created thing becomes an agent through this second capacity.

Key Terms
سلامة الأسباب
صحة الآلات
التهيؤ لتنفيذ الفعل
عرض
علة
سبب
البغدادية
البصرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soundness of means (first type of istita'a) is the direct cause of the action.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states that the first type is not attained as a cause for actions; it is only a precondition and readiness.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the definition of the first type of istita'a (soundness of means)?
A) The cause that necessarily produces the action.
B) The readiness to execute the action when the free agent wills.
C) An accident created by Allah at the moment of action.
D) The power to do both good and evil.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi distinguishes two types of human capability: the pre-existing soundness of means (a precondition) and the accidental power created by Allah at the moment of action (the actual cause).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed your ability to perform an action solely to your health and resources (first type), forgetting that the very power to act in that moment is a direct creation of Allah (second type)? How can you train yourself to acknowledge His immediate involvement in every deed?
?
If the second type of istita'a is created at the moment of action, then every good deed you do—even the ability to lift your hand in charity or speak a kind word—is a fresh gift from Allah. Does this realization change how you feel about your own efforts? What does it demand from your heart in terms of gratitude and humility?

The Mu'tazila Proof That Capacity Precedes the Act
The author announces the discussion of whether capacity precedes the act and presents the Mu'tazila's scriptural and rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's scriptural proofs for the claim that capacity precedes the act, and the initial structure of their rational argument.

Core Idea
Capacity must be prior to the act, or the command would be impossible.

This lesson examines the Mu'tazila's case for the precedence of capacity over the act. They base their argument on two Quranic verses and a rational premise. Although the rational argument is incomplete in the text, the scriptural proofs stand on their own. Understanding this argument is key to the broader debate about human capacity and divine command.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the Mu'tazila's position and their method of proof.
Notice that the author announces the issue before presenting the proofs.
2
Identify the two scriptural proofs
Note the two Quranic verses the Mu'tazila cite and how they interpret each to support their claim that capacity must precede the act.
The first verse is about fearing Allah as much as you are able; the second about taking with strength.
3
Consider the rational argument
Even though the rational argument cuts off, follow its logic: if capacity were not prior, the command to a disbeliever to have faith would be impossible.
Think about why the Mu'tazila see this as a problem.
4
Reflect on the broader debate
Consider why this issue matters: it affects how we understand human responsibility, divine commands, and God's justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنتكلم في أن الاستطاعة متقدمة على الفعل أم مقارنة له، ويدخل الكلام في أكثر الفصول تحت الكلام في هذه المسألة. حجة المعتزلة بتقدم الاستطاعة: فاحتجت المعتزلة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَاتَّقُوا اللَّهَ مَا اسْتَطَعْتُمْ ﴾ فينبغي أن يكون كل من لزمه التقوى مع استطاعة التقوى. واحتجوا أيضا بقوله: ﴿ خُذُوا مَا ءَاتَيْنَاكُم بِقُوَّةٍ ﴾ والأخذ بقوة لن يتصور أن تكون القوة سابقة على الأخذ كالأخذ باليد لن يتصور إلا وأن تكون اليد سابقة عليه. والمعقول لهم أن القدرة لو لم تكن سابقة على الفعل ولم تكن مع الكافر قدرة الإيمان سابقة على الإيمان لكان الأمر له بالإيمان محالاً لما فيه...
We will now discuss whether capacity precedes the act or is concurrent with it, and the discussion of most chapters enters under the discussion of this issue. The Mu'tazila's proof for the precedence of capacity: The Mu'tazila argued by the saying of the Exalted: “So fear Allah as much as you are able” (Quran 64:16). So it should be that everyone upon whom piety is obligatory has with him the capacity for piety. And they also argued by His saying: “Take what We have given you with strength” (Quran 7:171). And taking with strength cannot be conceived unless the strength is prior to the taking, just as taking with the hand cannot be conceived unless the hand is prior to it. And their rational argument is that if capacity were not prior to the act, and if with the disbeliever there were not a capacity for faith prior to faith, then commanding him to believe would be impossible because of what it contains...
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage transitions to the discussion of whether capacity precedes the act or is concurrent with it, noting that many topics fall under this question. Then it presents the Mu'tazila's proof for the precedence of capacity. Their first proof is scriptural: the verse 'So fear Allah as much as you are able' implies that one has capacity for piety before performing it, because the command to fear is directed at all, including those not currently pious, so they must have capacity for piety prior to their piety. Their second proof is another verse: 'Take what We have given you with strength,' which implies that the strength (al-quwwa) must be prior to the taking (al-akhdh), just as the hand must be prior to grasping. Finally, their rational argument: if capacity were not prior to the act, and if the disbeliever did not have a capacity for faith prior to his faith, then the command to him to believe would be impossible (muhal) because it would involve... (the passage cuts off).

Key Terms
استطاعة
تقدم
قوة
حجة
تالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila's scriptural proof is sufficient to establish the priority of capacity, but the Ash'aris respond by interpreting 'as much as you are able' as referring to the capacity that is concurrent with the act.
Correction: The Mu'tazila interpret the verse as proof of precedence, but Ash'aris argue that the capacity is concurrent with the act, not prior.

Checkpoint
Which verse did the Mu'tazila use to argue that capacity must precede the act?
A) 'Take what We have given you with strength'
B) 'So fear Allah as much as you are able'
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use Quranic verses to argue that capacity must exist before the act, or else the command to do the act would be impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to obey a clear command of Allah, do you often tell yourself that you simply weren't 'able' to do it at that moment? How does this lesson challenge you to examine whether that feeling of inability is a genuine lack of capacity or a excuse for negligence?
?
If you truly believed that Allah grants you the capacity to obey every command before you even attempt it, how would your approach to prayer, fasting, or other obligations change starting today?
?
Consider a specific sin you struggle with. According to the Mu'tazila logic, since Allah commands you to avoid it, He must have given you the capacity to avoid it before the temptation arises. Does this truth convict you to rely on Allah's prior gift of strength rather than your own weakness? What step will you take tonight to act on that conviction?

The Intuitive Ugliness and Power Relation Arguments
Imam al-Nasafi refutes Mu'tazilite arguments using intuition and logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two Mu'tazilite arguments refuted by Imam al-Nasafi: the intuitive ugliness of commanding the incapable and the impossibility of power relating to the existent.

Core Idea
Commanding the incapable is rationally ugly, and power only relates to the non-existent.

This lesson examines the opening refutations where the author appeals to common sense (badahat al-'aql) and logical analysis of power (qudra). The Mu'tazila claimed that human power must precede the act and is a condition for it; here the author shows that such reasoning leads to absurd commands and metaphysical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Focus on the two distinct arguments presented: the intuitive argument about commanding the incapable, and the metaphysical argument about power and existence.
Notice the use of 'qabīḥ' (ugly) and 'badahat al-'aql' (primary intuition).
2
Understand the intuitive argument
Why is commanding a lame man to run considered foolish? This shows that obligating someone without ability is rationally unacceptable.
3
Analyze the power relation argument
The author argues that power (qudra) cannot relate to something already existent, only to non-existent. If power coexists with the act, it relates to the existent, which is impossible.
Contrast this with the Mu'tazilite view that power precedes the act.
4
Connect to the Mu'tazilite position
How do these arguments undermine the claim that ability must precede the act? Reflect on the implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو قبيح في بديهة العقل فإن من أمر عبده المقعد بالعدو وعبده الأعمى بالنظر يعد سفيهًا خارجًا عن الحكمة، ولأن قدرة الإيجاد يستحيل تعلقها بالموجود لما في إيجاد الموجود من الاستحالة فإذا هي تتعلق بالمعدوم ليوجد بها، وإنما تكون قدرة على المعدوم دون الموجود أن لو كانت سابقة عليه، فأما إذا كانت مقارنة للفعل فهي متعلقة بالموجود، وهو محال، ولأن القدرة لو كانت الفعل لم تكن القدرة بكونها علة لوجود الفعل أولى من القلب إذ خروجها من العدم إلى الوجود مطابقا فلا يمكن إضافة وجود أحدهما إلى الآخر بل أضيف وجودهما جميعًا إلى غيرهما، ولو أضيف وجود الفعل إلى القدرة مع استواء حالهما لجاز إضافة وجود القدرة إلى الفعل، ولأنه لو كان لا يؤمن حتى يقدر ولا يقدر حتى يؤمن يبقى أبدًا غير مؤمن كالواقع في البشر إذا كان لا يخرج حتى يأتيه بالحبل ولا يأتيه بالحبل حتى يخرج لم يخرج أبدًا، ولأن الكافر لو لم يكن معه قدرة الإيمان لكان معذورًا ولم يكن تعذيبه عدلاً، إذ لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم من أن يقول: لو قيل له لم لم تفعل كذا؟ فيقول : لأني لم أقدر عليه، فمثله في الغائب، ويسألون ويقولون هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو قادر عليها مراقبة الله تعالى؟ فإن قلتم : لا، فقد أعظمتم القول في وصف الأنبياء عليهم السلام، وينبغي أن لا يثاب في الاتقاء عن المعاصي وإن قلتم : نعم، فقد أقررتم بوجود الاستطاعة ولا فعل. كلامنا هي الشبه المعروفة لهم، ولهم سوى هذه الشبهة يُذكر بعضها في أثناء إن شاء الله تعالى.
And it is ugly in the primary intuition of the intellect, for whoever commands his lame servant to run and his blind servant to look is considered foolish, outside of wisdom. And because the power of bringing into existence cannot possibly be related to an existent, due to the impossibility in bringing into existence something already existent; so it is related to the non-existent to bring it into existence. And power is over the non-existent, not the existent, if it precedes the act. As for when it coexists with the act, then it is related to the existent, and that is impossible. And because if power were the act, then power being the cause of the existence of the act would not be more appropriate than the opposite, since both emerge from non-existence to existence in a similar manner; thus the existence of one cannot be attributed to the other; rather their existence is attributed to something else. And if the existence of the act were attributed to power while both are equal, then the existence of power could be attributed to the act. And because if one does not believe until he is able, and is not able until he believes, he will remain forever a non-believer, like someone in a pit who does not get out until he brings a rope, and does not bring a rope until he gets out, so he never gets out. And because if the disbeliever does not have the power to believe, he would be excused and his punishment would not be just; for there is no excuse for a servant in the witnessed world greater than saying, if asked 'Why did you not do such-and-such?', 'Because I was not able to do it.' Similarly in the unseen. And they ask: Does anyone refrain from disobeying Allah while being capable of it, out of fear of Allah? If you say: No, then you have greatly wronged in describing the prophets, peace be upon them, and they should not be rewarded for refraining from sins. And if you say: Yes, then you have admitted the existence of ability (al-istita'a) and no act. Our speech is the known confusions (al-shubah) for them, and besides this confusion, some will be mentioned elsewhere, if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a series of logical arguments refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that human power (qudra) must precede the act and is a condition for it. The author first states that commanding an incapable person is intuitively ugly (qabīḥ), using the example of a lame man being ordered to run. He then argues that the power to create cannot relate to an existent, only a non-existent. If power coexists with the act, it relates to the existent, which is impossible. Next, he presents a regress argument: if faith depends on ability and ability on faith, one never attains faith, analogous to a man in a pit who needs a rope to get out but cannot get the rope without getting out. He then argues that if a disbeliever lacked the power to believe, he would be excused from punishment, which is false. Finally, he poses a dilemma about refraining from sin while capable: if the Mu'tazilites deny anyone can refrain while capable, they slander the prophets; if they affirm it, they admit ability exists without an act. The passage concludes by noting that these are known confusions (shubah) of the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
بديهة العقل (badahat al-'aql)
معدوم (ma'dum)
موجود (mawjud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That ability must precede the act and is a condition for it, as claimed by the Mu'tazila.
Correction: The author shows that commanding an incapable person is intuitively ugly, and that power can only relate to the non-existent, not the existent act.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to argue that commanding an incapable person is irrational?
A) A scriptural verse
B) An example of ordering a lame man to run
C) A consensus of scholars
D) A logical proof from causality

Key Takeaway
Commanding an incapable person is rationally repugnant, and power can only bring non-existents into existence, not relate to existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If commanding a lame man to run is rationally ugly, what does this say about your own expectations of others – especially those you know are incapable? Have you ever judged someone unfairly for failing to do what they were never empowered to do? Repent and commit to seeing people as Allah sees them.
?
The passage shows that power can only relate to what does not yet exist. How does this reframe your understanding of your own abilities? Are you taking credit for acts that Allah alone created, or excusing yourself from acts you could do by claiming inability? Confront your soul: where is your reliance misplaced?
?
The Mu'tazilites believed ability must be present with the act – but the author shows this leads to impossibility. Have you ever felt burdened by the expectation that you must produce good deeds from your own power? Lay down that false burden; your only power is from Allah, and He does not command you beyond your capacity. Will you trust Him completely today?

The Causal Regress and Faith-Ability Circle
Imam al-Nasafi presents logical paradoxes against Mu'tazilite causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the causal regress argument and the infinite regress of faith and ability, showing that ability cannot be a condition for belief.

Core Idea
If ability and act are interdependent, neither can be the cause of the other, leading to an infinite regress.

In this lesson, the author presents two logical paradoxes that arise from the Mu'tazilite view that ability (qudra) is a condition for the act. The first concerns causality: if ability and act are simultaneous, neither can be the cause. The second imagines a vicious circle: if faith depends on ability and ability on faith, one never attains faith—like a man stuck in a pit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Note the two sub-arguments: the causality argument (power and act equal) and the faith-ability regress.
The text uses 'لو كانت' for hypothetical reasoning.
2
Understand the causality point
Why can't power be the cause of the act if both appear simultaneously? Because both emerge from non-existence similarly, so neither can be attributed to the other.
This is a symmetry argument.
3
Analyze the pit analogy
How does the analogy of a man in a pit illustrate the impossibility of requiring ability before faith? The man needs a rope to get out and needs to get out to get the rope—so he never escapes.
Similarly, if one needs ability to believe and belief to have ability, one never believes.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazilite position
How do these arguments show that ability cannot be a condition for the act?

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Divine Justice and the Dilemma of Refraining from Sin
Imam al-Nasafi uses justice and prophetic example to refute Mu'tazilitism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from justice and the dilemma about refraining from sin, showing that the Mu'tazilite position leads to unjust punishment or slander of the prophets.

Core Idea
If the disbeliever lacks ability, punishment is unjust; if ability exists without action, the Mu'tazilites contradict themselves.

In this final set of arguments, the author appeals to divine justice and the example of the prophets. He argues that if a disbeliever had no power to believe, punishing him would be unjust—contradicting the Mu'tazilite claim of God's justice. Then he poses a dilemma: either no one can refrain from sin while capable (which slanders prophets) or they can (which admits ability without act).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Note the two arguments: the excuse argument and the dilemma about refraining from sin.
The text uses 'لو لم يكن معه قدرة' for the hypothetical.
2
Understand the excuse argument
Why would lack of ability excuse the disbeliever? Because in worldly affairs, 'I was not able' is the strongest excuse.
This invokes the principle of justice: punishing the incapable is unjust.
3
Analyze the dilemma
What are the two options regarding refraining from sin while capable? If the Mu'tazilites deny it, they slander the prophets; if they affirm it, they admit ability exists without the act.
This dilemma forces them to contradict their own position.
4
Conclusion
How do these arguments collectively refute the Mu'tazilite position?
The author ends by calling these 'shubah' (confusions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو قبيح في بديهة العقل فإن من أمر عبده المقعد بالعدو وعبده الأعمى بالنظر يعد سفيهًا خارجًا عن الحكمة، ولأن قدرة الإيجاد يستحيل تعلقها بالموجود لما في إيجاد الموجود من الاستحالة فإذا هي تتعلق بالمعدوم ليوجد بها، وإنما تكون قدرة على المعدوم دون الموجود أن لو كانت سابقة عليه، فأما إذا كانت مقارنة للفعل فهي متعلقة بالموجود، وهو محال، ولأن القدرة لو كانت الفعل لم تكن القدرة بكونها علة لوجود الفعل أولى من القلب إذ خروجها من العدم إلى الوجود مطابقا فلا يمكن إضافة وجود أحدهما إلى الآخر بل أضيف وجودهما جميعًا إلى غيرهما، ولو أضيف وجود الفعل إلى القدرة مع استواء حالهما لجاز إضافة وجود القدرة إلى الفعل، ولأنه لو كان لا يؤمن حتى يقدر ولا يقدر حتى يؤمن يبقى أبدًا غير مؤمن كالواقع في البشر إذا كان لا يخرج حتى يأتيه بالحبل ولا يأتيه بالحبل حتى يخرج لم يخرج أبدًا، ولأن الكافر لو لم يكن معه قدرة الإيمان لكان معذورًا ولم يكن تعذيبه عدلاً، إذ لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم من أن يقول: لو قيل له لم لم تفعل كذا؟ فيقول : لأني لم أقدر عليه، فمثله في الغائب، ويسألون ويقولون هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو قادر عليها مراقبة الله تعالى؟ فإن قلتم : لا، فقد أعظمتم القول في وصف الأنبياء عليهم السلام، وينبغي أن لا يثاب في الاتقاء عن المعاصي وإن قلتم : نعم، فقد أقررتم بوجود الاستطاعة ولا فعل. كلامنا هي الشبه المعروفة لهم، ولهم سوى هذه الشبهة يُذكر بعضها في أثناء إن شاء الله تعالى.
And it is ugly in the primary intuition of the intellect, for whoever commands his lame servant to run and his blind servant to look is considered foolish, outside of wisdom. And because the power of bringing into existence cannot possibly be related to an existent, due to the impossibility in bringing into existence something already existent; so it is related to the non-existent to bring it into existence. And power is over the non-existent, not the existent, if it precedes the act. As for when it coexists with the act, then it is related to the existent, and that is impossible. And because if power were the act, then power being the cause of the existence of the act would not be more appropriate than the opposite, since both emerge from non-existence to existence in a similar manner; thus the existence of one cannot be attributed to the other; rather their existence is attributed to something else. And if the existence of the act were attributed to power while both are equal, then the existence of power could be attributed to the act. And because if one does not believe until he is able, and is not able until he believes, he will remain forever a non-believer, like someone in a pit who does not get out until he brings a rope, and does not bring a rope until he gets out, so he never gets out. And because if the disbeliever does not have the power to believe, he would be excused and his punishment would not be just; for there is no excuse for a servant in the witnessed world greater than saying, if asked 'Why did you not do such-and-such?', 'Because I was not able to do it.' Similarly in the unseen. And they ask: Does anyone refrain from disobeying Allah while being capable of it, out of fear of Allah? If you say: No, then you have greatly wronged in describing the prophets, peace be upon them, and they should not be rewarded for refraining from sins. And if you say: Yes, then you have admitted the existence of ability (al-istita'a) and no act. Our speech is the known confusions (al-shubah) for them, and besides this confusion, some will be mentioned elsewhere, if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a series of logical arguments refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that human power (qudra) must precede the act and is a condition for it. The author first states that commanding an incapable person is intuitively ugly (qabīḥ), using the example of a lame man being ordered to run. He then argues that the power to create cannot relate to an existent, only a non-existent. If power coexists with the act, it relates to the existent, which is impossible. Next, he presents a regress argument: if faith depends on ability and ability on faith, one never attains faith, analogous to a man in a pit who needs a rope to get out but cannot get the rope without getting out. He then argues that if a disbeliever lacked the power to believe, he would be excused from punishment, which is false. Finally, he poses a dilemma about refraining from sin while capable: if the Mu'tazilites deny anyone can refrain while capable, they slander the prophets; if they affirm it, they admit ability exists without an act. The passage concludes by noting that these are known confusions (shubah) of the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
استطاعة (istita'a)
عذر ('udhr)
عدل ('adl)
شبه (shubah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite view of ability is consistent with divine justice and prophetic example.
Correction: The author shows that if ability is lacking, punishment is unjust, and the dilemma about refraining from sin leads to slander of prophets or contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma the author presents to the Mu'tazilites about refraining from sin?
A) Either prophets are slandered or ability exists without act
B) Either ability precedes act or follows it
C) Either God is unjust or humans are not responsible
D) Either belief requires ability or not

Key Takeaway
Punishing those without ability is unjust, and the Mu'tazilite dilemma about refraining from sin forces them to admit ability without act or slander prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have often made excuses for your sins, saying 'I couldn't help it' or 'I was too weak.' But this passage teaches that the greatest excuse in worldly affairs is 'I was not able.' If you truly were not able, you would be excused, and punishing you would be unjust. Yet you know that in many cases, you were able to obey but chose not to. Think of one specific sin you commit repeatedly. Is it because you truly lack the ability, or because you have not mustered the fear of Allah? This lesson demands that you stop hiding behind false excuses. What will you do today to prove you have the ability by refraining?
?
The prophets peace be upon them were fully capable of sin but refrained out of taqwa. You claim to love and follow them, yet you often use your ability as an excuse to sin rather than as a reason to refrain. Where in your life are you fully capable of sin but choose to commit it anyway, betraying the very example you profess to follow? This passage strips away your comfort: if you say no one refrains while capable, you slander the prophets. If you say yes, you must admit you have no excuse. Which answer will you live by from now on?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that ability must always produce action, so if you have ability, you must act. But this leads to the absurdity that prophets would not be rewarded for their taqwa. Do you truly believe that your small acts of obedience—like lowering your gaze or controlling your tongue—are not rewarded because you had the ability to do otherwise? If not, then you must accept that ability and action are separate, and that your refraining from sin is a virtuous choice. Will you start seeing every moment of taqwa as a great act of worship that you chose despite your ability to sin?

The Rational Consensus Against Accidents Remaining Through a Separate Permanence
States the rational consensus and its justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational consensus that accidents cannot remain by a separate attribute of permanence and the logical basis for this impossibility.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot host other accidents; permanence itself would be an accident needing a locus.

Before we examine the specific views of theologians, the author establishes a foundational rational consensus: accidents cannot persist by means of a separate attribute of permanence. This is because such an attribute would itself be an accident subsisting in another accident, which is rationally impossible. This lesson unpacks the logical reasoning behind this consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Read the Arabic statement: 'وأجمع العقلاء: أن العرض لا يجوز أن يبقى ببقاء هو معنى زائدًا على الذات الباقي' and its translation.
Notice the term 'إجماع العقلاء' – rational consensus, not legal consensus.
2
Understand the justification
The text explains why: meanings cannot subsist in accidents because accidents cannot bear other accidents. Reflect on the impossibility of accidents being loci for others.
3
Connect to the broader argument
This consensus is used later to refute Abu Hudhayl. Consider how this principle underpins the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجمع العقلاء: أن العرض لا يجوز أن يبقى ببقاء هو معنى زائدًا على الذات الباقي إذ قيام المعاني بالأعراض مستحيل لاستحالة قيامها بذاتها وافتقارها إلى محل تحله ويستحيل كونها محال بأغيارها غير أن أبا الهذيل يقول بأنها تبقى من أجل بقاء لا في محل، وفي تقرر استحالة وجود بقاء غير قائم بمحل ما يبطل كلامه، لأن البقاء الحادث عرض، والعرض لا يوجد بدون محل كما في سائر أنواع الأعراض، وقد بينا قبل هذا ما يوجب بطلان ذلك ويقال له: إذا كان البقاء موجودًا لا في محل فما الذي جعل ذاتاً متعينًا أولى بأن يكون باقيًا به من ذات آخر سواه؟ ولم كان هذا بقاء له دون غيره؟ ويقال له: إنك أطلقت القول باستحالة بقاء الحركات والإرادات فما الذي يمنع الله تعالى من أن يقول لهما: ابقيا ؟ ويطالب بالفرق بين الحركات والإرادات، وبين غيرهما من الأعراض ولن يجد سبيلا إليه
And the rational persons agreed that an accident is not permitted to remain through a permanence that is a meaning additional to the remaining essence, because the subsistence of meanings in accidents is impossible due to the impossibility of their subsistence in themselves and their need for a locus that they inhere in, and it is impossible for them to be loci for others. But Abu Hudhayl says that they remain because of a permanence that is not in a locus. And in establishing the impossibility of the existence of a permanence not subsisting in a locus, his statement is invalidated, because originated permanence is an accident, and an accident does not exist without a locus, as in all other types of accidents. And we have clarified before this what necessitates the invalidity of that. And it is said to him: If the permanence exists not in a locus, then what makes a particular essence more deserving to remain by it than another essence? And why is this a permanence for it and not for another? And it is said to him: You have made a general statement about the impossibility of the permanence of motions and wills, so what prevents Allah the Exalted from saying to them: 'Remain!'? And he is asked to differentiate between motions and wills and other accidents, and he will not find a way to it.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the logical consensus (إجماع العقلاء) that an accident cannot remain by a separate attribute of permanence (بقاء) because that would require a meaning (معنى) subsisting in an accident, which is impossible since accidents cannot bear other accidents. The author then directly refutes Abu Hudhayl al-ʻAllāf, who claimed that accidents remain due to a permanence that is not located in a substrate. The refutation proceeds by showing that originated permanence is itself an accident and thus requires a locus; if it is not in a locus, it cannot exist. Moreover, rhetorical questions are posed: if permanence exists without a locus, why would it attach to one essence rather than another? And why should Abu Hudhayl deny the permanence of motions and wills when God could command them to remain? He is challenged to give a principled distinction but cannot.

Key Terms
إجماع العقلاء
بقاء
عرض
محل
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'إجماع العقلاء' refers to juristic consensus like in fiqh.
Correction: Here it refers to the consensus of rational thinkers and theologians, not a legal consensus.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given that an accident cannot remain through a separate permanence?
A) Because permanence is a substance
B) Because permanence would itself be an accident needing a locus
C) Because accidents are eternal
D) Because the Qur'an denies it

Key Takeaway
The rational consensus establishes that accidents cannot persist by a separate attribute of permanence because that attribute would itself be an accident requiring a locus, leading to impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your emotions and intentions are accidents—they come and go. Are you building your identity on these shifting states or on the unchanging essence of your faith? What would it mean to anchor your self-worth in Allah's permanence rather than your fleeting moods?
?
The rational consensus teaches that no accident can persist by a separate permanence. In your life, what 'accidents'—like popularity, wealth, or health—are you treating as permanent? Are you ready to release them and rely only on the Eternal?
?
If accidents cannot host other accidents, then your 'self' is not a container for temporary attributes; it is a substance sustained moment by moment by Allah. How does this reshape your understanding of dependence on Him? Will you consciously acknowledge your need for Him every time you feel you 'remain'?

Refutation of Abu Hudhayl on Accidental Permanence
Refutes Abu Hudhayl's position with multiple logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments used to refute Abu Hudhayl al-ʻAllāf's claim that accidents remain by a self-subsistent permanence.

Core Idea
A permanence without a locus is impossible; arguments from differentiation and parity expose the inconsistency.

After establishing the rational consensus, the author directly refutes Abu Hudhayl's minority view that accidents remain due to a permanence that is not located in any substrate. Several penetrating arguments are deployed, from the impossibility of a self-subsistent accident to the challenge of distinguishing between different types of accidents. This lesson traces the logical force of each refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Hudhayl's claim and initial rebuttal
Read the Arabic: 'غير أن أبا الهذيل يقول...' Understand that the first argument is that permanence is an accident and thus requires a locus.
2
Consider the specification problem
If permanence exists without a locus, why would it attach to one essence rather than another? This shows arbitrariness.
3
Examine the parity challenge
Abu Hudhayl denies permanence to motions and wills; but if permanence can be self-subsistent, why can't God command them to remain?
He is asked to differentiate but cannot.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that Abu Hudhayl has no basis for his distinction, thus his position is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجمع العقلاء: أن العرض لا يجوز أن يبقى ببقاء هو معنى زائدًا على الذات الباقي إذ قيام المعاني بالأعراض مستحيل لاستحالة قيامها بذاتها وافتقارها إلى محل تحله ويستحيل كونها محال بأغيارها غير أن أبا الهذيل يقول بأنها تبقى من أجل بقاء لا في محل، وفي تقرر استحالة وجود بقاء غير قائم بمحل ما يبطل كلامه، لأن البقاء الحادث عرض، والعرض لا يوجد بدون محل كما في سائر أنواع الأعراض، وقد بينا قبل هذا ما يوجب بطلان ذلك ويقال له: إذا كان البقاء موجودًا لا في محل فما الذي جعل ذاتاً متعينًا أولى بأن يكون باقيًا به من ذات آخر سواه؟ ولم كان هذا بقاء له دون غيره؟ ويقال له: إنك أطلقت القول باستحالة بقاء الحركات والإرادات فما الذي يمنع الله تعالى من أن يقول لهما: ابقيا ؟ ويطالب بالفرق بين الحركات والإرادات، وبين غيرهما من الأعراض ولن يجد سبيلا إليه
And the rational persons agreed that an accident is not permitted to remain through a permanence that is a meaning additional to the remaining essence, because the subsistence of meanings in accidents is impossible due to the impossibility of their subsistence in themselves and their need for a locus that they inhere in, and it is impossible for them to be loci for others. But Abu Hudhayl says that they remain because of a permanence that is not in a locus. And in establishing the impossibility of the existence of a permanence not subsisting in a locus, his statement is invalidated, because originated permanence is an accident, and an accident does not exist without a locus, as in all other types of accidents. And we have clarified before this what necessitates the invalidity of that. And it is said to him: If the permanence exists not in a locus, then what makes a particular essence more deserving to remain by it than another essence? And why is this a permanence for it and not for another? And it is said to him: You have made a general statement about the impossibility of the permanence of motions and wills, so what prevents Allah the Exalted from saying to them: 'Remain!'? And he is asked to differentiate between motions and wills and other accidents, and he will not find a way to it.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the logical consensus (إجماع العقلاء) that an accident cannot remain by a separate attribute of permanence (بقاء) because that would require a meaning (معنى) subsisting in an accident, which is impossible since accidents cannot bear other accidents. The author then directly refutes Abu Hudhayl al-ʻAllāf, who claimed that accidents remain due to a permanence that is not located in a substrate. The refutation proceeds by showing that originated permanence is itself an accident and thus requires a locus; if it is not in a locus, it cannot exist. Moreover, rhetorical questions are posed: if permanence exists without a locus, why would it attach to one essence rather than another? And why should Abu Hudhayl deny the permanence of motions and wills when God could command them to remain? He is challenged to give a principled distinction but cannot.

Misconception: Some might think Abu Hudhayl's view is based on a valid argument; but the text shows it leads to contradictions.
Correction: The rational arguments demonstrate the impossibility and incoherence of his position.

What is the first argument against Abu Hudhayl's position?
A) That permanence is a substance

?

?

?
Abu Hudhayl was asked to differentiate between types of accidents; he could not. What distinctions have you made in your faith that are actually arbitrary? Are you ready to let go of them for the sake of truth?
