Nasafi's Refutation of Accident Persistence Until Action
Nasafi refutes the objection that accidents persist to make action possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam theologians deny the persistence of accidents and how this refutes the argument that accidents must persist for action to be possible.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot persist; their momentary existence undermines claims about power and obligation at the time of action.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a key objection from those who argue that accidents must persist until an action occurs in order for the agent to have power and obligation. The opponents claim that without persistence, the agent would lack the capacity to act. Nasafi responds by reaffirming the established proof that accidents cannot persist, and draws the logical conclusion that action cannot rely on a power that has already ceased to exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Pay attention to the flow of objection and response.
Notice that the opponents begin with 'فإن قالوا' and Nasafi's reply with 'قيل لهم'.
2
Identify the objection
The opponents claim that accidents persist until the time of action, ensuring power (qudra) and obligation (taklīf). Underline this claim in the translation.
They use the phrase 'بل هي باقية إلى وقت وجود الفعل'.
3
Follow the refutation
Nasafi cites the established proof (dalāla) against accident persistence. Then he draws the consequence: if accidents cannot persist, they become non-existent at the very moment of action, so the action cannot be attributed to that power.
The key logical move is that the time of action coincides with the second moment of the accident's existence, when it has already perished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لا نسلم أن بقاءها مستحيل بل هي باقية إلى وقت وجود الفعل فيكون ذلك الفعل بقدرة موجودة ويكون التكليف ثابتا والفاعل قادراً. قيل لهم : قد أقمنا الدلالة على استحالة القول ببقاء الأعراض، وإذا استحال بقاؤها ولم تبق كانت منعدمة في الثاني من زمان وجودها، وذلك هو زمان وجود الفعل.
For they said: We do not accept that their persistence is impossible; rather, they persist until the time of the existence of the action, so that action is by an existing power (qudra), obligation (taklīf) is established, and the agent is capable. It was said to them: We have established the proof (dalāla) of the impossibility of the claim of the persistence of accidents (a‘rāḍ). And if their persistence is impossible and they do not persist, they become non-existent in the second [moment] of the time of their existence, and that is the time of the existence of the action.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage records an objection and its refutation. The opponents (likely Mu‘tazila) argue that accidents (a‘rāḍ) can persist until the moment an action is performed, thereby ensuring that the agent has power (qudra) at the time of action and that obligation (taklīf) is meaningful. Nasafi refutes this by citing the established proof that the persistence of accidents is impossible. Since they cannot persist, they become non-existent in the second moment of their existence—that is, at the very time when the action would occur. Therefore, the action cannot be attributed to a power that no longer exists.

Key Terms
بقاء الأعراض
قدرة
تكليف
فعل
دلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that accidents can persist indefinitely, allowing the agent to have power at any future time.
Correction: Kalam holds that accidents are momentary and cannot endure; they perish in the second moment of existence, so any action occurring after that moment cannot be attributed to the same power.

Checkpoint
What do the opponents claim about the persistence of accidents?
A) Accidents persist only for a single moment and then perish.
B) Accidents persist until the time of the action, allowing the agent to have power at that moment.
C) Accidents never persist at all.
D) Accidents persist forever without perishing.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of accident persistence means that action cannot be attributed to a power that has already perished, refuting the necessity of persistence for effective agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your ability to act vanishes the moment after you intend it, what does that mean for the excuses you make to delay good deeds? Are you relying on a power that has already expired?
?
The opponents clung to accident persistence to justify obligation. What false 'persistence' do you rely on to justify putting off repentance or worship—your health, your youth, your good intentions? How does this lesson strip that excuse away?
?
Nasafi's refutation shows that your very ability to act is fragile and fleeting. Does this urgency change how you will approach the next act of obedience? What will you do differently today because you know your power does not linger?

Refutation of Successive Powers
Nasafi refutes the argument for successive powers with a dilemma and an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument for successive powers fails and how the analogy of the hand shows the impossibility of an action by a preceding non-existent power.

Core Idea
A power that no longer exists cannot produce an action, whether the gap is one moment or many.

In this passage, Nasafi continues his refutation of the opponents who claim that powers occur successively. He forces them into a dilemma and then provides a powerful analogy to expose the logical flaw in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation dialogue
Read the passage from 'فإن قالوا' to 'لا تخلص لكم'. Notice how Nasafi presents two possible answers from the opponents and refutes each.
This is a typical kalam dialectical exchange.
2
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
The opponents must either say the new power is for the same action or for a different one. Trace the consequences of each.
3
Examine the hand analogy
Read the analogy of striking with a hand that becomes non-existent. Nasafi argues that a one-moment gap is as impossible as a many-moment gap.
The analogy reinforces the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the logical principle
The key principle: if a power is non-existent at the time of action, it cannot cause the action, regardless of the duration of non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن انعدمت تلك القدرة إلا أن قدرة أخرى تحدث عقيبها إذ القدرة تحدث تباعًا في الصحيح السليم. قيل لهم : تلك القدرة الموجودة في الثاني أهي عندكم قدرة هذا الفعل أم قدرة فعل آخر يعقبها؟ وهل يجوز وجود هذا الفعل في الثاني بهذه القدرة الموجودة في الثاني؟ فإن قالوا: هي قدرة هذا الفعل، ويجوز وجود الفعل بهذه القدرة المقارنة له في الوجود. فقد تركوا مذهبهم، حيث جعلوا الفعل حاصلاً بالقدرة المقارنة له في الوجود، وبقيت القدرة السابقة على الفعل فضلاً لا فائدة في وجودها ولا تعلق لها بفعل ما البتة، وهذا محال فساعدوا أهل الحق في الحقيقة وأثبتوا زيادة لا فائدة فيها، فلم يحصلوا من المخالفة إلا على سفه. فإن قالوا: لا يجوز وجود الفعل في الثاني بهذه القدرة المقارنة له بل وجوده بالقدرة المتقدمة، وتلك القدرة الموجودة في الثاني لفعل يوجد في الثالث لا لفعل يوجد مقارناً لها. قيل لهم : إذا كانت هذه القدرة الثابتة لفعل آخر لا لهذا الفعل ويستحيل تعلق الفعل المقارن لها بها بل تعلقه بالقدرة الأولى وحصوله بها لا بهذه، وكان وجود هذه القدرة في حق الفعل وعدمها بمنزلة أن القدرة على فعل لا يوجب زوال العجز عن فعل آخر ، ولزمكم جميع ما ألزمناكم، ولا تخلص لكم بوجه من الوجوه. والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه أن الخصوم ساعدونا على استحالة وجود الفعل بقدرة متقدمة عليه بأوقات كثيرة، فكذا يستحيل وجوده بقدرة متقدمة عليه بوقت واحد لاستوائهما في العدم وقت الفعل، ألا ترى أن البطش بيد كانت ثم انعدمت محال وإن اتصل وجود الفعل بزمان وجود اليد إذا كانت منعدمة وقت الفعل. كما أنه محال في زمان وجد بعد زمان انعدام اليد بمدة مديدة لاستوائهما في عدم اليد زمان البطش فكذا هذا، ويخرج هذا الكلام على طريق السؤال من وجهين: أحدهما من حيث القدرة ، وهو أن القدرة لما لم تكن إلا للفعل وقد تخلو عنه وقتا لم لا يجوز أن تخلو عنه أوقاتا؟ وهذا السؤال يرد أيضا على من يقول ببقاء القدرة من البصريين. والثاني: من حيث الفعل، وهو أنه لما جاز الفصل بالقدرة في الوقت الثاني وإن كانت القدرة فيه منعدمة لم لا يجوز الفعل بها في العاشر وإن كانت منعدمة وتقرير السؤال بالآلة كاليد والسكين وغيرهما، أنه لما استحال حصول الفعل بهما بعد العدم في الوقت العاشر من زمان العدم، فكذا استحال في الثاني منه لاستواء الوقتين في العدم.
For they said: If that power becomes non-existent, but another power occurs after it, since powers occur successively in the healthy sound [person]. It was said to them: That power existing in the second [moment] — according to you, is it the power for this action or the power for another action that follows it? And is it permissible for this action to exist in the second [moment] by this power existing in the second? If they say: It is the power for this action, and it is permissible for the action to exist by this power concurrent with it in existence. Then they have abandoned their doctrine, for they have made the action occur by the power concurrent with it in existence, and the preceding power remains superfluous with no benefit in its existence and no relation at all to any action, and this is impossible. So they have in reality helped the people of truth and established an addition with no benefit in it, and they gained nothing from opposition except foolishness. If they say: It is not permissible for the action to exist in the second [moment] by this power concurrent with it, but rather its existence is by the preceding power, and that power existing in the second is for an action that exists in the third, not for an action existing concurrent with it. It was said to them: If this power is established for another action, not for this action, and it is impossible for the concurrent action to be related to it, but rather its relation is to the first power and its occurrence is by it, not by this [power], and the existence of this power with respect to the action and its non-existence are equivalent — in that power over an action does not necessitate the removal of incapacity for another action — then all that we have charged you with is binding, and you have no escape in any way. And what indicates the correctness of what we hold is that the opponents have agreed with us on the impossibility of the existence of an action by a power preceding it by many times. So likewise, its existence by a power preceding it by one time is impossible, because they are equal in non-existence at the time of the action. Do you not see that striking with a hand that existed then became non-existent is impossible, even if the existence of the action is connected to the time of the existence of the hand if it is non-existent at the time of striking? Just as it is impossible in a time that exists after the time of the non-existence of the hand by a lengthy period, because of the equality of the two times in the non-existence of the hand at the time of striking. So likewise this. And this discourse proceeds according to two ways of questioning: One of them from the perspective of power (qudra) — namely, since power is only for the action, and it may be vacant from it for a time, why is it not permissible for it to be vacant from it for [many] times? And this question also applies to those who hold the persistence of power among the Basrans. And the second: from the perspective of action (fi‘l) — namely, since separation of power in the second moment is allowed, even though the power in it is non-existent, why is the action not permissible by it in the tenth [moment], even though it is non-existent? And the clarification of the question using the instrument (āla) like the hand and the knife and others: since the occurrence of the action by them after non-existence in the tenth moment from the time of non-existence is impossible, so likewise it is impossible in the second moment from it, because of the equality of the two moments in non-existence.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage refutes a second objection and then presents two analytical ways of questioning. The opponents retreat to the claim that although the original power perishes, a new power arises immediately afterward, because powers occur successively in a healthy person. Nasafi responds by forcing them to specify the nature of this new power: is it for the same action or a different one? If they say it is for the same action and can produce it concurrently, then they abandon their own doctrine because they now admit that an action is produced by a concurrent power, rendering the earlier power useless — an absurdity. If they instead say the new power is for a later action and the current action is produced by the preceding power, Nasafi points out that the new power, being for a different action, cannot be related to the current action, and the earlier power is non-existent at the time of action, which the opponents themselves agreed is impossible when the time gap is large. Thus, by parity of reasoning, a one-moment gap is as impossible as a many-moment gap. The analogy of striking with an existing hand that then ceases reinforces this. Finally, Nasafi extracts two general lines of attack (sual): (1) from the nature of power — if power can be temporally vacant from its effect for one moment, why not for many? (2) from the nature of the action — if the action can be produced by a power that is absent at the moment of action (allowing a temporal gap), what is the difference between a one-moment gap and a ten-moment gap? This exposes the inconsistency in the opponents' position.

Key Terms
قدرة
تتابع القدرات
بطش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A temporal gap of one moment between power and action is fundamentally different from a gap of many moments.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the two cases are equivalent because in both, the power is non-existent at the moment of action.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi's hand analogy demonstrate?
A) That a hand can strike even after it ceases to exist.
B) That if a hand is non-existent at the time of striking, the gap duration does not matter.
C) That a one-moment gap is easier to accept than a many-moment gap.
D) That the opponents' view is only wrong for large time gaps.

Key Takeaway
A power that does not exist at the moment of action cannot cause it, no matter how short the temporal gap.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever depended on a past moment of sincere repentance or resolve, thinking its power remains to carry you through today's struggles? What does this lesson say about that assumption, and what must you do now to truly change?
?
If a single moment of negligence can sever the connection between your intention and action, how urgently do you need to examine whether your current deeds are powered by a living, present faith rather than a dead memory of past devotion?
?
The opponents argued that a one-moment gap is different from a large gap. Where in your own life do you excuse small delays in obedience, thinking 'I'll do it soon'—while the lesson proves that delay nullifies the power? What one action will you take immediately, without postponement?

Two Ways of Questioning: Power and Action
Nasafi presents two ways of questioning the opponents' theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two analytical perspectives—power (qudra) and action (fi‘l)—used to question the opponents' position on temporal gaps.

Core Idea
If a power can be vacant for one moment, it can be vacant for many; the logic is the same.

After refuting the opponents, Nasafi extracts two general lines of attack that can be used to question any theory that allows a temporal gap between power and action. These questions highlight the inconsistency in the opponents' reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the two ways of questioning
Read the final part starting from 'ويخرج هذا الكلام'. Identify the two perspectives: from power and from action.
Also note the instrument analogy (hand, knife) that reinforces the point.
2
Understand the question from power
If power can be without its action for one moment, why not for many? This challenges the notion that a single moment gap is special.
3
Understand the question from action
If the action is produced by a power that is absent at the time, then what difference does the length of absence make?
4
Reflect on the instrument analogy
The instrument (like a hand) must exist at the time of striking. The same applies to power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن انعدمت تلك القدرة إلا أن قدرة أخرى تحدث عقيبها إذ القدرة تحدث تباعًا في الصحيح السليم. قيل لهم : تلك القدرة الموجودة في الثاني أهي عندكم قدرة هذا الفعل أم قدرة فعل آخر يعقبها؟ وهل يجوز وجود هذا الفعل في الثاني بهذه القدرة الموجودة في الثاني؟ فإن قالوا: هي قدرة هذا الفعل، ويجوز وجود الفعل بهذه القدرة المقارنة له في الوجود. فقد تركوا مذهبهم، حيث جعلوا الفعل حاصلاً بالقدرة المقارنة له في الوجود، وبقيت القدرة السابقة على الفعل فضلاً لا فائدة في وجودها ولا تعلق لها بفعل ما البتة، وهذا محال فساعدوا أهل الحق في الحقيقة وأثبتوا زيادة لا فائدة فيها، فلم يحصلوا من المخالفة إلا على سفه. فإن قالوا: لا يجوز وجود الفعل في الثاني بهذه القدرة المقارنة له بل وجوده بالقدرة المتقدمة، وتلك القدرة الموجودة في الثاني لفعل يوجد في الثالث لا لفعل يوجد مقارناً لها. قيل لهم : إذا كانت هذه القدرة الثابتة لفعل آخر لا لهذا الفعل ويستحيل تعلق الفعل المقارن لها بها بل تعلقه بالقدرة الأولى وحصوله بها لا بهذه، وكان وجود هذه القدرة في حق الفعل وعدمها بمنزلة أن القدرة على فعل لا يوجب زوال العجز عن فعل آخر ، ولزمكم جميع ما ألزمناكم، ولا تخلص لكم بوجه من الوجوه. والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه أن الخصوم ساعدونا على استحالة وجود الفعل بقدرة متقدمة عليه بأوقات كثيرة، فكذا يستحيل وجوده بقدرة متقدمة عليه بوقت واحد لاستوائهما في العدم وقت الفعل، ألا ترى أن البطش بيد كانت ثم انعدمت محال وإن اتصل وجود الفعل بزمان وجود اليد إذا كانت منعدمة وقت الفعل. كما أنه محال في زمان وجد بعد زمان انعدام اليد بمدة مديدة لاستوائهما في عدم اليد زمان البطش فكذا هذا، ويخرج هذا الكلام على طريق السؤال من وجهين: أحدهما من حيث القدرة ، وهو أن القدرة لما لم تكن إلا للفعل وقد تخلو عنه وقتا لم لا يجوز أن تخلو عنه أوقاتا؟ وهذا السؤال يرد أيضا على من يقول ببقاء القدرة من البصريين. والثاني: من حيث الفعل، وهو أنه لما جاز الفصل بالقدرة في الوقت الثاني وإن كانت القدرة فيه منعدمة لم لا يجوز الفعل بها في العاشر وإن كانت منعدمة وتقرير السؤال بالآلة كاليد والسكين وغيرهما، أنه لما استحال حصول الفعل بهما بعد العدم في الوقت العاشر من زمان العدم، فكذا استحال في الثاني منه لاستواء الوقتين في العدم.
For they said: If that power becomes non-existent, but another power occurs after it, since powers occur successively in the healthy sound [person]. It was said to them: That power existing in the second [moment] — according to you, is it the power for this action or the power for another action that follows it? And is it permissible for this action to exist in the second [moment] by this power existing in the second? If they say: It is the power for this action, and it is permissible for the action to exist by this power concurrent with it in existence. Then they have abandoned their doctrine, for they have made the action occur by the power concurrent with it in existence, and the preceding power remains superfluous with no benefit in its existence and no relation at all to any action, and this is impossible. So they have in reality helped the people of truth and established an addition with no benefit in it, and they gained nothing from opposition except foolishness. If they say: It is not permissible for the action to exist in the second [moment] by this power concurrent with it, but rather its existence is by the preceding power, and that power existing in the second is for an action that exists in the third, not for an action existing concurrent with it. It was said to them: If this power is established for another action, not for this action, and it is impossible for the concurrent action to be related to it, but rather its relation is to the first power and its occurrence is by it, not by this [power], and the existence of this power with respect to the action and its non-existence are equivalent — in that power over an action does not necessitate the removal of incapacity for another action — then all that we have charged you with is binding, and you have no escape in any way. And what indicates the correctness of what we hold is that the opponents have agreed with us on the impossibility of the existence of an action by a power preceding it by many times. So likewise, its existence by a power preceding it by one time is impossible, because they are equal in non-existence at the time of the action. Do you not see that striking with a hand that existed then became non-existent is impossible, even if the existence of the action is connected to the time of the existence of the hand if it is non-existent at the time of striking? Just as it is impossible in a time that exists after the time of the non-existence of the hand by a lengthy period, because of the equality of the two times in the non-existence of the hand at the time of striking. And this discourse proceeds according to two ways of questioning: One of them from the perspective of power (qudra) — namely, since power is only for the action, and it may be vacant from it for a time, why is it not permissible for it to be vacant from it for [many] times? And the second: from the perspective of action (fi‘l) — namely, since separation of power in the second moment is allowed, even though the power in it is non-existent, why is the action not permissible by it in the tenth [moment], even though it is non-existent? And the clarification of the question using the instrument (āla) like the hand and the knife and others: since the occurrence of the action by them after non-existence in the tenth moment from the time of non-existence is impossible, so likewise it is impossible in the second moment from it, because of the equality of the two moments in non-existence.

Commentary
This passage refutes a second objection and then presents two analytical ways of questioning. The opponents retreat to the claim that although the original power perishes, a new power arises immediately afterward, because powers occur successively in a healthy person. Nasafi responds by forcing them to specify the nature of this new power: is it for the same action or a different one? If they say it is for the same action and can produce it concurrently, then they abandon their own doctrine because they now admit that an action is produced by a concurrent power, rendering the earlier power useless — an absurdity. If they instead say the new power is for a later action and the current action is produced by the preceding power, Nasafi points out that the new power, being for a different action, cannot be related to the current action, and the earlier power is non-existent at the time of action, which the opponents themselves agreed is impossible when the time gap is large. Thus, by parity of reasoning, a one-moment gap is as impossible as a many-moment gap. The analogy of striking with an existing hand that then ceases reinforces this. Finally, Nasafi extracts two general lines of attack (sual): (1) from the nature of power — if power can be temporally vacant from its effect for one moment, why not for many? (2) from the nature of the action — if the action can be produced by a power that is absent at the moment of action (allowing a temporal gap), what is the difference between a one-moment gap and a ten-moment gap? This exposes the inconsistency in the opponents' position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two ways of questioning are independent and address separate issues.
Correction: Both questions derive from the same logical principle regarding temporal gaps between power and action; they are two sides of the same coin.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first way of questioning?
A) From the perspective of the agent.
B) From the perspective of power (qudra).
C) From the perspective of time.
D) From the perspective of the instrument.

Key Takeaway
The two ways of questioning expose the arbitrariness of allowing a one-moment gap but not a longer gap between power and action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you allowed a small inconsistency in your own reasoning or beliefs, thinking it harmless, while rejecting a larger one? What does this passage teach you about the danger of arbitrary distinctions? Will you commit to examining one such inconsistency today and follow reason fully?
?
The instrument analogy shows that impossibility does not depend on duration. In your spiritual life, do you treat small sins as acceptable while fearing major ones? How does this lesson challenge your view of sin's gravity based on duration or degree? What will you do to remove even 'small' compromises?
?
Nasafi's argument forces you to see that if you accept a gap of one moment, you logically must accept any gap. Where in your relationship with Allah do you act as if His power is 'vacant' for a moment, and how would your reliance on Him change if you saw His power as continuous and immediate? What concrete act of reliance will you perform today?

Body's Single State at Any Time
Nasafi argues for the body's single state at all times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a body cannot have two states simultaneously and is never without a state at any moment.

Core Idea
A body always has one state, never two, and is never stateless.

Nasafi establishes a foundational principle about physical bodies: at any given moment, a body possesses exactly one state—either motion or rest. This principle is crucial for understanding subsequent arguments about capability and actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall claim.
Notice the repetition of 'jā'iz' (permissible) and 'wājib' (obligatory).
2
Identify the three-pronged argument
The passage makes three points: (1) freedom from two states is permissible/obligatory; (2) at first existence, a state is necessary; (3) at no time does the body have two states or lack a state.
Each point builds on the previous.
3
Reflect on the logical necessity
Ask yourself: why can't a body have two states at once? Why must it always have one? The commentary explains the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخلو الجسم عن كونين جائز في كل وقت من أوقات البقاء بل واجب كما هو جائز بل واجب في أول أحوال وجوده. فإذا قط لا يوجد في زمان ما للجسم إلا كون واحد، ولا يخلو عنه في زمان ما لا زمان وجوده ولا زمان بقائه. فأما القدرة فإنها خالية عن الفعل حال وجودها وإن تحقق ذاتها، ويستحيل بعد ذلك خلوها عن الفعل وإن لم تتغير ذاتها، فالمعترض بفعل خلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون إن كان عالما بما بينا من حقيقة الحركة والسكون واستحالة خلو الجسم في أول أوقاته عن كون، واستحالة وجود كونين في حال ما وإن كانت حالة البقاء وثبوت المساواة بين الأحوال أجمع في وجوب وجود كون فيه واستحالة وجود كونين فيه مع علمه بذلك اعترض بهذا الاعتراض ؛ فهو مغالط، وإن لم يكن عالمًا بذلك كان السؤال صادرا عن الجهل بالحقائق.
And the body's being free of two states (kawnayn) is permissible at every time of the times of subsistence, nay obligatory, just as it is permissible, nay obligatory, at the first of its states of existence. For indeed, there never exists in any time for the body except one state, and it is never free of it in any time—neither the time of its existence nor the time of its subsistence. As for capability (al-qudra), it is free of the action at the moment of its existence even if its essence is realized, and it is impossible after that for it to be free of the action even if its essence has not changed. So the objector objecting concerning the body's being free of motion and rest—if he is knowledgeable of what we have explained of the reality of motion and rest and the impossibility of the body's being free of a state in its first times, and the impossibility of two states existing in any state even if it is a state of subsistence, and the establishment of equality among all states in the necessity of the existence of a state in it and the impossibility of two states existing in it—and with his knowledge of that, he objects with this objection, then he is a sophist; and if he is not knowledgeable of that, then the question arises from ignorance of realities.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents a proof about the states of the body and the nature of capability. The author first establishes that a body cannot simultaneously have two states (kawnayn) and is never without a single state at any moment, whether at the beginning of its existence or during its continuation. He then contrasts this with capability: capability can exist without its corresponding action, but once the action is present, the capability cannot be separate from it. The author then addresses an objection concerning the body's being free of motion or rest. He argues that if the objector understands the reality that a body always has a state and never has two states simultaneously, then his objection is sophistry; if he does not understand, it is ignorance. The argument relies on the principle that the body always has exactly one state (either motion or rest) at any time, so being free of both is impossible at the first moment, but capability is different—it can exist without the action.

Key Terms
كون
كونين
زمان
بقاء
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body can be momentarily without any state, such as being neither in motion nor at rest.
Correction: The passage asserts that a body always has exactly one state at any moment, so being state-free is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about a body at any given time?
A) It can have two states simultaneously.
B) It can be completely without any state.
C) It has exactly one state.
D) It has at least one state, possibly two.

Key Takeaway
A body always possesses exactly one state at any moment; it cannot have two states nor be without a state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
At every single moment of your life, you are in a specific state before Allah—obedience, disobedience, gratitude, or heedlessness. You cannot be in two opposite states at once. Right now, what is the true state of your heart? Will you let this moment pass without choosing to be in a state of repentance?
?
You were brought into existence in a state (fitrah). That initial state was one of pure submission. Have you preserved that state, or have you allowed it to become corrupted? Since you can never be stateless, what are you actively doing to ensure your current state aligns with what Allah originally created you to be?

Capability Can Exist Without Action
Nasafi argues that capability can exist without action and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why capability can exist without its corresponding action, and how this differs from the body's state.

Core Idea
Capability (qudra) can be present without the action, but once the action occurs, capability is inseparable.

Having established that a body always has a single state, Nasafi now turns to capability. He argues that capability can exist independently of its action, addressing a potential objection about the body being free of motion or rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the lines about capability and the objection. Read both Arabic and translation.
Notice the contrast with the body's state.
2
Understand the two claims about capability
The passage says (1) at the moment of its existence, capability is free of action; (2) after action exists, capability cannot be free of it.
The essence of capability does not change.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector claims the body can be free of motion/rest. Nasafi says if he knows the truth, he is a sophist; if not, ignorant.
The objection fails because it confuses capability with the body's state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخلو الجسم عن كونين جائز في كل وقت من أوقات البقاء بل واجب كما هو جائز بل واجب في أول أحوال وجوده. فإذا قط لا يوجد في زمان ما للجسم إلا كون واحد، ولا يخلو عنه في زمان ما لا زمان وجوده ولا زمان بقائه. فأما القدرة فإنها خالية عن الفعل حال وجودها وإن تحقق ذاتها، ويستحيل بعد ذلك خلوها عن الفعل وإن لم تتغير ذاتها، فالمعترض بفعل خلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون إن كان عالما بما بينا من حقيقة الحركة والسكون واستحالة خلو الجسم في أول أوقاته عن كون، واستحالة وجود كونين في حال ما وإن كانت حالة البقاء وثبوت المساواة بين الأحوال أجمع في وجوب وجود كون فيه واستحالة وجود كونين فيه مع علمه بذلك اعترض بهذا الاعتراض ؛ فهو مغالط، وإن لم يكن عالمًا بذلك كان السؤال صادرا عن الجهل بالحقائق.
And the body's being free of two states (kawnayn) is permissible at every time of the times of subsistence, nay obligatory, just as it is permissible, nay obligatory, at the first of its states of existence. For indeed, there never exists in any time for the body except one state, and it is never free of it in any time—neither the time of its existence nor the time of its subsistence. As for capability (al-qudra), it is free of the action at the moment of its existence even if its essence is realized, and it is impossible after that for it to be free of the action even if its essence has not changed. So the objector objecting concerning the body's being free of motion and rest—if he is knowledgeable of what we have explained of the reality of motion and rest and the impossibility of the body's being free of a state in its first times, and the impossibility of two states existing in any state even if it is a state of subsistence, and the establishment of equality among all states in the necessity of the existence of a state in it and the impossibility of two states existing in it—and with his knowledge of that, he objects with this objection, then he is a sophist; and if he is not knowledgeable of that, then the question arises from ignorance of realities.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents a proof about the states of the body and the nature of capability. The author first establishes that a body cannot simultaneously have two states (kawnayn) and is never without a single state at any moment, whether at the beginning of its existence or during its continuation. He then contrasts this with capability: capability can exist without its corresponding action, but once the action is present, the capability cannot be separate from it. The author then addresses an objection concerning the body's being free of motion or rest. He argues that if the objector understands the reality that a body always has a state and never has two states simultaneously, then his objection is sophistry; if he does not understand, it is ignorance. The argument relies on the principle that the body always has exactly one state (either motion or rest) at any time, so being free of both is impossible at the first moment, but capability is different—it can exist without the action.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Capability must always be accompanied by the action; otherwise it's not real capability.
Correction: The passage shows that capability exists before the action; it only becomes inseparable from the action after the action occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to capability after the action exists?
A) It becomes free of the action.
B) It remains separate from the action.
C) It becomes inseparable from the action.
D) It ceases to exist.

Key Takeaway
Capability (qudra) can exist without its action initially, but once the action appears, capability is inseparable from it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an ability you know you have—a talent, a skill, or a spiritual capacity—that you have not yet put into action. What is the real reason you haven't acted? Are you willing to take the first step today, knowing that once you act, that ability will become tied to that action?
?
Recall a time you did something you regret, and afterward felt that you could no longer claim you had the ability to have done otherwise. How does Nasafi's point that capability becomes inseparable from action after the act make you reconsider your choices? What action will you commit to today to avoid being trapped by a wrong action?
?
The body cannot be without motion or rest, but your capability can be without action. How does this freedom of capability before action challenge you to take responsibility for your potential? What one thing will you do in the next 24 hours to turn a capability into a righteous action?

Refuting the Objection on Power and Motion
Nasafi refutes an objection about the timing of capability for motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi resolves the objection about the timing of capability for motion by clarifying the definition of motion.

Core Idea
The timing of capability depends on where motion is defined to occur.

This passage addresses a classic objection against the Ash'ari view on capability. The objector asks a seemingly impossible question: when exactly does the power to move exist? Nasafi shows that the answer depends on whether motion is defined as a single state in the second place or as two states. The objection collapses once the definition is clarified.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the passage from the beginning to where the objector's dilemma is presented. Notice the two horns: capability in the second place (transition without power) or capability in the first place (power before motion).
The objection assumes a single capability for the whole motion.
2
Identify Nasafi's Key Move
Nasafi responds by saying the question depends on: 'in which place does motion occur?' He shows that the definition of motion determines where capability lies.
This is the crucial step: the objection is not independent of definitions.
3
Understand the Two Views
Two views are presented: (1) motion is a single state in the second place, so capability is there; (2) motion is two states in two places, so each has its own capability. This dissolves the original dilemma.
Each view is internally consistent; the objector's dilemma only works if you assume an unspecified definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن الإنسان متى يكون قادرًا على الحركة، يقدر عليها وهو في المكان الأول أم يقدر وهو في المكان الثاني؟ فإن قلتم : إنه يقدر وهو في المكان الثاني فقد أثبتم الانتقال من المكان الأول إلى المكان الثاني بلا قدرة، وهذا محال - وإن قلتم : إنه يقدر وهو في المكان الأول فقد أثبتم الاستطاعة قبل الحركة. فيقال لهم : هذا السؤال بناء على أن الحركة في أي مكان توجد؟ فمن قال: توجد في المكان الثاني فالقدرة عليها توجد في المكان الثاني - ومن قال إنها توجد فيهما القدرة توجد فيهما، وكشفه ما بينا أن من زعم أن الحركة اسم للكون فهي المكان الثاني ويستحق الاسم بشريطة تقدم كون آخر في مكان آخر قبله يقول : توجد قدرة الحركة في المكان الثاني. ومن قال: إن الحركة اسم لكونين في مكانين يقول : توجد قدرة الكون الأول في المكان الأول، وقدرة الكون الثاني في المكان الثاني إذ هما فعلان لابد لكل فعل من قدرة تقارنه، والله الموفق.
And with this they are answered concerning their statement: 'When is a human capable of motion: is he capable of it while in the first place or is he capable while in the second place? For if you say he is capable while in the second place, you have affirmed the transition from the first place to the second place without capability, and this is impossible; and if you say he is capable while in the first place, you have affirmed capacity before motion.' So it is said to them: This question is based on: in which place does motion occur? For whoever says it occurs in the second place, then the capability for it occurs in the second place; and whoever says it occurs in both places, the capability occurs in both places. And its explanation is what we have made clear: that whoever claims that motion is a name for a state (kawn) in the second place and deserves the name on condition of the precedence of another state in another place before it, says: the capability for motion occurs in the second place. And whoever says motion is a name for two states in two places says: the capability for the first state occurs in the first place, and the capability for the second state in the second place, since they are two actions, and for every action a capability must accompany it. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific objection: when does a person have the capability to move? If the capability is in the second location, then the transition from the first to the second would occur without capability, which is absurd. If it is in the first location, then capability exists before motion, also problematic. The author responds by noting that the objection depends on where motion is located. He explains two views. According to one, motion is a single state (kawn) in the second location, and the name 'motion' is given on condition that there was a prior state in the first location. On this view, the capability for motion exists only in the second location, accompanying the motion itself. According to the second view, motion is the name for two states in two locations—the first state and the second state—and each state requires its own accompanying capability. Thus, the capability for the first state is in the first place, and for the second in the second place. Therefore, the objection is dissolved because it assumed only one capability, but the answer depends on the definition of motion.

Misconception: One might think that the same capability must cover the entire motion from first to second place.
Correction: The passage shows that capability can be split according to the definition of motion. In the first view, capability is only in the second place; in the second view, each state has its own capability.

Checkpoint
According to the second view mentioned in the passage, where does the capability for the first part of motion occur?
A) In the second place.
B) In the first place.
C) In both places simultaneously.
D) It does not require a capability.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi resolves the objection by showing that the timing of capability is tied to the definition of motion, and the dilemma only arises when the definition is unspecified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a dilemma you currently face. Are you assuming a particular definition of the situation—like the objector assumed motion must be defined in a certain way—without stepping back to question whether the dilemma itself is built on an unexamined premise? Will you pause to clarify the terms before committing to a path?
?
The objector believed that if capability is not in the first place, then transition is impossible. Are there areas in your life where you have refrained from acting because you felt you lacked the ability at the start, not realizing that the ability might come with the action itself? What step can you take today, trusting that capability can accompany the act rather than precede it?
?
Nasafi's response shows that a wrong definition can trap you in a false contradiction. What assumptions in your life—about yourself, others, or even about Allah—might be based on definitions that are not truly from revelation or sound reason? Are you willing to examine those definitions honestly, even if it means admitting a long-held dilemma was actually a confusion?

Power Must Coexist with the Act
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim about power and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's rational refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that action cannot occur from an agent without power, and why power must coexist with the act.

Core Idea
Action without coexisting power is impossible; power must be present at the moment of the act.

This passage presents a crucial argument in the kalam debate on divine and human agency. Al-Nasafi takes on a Mu'tazilite objection and uses logical deduction to prove that power must exist simultaneously with the act. Understanding this refutation clarifies the Maturidi position on the relationship between power and action.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
The opponent argues that action cannot occur from an incapable person because power is absent, as power and incapacity are opposites.
The Mu'tazilites claim that power is absent at the time of the act.
2
Identify al-Nasafi's first move
Al-Nasafi points out that according to the Mu'tazila themselves, power is nonexistent at the moment of the act. He then supposes someone concedes that the agent is not incapable even without power.
He uses their own premise against them.
3
Follow the logical consequence
Since power is absent, the act should be impossible. Yet acts are observed, so power must exist at the time of the act, not before.
This shows that power must coexist with the act.
4
Grasp the reductio ad absurdum
Al-Nasafi argues: if power did not need to coexist, an act could occur at any later moment without power, which is absurd. Thus, power must coexist with the act.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا إنما يستحيل ثبوت الفعل من العاجز لانعدام القدرة إذ هو يضادها فينعدم عند ثبوت ضدها.
قلنا: والقدرة عندكم عند الفعل منعدمة (۱)، فهب أن إنسانا ساعدكم على قولكم الفاسد أن الفاعل في حال انعدام القدرة عنه ليس بعاجز، لثبوت الواسطة إلا أن القدرة منعدمة واستحال ثبوت الفعل من العاجز لعدم القدرة إلا لثبوت العجز، فينبغي أن لا يتصور منه الفعل حال، عدمها، وإذا ثبت الفعل دل أنه ثبت لثبوت القدرة وهي لا تبقى فكانت موجودة وقت) (۲) وجود الفعل لا سابقًا عليه، وإذا كان الأمر على ما بينا لم يكن لكم في نفي العجز عنه وقت وجود الفعل منفعة لما مر أن استحالة ثبوته من العاجز ليس لثبوت العجز بل لفقد القدرة، وقد تحقق الفقد
على أن الأمر لو كان على ما زعمتم لكان ينبغي أن يجوز ثبوت الفعل في العاشر من وقت (۳) عدم القدرة ويكون في تلك الحالة غير عاجز، وإن كان عديم القدرة، واستحالة ذلك تقرر ما قلنا والله الموفق.
And if they say: The occurrence of the act from the incapable is impossible only due to the nonexistence of power, since it (power) opposes it (incapacity) and ceases when its opposite (incapacity) is established. We say: And power, according to you, is nonexistent at the time of the act (۱). So suppose that a person agrees with your corrupt statement that the agent in the state of nonexistence of power is not incapable, due to the existence of an intermediate [state]—but power is nonexistent and the occurrence of the act from the incapable is impossible due to lack of power, not due to the existence of incapacity. Therefore, the act should not be conceivable from him in the state of its nonexistence. And if the act is established, it indicates that it is established due to the existence of power, and it (power) does not endure, so it existed at the time of the existence of the act (۲), not prior to it. And if the matter is as we have explained, then you gain no benefit from negating incapacity from him at the time of the act's existence, because it has been mentioned that the impossibility of its occurrence from the incapable is not due to the existence of incapacity but due to the lack of power, and the lack is verified. Moreover, if the matter were as you claim, then it should be permissible for the act to occur in the tenth [moment] from the time (۳) of nonexistence of power, and he would be in that state not incapable, even though he is devoid of power. The impossibility of that confirms what we said, and God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite objection. The opponent argues that action cannot occur from an incapable person because power is absent, as power and incapacity are opposites. Al-Nasafi first notes that according to the Mu'tazila themselves, power is nonexistent at the moment of the act. He then supposes someone concedes that the agent is not incapable even without power, but insists that since power is absent, the act should be impossible. Yet acts are observed, so power must exist at the time of the act, not before. He then uses a reductio: if power did not need to coexist with the act, then an act could occur at any later moment without power, which is absurd. Thus, power must coexist with the act, refuting the Mu'tazilite claim.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
فعل (act)
عاجز (incapable)
عدم (nonexistence)
ثبوت (establishment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Nasafi denies the need for power to act, or that power must precede the act.
Correction: Al-Nasafi actually affirms that power must exist simultaneously with the act, not before or after.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of al-Nasafi's refutation in this passage?
A) Power must precede the act.
B) Power must exist at the same time as the act.
C) Power is irrelevant for action.
D) The incapable can act without power.

Key Takeaway
Power must coexist with the act; the impossibility of action from the incapable is due to lack of power, not the presence of incapacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you been relying on a past ability or past repentance, forgetting that you need Allah's power at this very moment to do good? What act will you do today to remind yourself of that need?
?
Al-Nasafi argues that the impossibility of action from the incapable is due to lack of power, not due to presence of incapacity. Do you ever use past failures (incapacity) as an excuse not to act, rather than recognizing that you currently lack the power and need to seek it from Allah? What is one step you can take right now to seek that power?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought power could precede the act and then the act could happen without power. When have you assumed that because you succeeded in one deed, you have the power to succeed in the next without renewed dependence on Allah? How will you change that assumption starting now?

The Impossibility of the Capable Agent Being Devoid of Action
The author states the Mu'tazili dilemma and concludes the agent must have power and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author demonstrates that the Mu'tazili view on the temporality of power and action leads to a contradiction, showing that the agent cannot be described as having power and action simultaneously, and thus cannot be a creator.

Core Idea
A being without the ability to act cannot be a creator.

The author presents a logical dilemma for the Mu'tazila. He takes their own premises about the temporal sequence of power and action and shows that they lead to the absurd conclusion that the agent is neither able to act nor has power over the act. This passage is a classic example of internal critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazili position on power and action
The Mu'tazila hold that power (qudra) precedes the act in time. In the first moment, the agent has power but the act does not occur; in the second moment, the act occurs but the agent no longer has power. The author takes this and shows the consequence.
2
Follow the author's logical deduction
The author argues that if in the first moment no act occurs, and in the second moment the agent is not described as having power, then in both moments combined, the agent neither acts nor has power. Therefore, the agent cannot be considered a creator.
3
Examine the additional argument about the necessity of action for power
The author adds that if power could exist without action for one moment, it could exist for many, showing that action is not a necessary requirement of power. This undermines the Mu'tazili claim.
This second argument strengthens the refutation.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument defends the Ash'ari-Maturidi view that power and action are simultaneous and that the agent must have both. The author uses the Mu'tazili's own reasoning against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأوحشه، وهو ثم نقول : من مذهبكم أن الفعل لا يصح من القادر في الأول، وفي الثاني لا يوصف هو بالقدرة على الفعل، فكان مجموع الحالين أنه لا يصح منه الفعل ولا يقدر عليه، فكيف تجعلونه فاعلاً بل خالقًا لقوله؟ وما لا يصح وجوده ولا قدرة عليه لا يتصور وجوده. على أننا بينا من الكلام في جانب القدرة أن خلوها عن الفعل لو جاز وقتًا من مع وجود ذاتها لجاز أوقاتًا، إذ عرف بذلك أن ثبوت الفعل ليس من مقتضيات ذاتها لازم على هذا ويبطله ولا اعتراض لهم عليه.
And [؟], and then we say: According to your doctrine, the act is not valid from the capable agent in the first [moment], and in the second he is not described as having power over the act. So the sum of the two states is that the act is not valid from him nor does he have power over it. So how do you make him an agent, rather a creator of his speech? And that whose existence is not valid and over which there is no power, its existence is inconceivable. Moreover, we have shown from the discussion concerning power that if its being free of the act were permissible for one moment while its essence exists, it would be permissible for many moments, since it is known thereby that the establishment of the act is not a necessary requirement of its essence. This is necessary upon this [view] and invalidates it, and they have no objection against it.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a direct objection against the Mu'tazila. The author takes their own position and shows its absurdity. He begins by saying: 'According to your doctrine, the act is not valid from the capable agent in the first [moment], and in the second he is not described as having power over the act.' This refers to a Mu'tazili theory about the temporality of power and action. He then concludes that in both moments combined, the agent can neither act nor have power, so how can they call him a creator? Then he adds a further argument: if it were possible for power to be free of action for one moment, it would be possible for many, proving that action is not a necessary concomitant of power. This refutes their position.

Key Terms
القادر
القدرة
الفعل
الخالق
مذهبكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that their view on the temporality of power and action is consistent.
Correction: The author demonstrates that their view leads to the conclusion that the agent neither acts nor has power, which contradicts the idea that he is a creator.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the consequence of the Mu'tazili view for the capable agent in the first and second moments?
A) The agent can act in the first but not in the second.
B) The agent cannot act and has no power in either moment.
C) The agent has power but cannot act.
D) The agent can act but has no power.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili view that power and action are temporally separate leads to the impossibility of the agent being a true creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author shows that if power can be without action for one moment, it could be for all moments. Is there a belief you hold about God that, if carefully examined, would lead to a similar logical dead end? Are you willing to abandon that belief to preserve His perfection?
?
In your own life, do you ever claim to have the power to do something but never act on it? How does the logic of this argument expose the emptiness of such a claim, and what will you do today to align your power with action?
?
If you truly believe God is the Creator who constantly brings things into being, does your daily life reflect a sense of His perpetual creative act? Or do you live as if He created once and then stopped? How must you change to live in awareness of His continuous action?

Isfarayini's Argument on Impossibility of Taklif Ma La Yuta
Isfarayini argues that impossibility of taklif ma la yutaq stems from the logical condition of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Isfarayini's reasoning that the impossibility of imposing the impossible is based on the very meaning of obligation, not on moral considerations.

Core Idea
Obligation implies possible punishment, so incapacity makes it logically impossible.

Isfarayini presents a sophisticated argument that reframes the debate about taklif ma la yutaq. Rather than appealing to divine justice or human capacity, he focuses on the internal logic of obligation itself. In this passage, he explains why obligation requires the possibility of deserved punishment and why incapacity destroys that possibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting claim
Isfarayini begins by stating the position of 'ahl al-haqq' that imposing the impossible is impossible because the meaning of obligation is impossible with incapacity, not because of ugliness or foolishness.
2
Identify the core reasoning
Obligation necessarily entails deserving punishment for violation. Without capacity for violation, that deserving cannot exist, making genuine obligation impossible.
This is the key logical move.
3
Contrast two scenarios
Isfarayini distinguishes between inherently impossible commands (al-mu'juz anhu) and commands that become impossible due to engagement in the opposite. In the latter, deserving punishment is still conceivable (mawhum).
4
Note the exceptional cases
He adds that such commands may be permissible if they serve other purposes like subjugation (tashir), showing incapacity, or manifesting power.
This protects the Ash'ari position from criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني: قال أهل الحق يستحيل تكليف ما لا يطاق لاستحالة وجود المعنى الذي يقتضيه التكليف مع العجز لا للقبح والسفه، وهذا لأن الذي يقتضيه التكليف استحقاق نوع من العقوبة على ضرب من المخالفة، وهذا لأن التكليف إنما يتميز عما ليس بتكليف بهذا قال ولا يتوهم ذلك بغير استحقاق نوع من العقوبة على ضرب من المخالفة فعلى هذا ظهر الفرق له بين تكليف المعجوز عنه وتكليف ما ليس بمعجوز عنه إلا أنه عجز عنه لاشتغاله بضده فإن استحقاق نوع من العقوبة عليه على ضرب من المخالفة موهوم قال: ويجوز ورود ذلك على التسخير والإعلام بالعجز وإظهار القدرة. فعلى هذا يدفعون هذا التشنيع عن أنفسهم.
And Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini said: The people of truth said: It is impossible to impose what cannot be borne because the meaning that obligation necessitates is impossible with incapacity, not because of ugliness or foolishness. This is because what obligation necessitates is deserving (istihqaq) a type of punishment for a type of violation. And this is because obligation is only distinguished from what is not obligation by this. He said: And one does not imagine that without deserving a type of punishment for a type of violation. Thus the distinction appears for him between imposing what is incapable of being done (al-mu'juz anhu) and imposing what is not incapable of being done except that he is incapable of it due to his occupation with its opposite, for then the deserving of a type of punishment for a type of violation is imaginary (mawhum). He said: And that is permissible to occur for subjugation (tashir), informing of incapacity, and manifesting power. Thus they repel this criticism from themselves.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini's argument that imposing what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq) is impossible. Isfarayini begins by stating the position of the people of truth: it is impossible not because it is ugly or foolish, but because the essential meaning of obligation (taklif) — the deserving of punishment for violation — cannot exist when the person is incapable. He explains that obligation is distinguished from non-obligation by this element of deserving punishment. Then he distinguishes between two cases: commanding something inherently impossible (al-mu'juz anhu) and commanding something that becomes impossible due to engagement in its opposite. In the latter case, the deserving of punishment is still conceivable (mawhum). He notes that such commands could be permissible if they serve other purposes like subjugation (tashir), showing incapacity, or manifesting power. This argument refutes the accusation that the Ash'aris affirm the impossibility of taklif ma la yutaq.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
استحقاق العقوبة
المعجوز عنه
التسخير
موهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris deny taklif ma la yutaq based on divine justice (it would be unjust).
Correction: Isfarayini explicitly states it is not due to ugliness or foolishness, but due to the logical meaning of obligation.

Checkpoint
What is Isfarayini's basis for the impossibility of taklif ma la yutaq?
A) It would be unjust of God.
B) The commanded action is physically impossible.
C) The meaning of obligation (deserved punishment) cannot coexist with incapacity.
D) The Law prohibits commanding the impossible.

Key Takeaway
Isfarayini argues that the impossibility of taklif ma la yutaq is due to the logical impossibility of the meaning of obligation (deserved punishment) under incapacity, not due to moral ugliness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim you 'can't' do something Allah has commanded, are you genuinely incapable, or are you occupied with its opposite? What would change if you honestly answered that question right now?
?
Is there a sin you continue committing because you've convinced yourself you are powerless to stop? How does Isfarayini's distinction between inherent incapacity and distraction expose your excuse?
?
If you truly believed that every obligation carries the implication of deserved punishment, would you approach your daily prayers and duties differently starting today? What is one specific obligation you will treat as a matter of life and death from now on?

Comparison of Positions on Taklif Conditions (Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, Jabriyya)
Nasafi compares Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, and Jabriyya on conditions for taklif

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing views of the Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, and Jabriyya regarding the conditions for valid religious obligation (taklif).

Core Idea
The three schools diverge radically on whether capability is required for obligation.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by juxtaposing the positions of the major theological schools on the essential prerequisite for valid taklif. The Mu'tazila insist on actual capability at the moment of obligation, while the Ash'aris and Jabriyya reject both actual and instrumental capability. This passage lays out these contrasts before presenting the Maturidi middle path.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Notice how each school is introduced and defined by its condition.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify each school's position
List the three groups: Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, and Jabriyya. For each, note what condition they require for taklif: Mu'tazila require real capability (ḥaqīqat al-qudra); Ash'aris/Jabriyya require neither soundness of means nor real capability.
The text explicitly states 'لا يشترطون لا استطاعة الأسباب والآلات ولا حقيقة الاستطاعة'.
3
Analyze the conflation claims
The author says the Mu'tazila attached real capability to soundness of means by requiring both, and the Jabriyya attached soundness to real capability by requiring neither. Understand how each group aligns the two types of capability.
This shows the logical extremes.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the Mu'tazila think obligation without real capability is unjust, and why Ash'aris/Jabriyya think it is permissible. This sets the stage for the Maturidi middle position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الابد لصحة التكليف من سلامة الآلات خلافًا للمعتزلة والأشاعرة والمعتزلة يشترطون لصحة التكليف حقيقة القدرة، ويموهون أن التكليف بدونها تكليف ما ليس في الوسع ، ثم هم القائلون به في التحقيق دون خصومهم. والأشعرية والجبرية لا يشترطون لصحة التكليف لا استطاعة الأسباب والآلات ولا حقيقة الاستطاعة ، فألحقت المعتزلة حقيقة القدرة بقدرة سلامة الأسباب لاشتراط التكليف، وألحقت الجبرية استطاعة سلامة الأسباب بحقيقة الاستطاعة في ترك الاشتراط للتكليف، وأصحابنا اشترطوا لذلك سلامة الأسباب والآلات ولم يشترطوا حقيقة القدرة ، والصحيح ما ذهبنا إليه لما مر من الدلائل المعقولة.
The principle (al-abad?) for the validity of obligation is the soundness of instruments, contrary to the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris. The Mu'tazila stipulate for the validity of obligation the reality of capability and they falsely claim that obligation without it is an obligation of what is not within capacity, yet they themselves are the ones who say it in reality, not their opponents. The Ash'aris and Jabriyya stipulate for the validity of obligation neither the capacity of means and instruments nor the reality of capacity. So the Mu'tazila attached the reality of capability to the capability of soundness of means due to requiring (the condition) for obligation, and the Jabriyya attached the capacity of soundness of means to the reality of capability in leaving off the requirement for obligation. And our companions (the Maturidis) stipulated for that the soundness of means and instruments and did not stipulate the reality of capability. And the correct is what we have gone to due to the rational proofs that have passed.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the different theological schools regarding the conditions for the validity of religious obligation (taklif). Imam al-Nasafi first states the Maturidi position: the basis for valid obligation is the soundness of instruments (salāmat al-ālāt), meaning the person has the physical and mental capacity to perform the act if they choose. Then he contrasts this with the Mu'tazila, who require actual capability (ḥaqīqat al-qudra) at the moment of obligation, arguing that obligation without it is imposing what is beyond capacity. The Ash'aris and Jabriyya take the opposite extreme: they require neither soundness of means nor actual capability. The Mu'tazila conflate actual capability with capability of sound means by requiring both for obligation, while the Jabriyya conflate capability of sound means with actual capability by requiring neither. The Maturidi position is the moderate one: they require soundness of means but not actual capability, and they claim this is supported by rational proofs already mentioned.

Key Terms
سلامة الآلات
حقيقة القدرة
تكليف
معتزلة
أشاعرة
جبرية
استطاعة الأسباب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila require only soundness of means for taklif.
Correction: In fact, the Mu'tazila require real capability (ḥaqīqat al-qudra), which is more than just soundness of means.

Checkpoint
Which school requires real capability (ḥaqīqat al-qudra) for the validity of obligation?
A) Maturidis
B) Ash'aris
C) Mu'tazila
D) Jabriyya

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila require real capability, while Ash'aris and Jabriyya require none at all, setting up the Maturidi middle position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from an Islamic obligation by claiming it was simply beyond your capacity? After learning that the Maturidi position only requires soundness of instruments, does your excuse hold weight? Will you now re-evaluate your commitment to that obligation?
?
The Mu'tazila, for all their emphasis on justice, end up obligating what they claim is impossible. Is there an area of your religious life where you hold a double standard—demanding something of others that you subtly exempt yourself from? Will you confess this to Allah and ask for strength to close that gap?
?
If Allah's commands are never beyond the capacity He gives us (as per the Maturidi view), what does that mean for the prayers and duties you have been postponing? How will you change your schedule or mindset to act on this truth starting today?

The Maturidi Position on Conditions for Taklif
Nasafi presents the Maturidi position and asserts its correctness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that valid obligation requires soundness of instruments but not actual capability, and why this is the correct view.

Core Idea
The Maturidi position is the balanced middle path: soundness of means, not real capability.

Having presented the views of the other schools, Imam al-Nasafi now states the Maturidi doctrine. The school requires only that a person has the physical and mental capacity to perform the act if they choose, not that they already possess the actual capacity to act. This is supported by rational proofs already mentioned.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi position
Read the Arabic passage: 'وأصحابنا اشترطوا لذلك سلامة الأسباب والآلات ولم يشترطوا حقيقة القدرة'. Notice the two conditions: required (soundness of means) and not required (real capability).
2
Understand the distinction
Contrast this with the Mu'tazila (who require real capability) and Ash'aris/Jabriyya (who require neither). The Maturidis hold a middle ground.
This is a classic example of the Maturidi methodology of avoiding extremes.
3
Note the claim of correctness
The author asserts that the Maturidi position is correct ('والصحيح ما ذهبنا إليه') and refers to rational proofs already given. Reflect on why this might be the most coherent view.
The passage does not repeat the proofs, so trust that they have been established earlier.

The Proof from the General Verse and the Mu'tazila Objection
Nasafi presents the proof from Q. 13:16 and refutes the Mu'tazila objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi school uses the verse 'Creator of everything' to argue that God creates human actions, how the Mu'tazila object using the purpose of praise, and how Nasafi refutes them.

Core Idea
The general verse must be interpreted in light of God's praise.

This passage presents a key argument in the debate over divine causation. Nasafi begins by citing the Qur'anic proof that God creates all things, then presents the Mu'tazila objection, and finally offers a refutation. Understanding this exchange clarifies the Maturidi stance on human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof from the verse
Read the first part of the passage where Nasafi states the proof: the verse 'Creator of everything' includes human actions.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila argue that the verse is a statement of praise, so including evil actions would contradict praise.
They list examples of evil actions.
3
Follow Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi uses an analogy from the witnessed world to show that attributing such actions to God would be foolish.
This refutes the objection.
4
Reflect on the shāhid-ghā'ib principle
Notice how Nasafi applies the principle that the witnessed realm informs the unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحجة لأهل الحق قوله تعالى : ( خَلِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ ) وأفعال العباد أشياء فيكون الله تعالى خالقها لعموم الآية، والخصوم يعترضون على ذلك فيقولون: أفعال العباد مخصوصة من هذه الآية بدليل غرض الآية، فإنها خرجت مخرج التمدح وبدخول أفعال العباد تحتها يزول معنى التمدح بل يثبت معنى يوجب الذم، وهذا لأن من جملة أفعال العباد ما هو افتراء على الله تعالى ووصف له بما لا يليق بصفاته وشتم له وقتل أوليائه، وبسط اليد واللسان في رسله وأنبيائه، ومقابله سفرائه إلى خلقه وأمنائه على وحيه ومبلغي أمره ونهيه بكل ما قدروا عليه من المكروه ووسعه طوقهم من الجفوة، والمتعرض لشتم نفسه والافتراء عليه سفه في الشاهد الذي هو دليل يبتنى عليه أمور الغائب، فكيف الموجد لذلك والمخرج له من العدم إلى الوجود، فعلم بهذا أنه تعالى لم يرد بهذه الآية إن خرجت مخرج العموم إلا الخصوص.
And the proof for the people of truth is His statement, the Exalted: 'Creator of everything' (Qur'an 13:16). And the actions of servants are things, so God, the Exalted, is their Creator due to the generality of the verse. And the opponents object to that and say: The actions of servants are excluded from this verse by the evidence of the purpose of the verse, for it came in the form of self-praise (tamadduh), and if the actions of servants enter under it, the meaning of self-praise would be removed, rather a meaning necessitating blame would be established. This is because among the actions of servants are slander against God the Exalted, describing Him with what is not befitting His attributes, insulting Him, killing His saints, stretching out the hand and tongue against His messengers and prophets, opposing His emissaries to His creation, His trustees over His revelation, and those who convey His command and prohibition with all the harm they could and the harshness their capacity allowed. And one who exposes himself to insulting and slandering himself is foolish in the witnessed realm (shāhid), which is a proof upon which matters of the unseen (ghā'ib) are based — so how about the one who brings that [action] into existence and brings it out from non-existence to existence? So it is known by this that He, the Exalted, did not intend by this verse, if it came in the form of generality, except specificity.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth) for God being the creator of human actions, followed by the Mu'tazila objection and a refutation. The proof is based on the general verse 'Creator of everything' (Qur'an 13:16), arguing that since human actions are 'things', God must be their Creator. The Mu'tazila object that the verse is a statement of praise (tamadduh), and including evil human actions would contradict praise; they list examples such as slander, insulting God, killing prophets, etc. The refutation then points out that in the witnessed world, a person who slanders or insults himself is considered foolish, so how much more foolish would it be to attribute such acts to God as their Creator? Therefore, the verse must be interpreted as specific, not general, to avoid such implications. This is a key argument in the debate over divine causation of human actions.

Key Terms
خلق
عموم
خصوص
تمددح
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the verse 'Creator of everything' applies absolutely to all things including evil actions without any specification.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that the purpose of the verse (praise) requires specificity; Nasafi's refutation shows that the verse must be interpreted in context.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection of the Mu'tazila against the proof from the verse 'Creator of everything'?
A) The verse is abrogated.
B) The verse refers only to good actions because it is a verse of praise.
C) Human actions are not 'things'.
D) The verse applies only to God's essence.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Creator of everything' must be interpreted in light of its purpose, and the Maturidi response uses analogy from the witnessed world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself blaming God for your own wrong actions, saying 'God made me do it'? How does this lesson's distinction between God's creation and your responsibility challenge that excuse?
?
When you read the list of evil actions in the passage (slander, killing saints, harming messengers), does it make you uncomfortable knowing that God created them? How does the analogy from the witnessed world (a self-slanderer is foolish) reshape your understanding of God's involvement in your sins?
?
If God creates all actions, even your acts of disobedience, does that make you more grateful for His forgiveness or more accountable for your choices? What specific habit will you change today to align your actions with the fact that God is the Creator of everything, including your deeds?
