Why a Specified General Cannot Prove Creed
Nasafi refutes using a general verse in creed by arguing specification destroys certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that a general verse, once specified, cannot provide the certainty required for creedal proofs.

Core Idea
A specified general is ambiguous or yields only probability, not certainty.

In this passage, Nasafi engages with a Mu'tazilite argument based on a general verse. He systematically shows that once a general term has been specified—as it must be—it loses its ability to yield certainty. This is a key methodological point distinguishing uṣūl al-fiqh from creedal argumentation, where only decisive proofs suffice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage, then the literal translation. Focus on the logical flow: Nasafi first agrees on a point of common ground, then builds his refutation.
2
Identify the common ground
Nasafi notes that even the Mu'tazila agree God's essence and attributes are 'things' but are not included under the verse. See how he uses this as a starting point.
This is a rhetorical strategy: agreeing on one point before disagreeing.
3
Trace the principle of equality
Find where Nasafi argues that if something is excluded from a general for a certain reason, anything sharing that reason must also be excluded. This is a key logical move.
4
Understand the consequence: ambiguity or probability
Nasafi then states that a specified general becomes ambiguous (mujmal) for many scholars, and even if not, it only yields predominant opinion (ghālib al-raʾy), not certainty. This undermines its use in creed.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Finally, note his sharp conclusion: relying on such a proof in creedal matters is 'ignorance of the places of argumentation.' Reflect on the significance of this methodological boundary.
This lesson shows how careful uṣūl reasoning can dismantle an apparently strong proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يحققه أن ذات الله تعالى شيء بلا خلاف بيننا وبينكم، وكذا صفاته عند كثير منكم أشياء ولم تكن ذاته ولا صفاته داخلاً تحت هذه الآية لما في دخول ذلك فيها زوال ما سبقت له الآية من إثبات ما كان دخوله تحت الخطاب يوجب إبطال الفرض الذي سيق له الخطاب ويثبت ضد ذلك يمتنع دخوله تحته، وإن كان بحيث يتناوله اللفظ من حيث اللغة، وإن لم يخص شيء سواه مما يتناوله لفظ الخطاب فكيف وقد خص منه غيره مما هو مساو لذلك الغير في المعنى الموجب خروجه عن قضية الكلام، ولا شك أن إخراج [؟] بعض ما يتناوله اللفظ عن وضعه لمعنى يوجب إخراجه [؟] موجب إخراج [؟] يوجد فيه ذلك المعنى لاقتضاء القول التسوية في الحكم عند ثبوت المساواة في المعنى الموجب لذلك، على أن العام الذي خص منشئ يتوقف فيه عند كثير من أهل الأصول لصيرورته مجملاً، ولا يصح التعلق به لإثبات حكم جار مجرى الفروع يمكن إثباته بما لا وجه إلى إثباتها إلا الدليل القاطع الموجب للعلم الثابت بطريق التيقن، وعند أكثر أهل الأصول وإن لم ينقلب مجملاً إلا أنه يجب الحكم فيما وراء المخصوص من أفراد العموم وأحاده بطريق التيقن، بل يوجب ذلك بطريق غالب الرأي بلا خلاف بيننا وبينكم على أن هذا عندكم حكم العام وإن لم يخص منه شيء، والتعلق بمثل هذا الدليل في الأبواب الاعتقادية جهل بمواضع الحجاج ومواقع الأدلة ومراتبها في أنفسها ودرجات المحال التي يتعلق بها فيها، ويتبين بهذا أن لا متعلق بالآية في محل النزاع وموضع الخلاف.
What he establishes is that the essence of God, the Exalted, is a thing without disagreement between us and you, and likewise His attributes according to many of you are things. But His essence and His attributes were not included under this verse because their inclusion in it would entail the removal of what the verse was brought for — from establishing what its inclusion under the discourse would necessitate the invalidation of the purpose for which the discourse was brought, and it would establish the opposite of that. So their inclusion under it is impossible, even if the wording encompasses them from a linguistic perspective. And if nothing other than them is excluded from what the wording of the discourse encompasses, how then when something else has been excluded from it that is equal to that other in the meaning necessitating its exclusion from the purport of the speech? And there is no doubt that the exclusion of [؟] some of what the wording encompasses from its [usual] application for a meaning that necessitates its exclusion necessitates the exclusion of [؟] what contains that meaning, because the principle requires equality in judgment when equality in the meaning necessitating that is established. Moreover, the general term that has been specified — many of the scholars of uṣūl hesitate regarding it because it becomes ambiguous (mujmal), and it is not valid to rely on it to establish a ruling that runs in the course of subsidiary matters, which can only be established by a decisive proof (dalīl qāṭiʿ) necessitating knowledge established by way of certainty. According to most scholars of uṣūl, even if it does not become ambiguous, it is necessary to judge regarding what is beyond the specified individuals of the general and its singulars by way of certainty; rather, it necessitates that by way of predominant opinion (ghālib al-raʾy) without disagreement between us and you, and this is according to you the ruling of the general even if nothing has been excluded from it. And relying on such a proof in the matters of creed is ignorance of the places of argumentation, the positions of proofs, their ranks in themselves, and the degrees of the subjects with which they are engaged. And by this it becomes clear that there is no reliance on the verse in the place of contention and the point of dispute.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila argument from the general verse. Nasafi begins by noting that even the Mu'tazila agree God's essence and attributes are 'things' but are not included under the verse, because including them would defeat its purpose (to establish creation of contingent things). Similarly, human actions, though linguistically included, are excluded because they would negate the purpose of praise. He then appeals to the principle of equality: if a general is specified for a certain reason, then anything sharing that reason must also be excluded. Furthermore, once a general is specified, many uṣūl scholars consider it ambiguous (mujmal) and insufficient for certainty, especially in creedal matters requiring decisive proofs. Even if it is not ambiguous, it only yields predominant opinion, not certainty. Therefore, relying on such a verse in creed is ignorance of proper argumentation, and the verse cannot be used as proof that God creates all human actions.

Key Terms
عام (ʿāmm)
خاص (khāṣṣ)
مُجمل (mujmal)
دليل قاطع (dalīl qāṭiʿ)
تيقن (yaqīn)
غالب الرأي (ghālib al-raʾy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A general verse like 'Creator of everything' can be used as definitive proof that God creates all human actions.
Correction: Nasafi shows that such verses have been specified (e.g., human actions excluded), and a specified general cannot provide certainty in creed.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is the verse 'Creator of everything' not a valid proof that God creates all human actions?
A) The verse is abrogated.
B) The verse has been specified, and a specified general cannot provide certainty in creedal matters.
C) The verse is ambiguous due to an unknown meaning.
D) The verse only applies to good actions.

Key Takeaway
A specified general term does not provide the certainty required for creedal proofs; relying on it reveals ignorance of proper argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses like 'Allah is Creator of everything,' do you automatically assume it includes absolutely everything without considering the verse's context and purpose? How might this assumption affect your understanding of Allah's attributes? Today, commit to studying the occasion of revelation (sabab al-nuzūl) of a general verse before drawing a creedal conclusion.
?
Are there beliefs in your faith that you hold based on popular arguments or widespread interpretations rather than on decisive proofs? Nasafi warns that relying on weak proofs in creed is 'ignorance of methodology.' Can you identify one such belief and honestly evaluate whether your certainty is based on yaqīn or mere predominant opinion? Write down one step you will take to strengthen that belief with clear evidence.
?
Think of a time you used a general verse to support a point in a discussion without careful consideration. Did you assume its generality was absolute? How might Nasafi's principle of equality and specification challenge that approach? Before your next discussion, resolve to first check whether the verse has been specified and whether it can deliver certainty in that context.

Three Rational Proofs on Human Actions
Lists three rational proofs of Ahl al-Sunna on human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs of Ahl al-Sunna regarding human actions: the impossibility of creation power for other than Allah, the servant's real action without creative power, and the possibility of one object under two powers.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunna defends human action without granting creative power to humans.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi presents the three rational arguments that form the backbone of the Ahl al-Sunna position on the issue of human actions (qadar). These proofs are foundational for understanding the doctrine of acquisition (kasb) and distinguishing it from both compulsion (jabr) and Mu'tazilite rationalism. Let's examine each argument in turn.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening and first proof
Read the Arabic and translation for the first point: establishing the impossibility of anyone other than Allah having the power of creation.
This proof refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions.
2
Read the second proof
Read the second point: the servant does have an action, but not from creative power; he is not compelled.
This affirms human responsibility without divine compulsion.
3
Read the third proof
Read the third point: it is possible for a single object of power to be under two powers simultaneously.
This is the key argument for the doctrine of acquisition (kasb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعقول لنا في المسألة فنقول – وبالله التوفيق: إن الكلام في المسألة يقع في مواضع أحدها في إثبات الاستحالة لثبوت قدرة التخليق لغير الله تعالى. والثاني: في أن العبد له فعل وإن لم يكن له قدرة التخليق، وليس بمضطر فيه. والثالث: في أن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين من جملة المجوزات دون الممتنعات.
And as for the rational proof for us on this issue, we say – and with God is success: The discussion on the issue falls into points: one of them is establishing the impossibility of the existence of the power of creation for other than Allah the Exalted. The second: that the servant has action even though he does not have the power of creation, and he is not compelled in it. The third: that the entry of a single object of power under the power of two powerful beings is among the possible things, not the impossible.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of three rational proofs (adilla ‘aqliyya) of Ahl al-Sunna regarding human actions. The first proof establishes that it is impossible for anyone other than Allah to have the power of creation (qudrat al-takhlīq). The second asserts that the servant does have an action (fi‘l) even though he does not possess creative power, and he is not compelled (muḍṭarr) in that action – meaning he has free choice. The third argument states that it is possible for a single object of power (maqdūr wāḥid) to fall under the power of two powerful beings (qādirayn), contrary to the Mu'tazilite claim that it is impossible. This is a defense of the Ash‘ari/Maturidi position on human acquisition (kasb).

Key Terms
استحالة
قدرة التخليق
فعل
مقدور واحد
قادرين
مجوزات
ممتنعات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: For a human action to be real and accountable, the human must be the creator of that action.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirms that the servant has a real action (fi‘l) without possessing the power of creation. The action is attributed to the servant in terms of acquisition (kasb), while creation (takhlīq) belongs exclusively to Allah.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is one of the three rational proofs mentioned by the author?
A) The impossibility of any act from the servant.
B) The servant is compelled in his actions.
C) It is possible for one object to be under two powers.
D) God's power and man's power cannot coexist.

Key Takeaway
The three rational proofs establish that human actions are real but not independently created, with the possibility of one act being subject to both divine and human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only Allah has the power to create, and your every action is ultimately from Him, what does that mean for the pride you feel in your achievements? Will you now thank Him for every thought you think and every move you make?
?
When you sin, do you truly feel responsible, or do you blame Allah's will? The lesson says you have real action and are not compelled. How will this change the way you repent and resolve to change?
?
If one act can be simultaneously under your power and Allah's power, how does that reshape your view of your daily choices? Will you start each day by acknowledging that your actions are both yours and His, and ask for His help in every single one?

Refuting the Objection: Conceivable Means Rationally Proven
The author refutes the objection by redefining 'conceivable' and using analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'conceivable' (ma‘qūl) in Islamic theology is defined by rational proof, not by imagination, and how this refutes the objection against simultaneous divine creation and human action.

Core Idea
Rational proof, not imagination, determines what is conceivable.

The author addresses a common objection: how can an act be both created by Allah and performed by the human? Critics claim it is 'inconceivable.' In this passage, the author defines 'conceivable' and shows that the objection relies on a misunderstanding of rationality versus imagination. He uses the examples of spirit and intellect to demonstrate that many realities are known through reason despite being unimaginable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal English translation to grasp the full argument.
Notice the author's direct response style: 'We say: Why do you say it is inconceivable?'
2
Identify the author's definition of ma'qul
Locate where the author defines 'conceivable' (al-ma'qul). He states it is that for which rational proof has been established.
This is the key move: shifting the criterion from imagination to reason.
3
Examine the analogy of spirit and intellect
The author argues that even the existence of spirit and intellect in the body is not imaginable but is rationally known. This shows that unimaginability does not imply impossibility.
Think about how this analogy supports the case for human action being conceivable.
4
Consider the reductio ad absurdum
The author warns that rejecting something due to lack of imagination would lead to denying the existence of the Creator, since He is unimaginable. This forces the opponent to accept the proof.
This is a powerful logical consequence that ties the argument together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا هذا غير معقول أن يكون الله تعالى مخرجًا للفعل من العدم إلى الوجود والعبد يكون فاعلاً.
قلنا: لم قلتم أنه غير معقول، والمعقول ما قام عليه الدليل العقلي وقد قام فيما نحن فيه، فإن الدليل قد قام على استحالة ثبوت قدرة التخليق لغير الله سبحانه وتعالى، وقد قام على أن العبد له فعل، إلا أنه لا يتصور في أوهامكم ذلك، لأنكم لم تروا في الشاهد من له قدرة الفعل في محل قدرة غيره ومن له قدرة الاختراع ليفعل فعله الاختياري ويوجده في محل قدرته ، فلم يتصور ذلك في أوهامكم عند قصور حواسكم عند الوقوف عليه، والوهم من نتائج الحس على ما سبق ذكره، وخروج الشيء عن الوهم مما لا يوجب استحالة ثبوته عند قيام الدليل على ثبوته، إن العلم محيط بثبوت الروح في البدن ووجود العقل فيه، وإن كان لا يتقرر في الوهم معنى تحيا به هذه الأعضاء ولا معنى يوقف به على ما غاب عن الحواس من حقائق الأشياء لخروجه عن الحواس، وقد مر أن من رام أن يعرف بالوهم ما سبيل معرفته العقل فقد حاد عن الطريق، ومن يتبع الوهم ولم يفتقد الدليل فيما يجره إلى معرفته لخروجه عن الوهم، فأول ما يلزمه إنكاره ثبوت الصانع إذ لا تصور في الوهم لما ليس بجوهر ولا جسم ولا عرض ولا قائم بنا ولا بجهة من الجهات منا ولا اتصال له بنا ولا انفصال له عنا، ويلزمه أن يخرج ثبوت الصانع عن العقل لخروجه عن الوهم، ويقول إنه ليس ثبوته بمعقول لما أنه ليس بموهوم، فمن أقر بثبوت الصانع اتباعًا للدليل وإن لم يتقرر ذلك في الوهم يلزمه الإقرار بما بينا من الدليل وإن لم يتصور ذلك في الوهم.
For if they say: This is inconceivable (ghayr ma‘qūl) – that Allah the Exalted brings the act from non-existence into existence while the slave is an agent (fā‘il). We say: Why do you say it is inconceivable? The conceivable (al-ma‘qūl) is that for which rational proof (al-dalīl al-‘aqlī) has been established, and proof has been established in this case. For proof has been established for the impossibility (istiḥāla) of the power of creation (qudrat al-takhlīq) belonging to other than Allah, and it has been established that the slave has an act. However, this is not represented (lā yataṣawwar) in your imaginations (awhām) because you have not seen in the witnessed world (al-shāhid) anyone who has the power of acting in the locus of another's power, and anyone with the power of invention to perform his voluntary act and bring it into existence in the locus of his power. So it was not represented in your imaginations due to the deficiency of your senses when apprehending it. And imagination (al-wahm) is a result of sensation (al-ḥiss) as previously mentioned. And something's being outside the imagination does not necessitate the impossibility of its existence when proof has been established for its existence. Indeed, knowledge (al-‘ilm) encompasses the existence of the spirit (al-rūḥ) in the body and the existence of the intellect (al-‘aql) in it, even though the meaning by which these limbs live is not fixed in the imagination, nor is there a meaning by which what is hidden from the senses of the realities of things can be apprehended, because it is beyond the senses. And it has passed that whoever seeks to know through imagination that whose means of knowledge is the intellect has deviated from the path. And whoever follows imagination and does not miss the proof (al-dalīl) regarding what leads him to its knowledge because of its being outside the imagination – the first thing necessitated for him is the denial of the existence of the Maker (al-Ṣāni‘), since there is no representation in the imagination of that which is neither substance (jawhar), nor body (jism), nor accident (‘araḍ), nor subsisting in us, nor in any direction from us, nor connected to us, nor separate from us. And it would necessitate that he remove the existence of the Maker from the intellect because of its being outside the imagination, and say that His existence is not conceivable because it is not imagined. So whoever affirms the existence of the Maker following the proof, even if that is not fixed in the imagination, must affirm what we have clarified of the proof, even if that is not represented in the imagination.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that it is inconceivable (ghayr ma‘qūl) for Allah to bring the act into existence while the slave is the agent. The author defines 'conceivable' as that which is supported by rational proof (dalīl ‘aqlī). He asserts that proof has been established for both the impossibility of creation power (takhlīq) for other than Allah and for the slave having an act. The opponent's inability to imagine this is due to sensory limitation (qusūr ḥawāss), as imagination (wahm) is derived from sensation. The author argues that something being outside imagination does not negate its existence when proof exists. He draws an analogy: the existence of the spirit (rūḥ) and intellect (‘aql) within the body is known through reason, though not directly imagined. Similarly, God's existence is beyond imagination, yet affirmed by proof. Therefore, rejecting human action because it is not imagined would lead to rejecting God's existence itself.

وهم
دليل عقلي
قدرة التخليق

عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Something is only conceivable if it can be imagined or sensed.
Correction: The author teaches that 'conceivable' means supported by rational proof, even if beyond imagination.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what determines whether something is 'conceivable' (ma‘qūl)?
A) If it can be imagined (mutawahham)
B) If it is supported by rational proof
C) If it is visible in the witnessed world
D) If it is a necessary action

Key Takeaway
Conceivability in theology is a matter of rational proof, not imaginative representation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a teaching of Islam that does not 'feel' right in your imagination or emotions, do you submit to rational proof from revelation, or do you rely on your own mental images? Which faculty do you trust more when your heart is uncomfortable?
?
This lesson demonstrates that even the existence of Allah is not imaginable. Have you ever tried to form a mental picture of Allah, and what did this lesson reveal about that habit? Will you commit to affirming Allah based on proof alone, even when your imagination is silent?
?
Think of a specific area in your life where you reject or resist a truth from Allah because you cannot 'see' how it works (e.g., predestination, divine wisdom in suffering). How does this lesson call you to surrender your imagination to rational proof? What one step will you take today to align your heart with what is rationally proven, even if unimaginable?

Ahl al-Hadith's Definition of Qabih and Its Application
Two definitions of evil with application to God and humans

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Hadith definition of evil (al-qabih) and how it distinguishes between God's creation and human acquisition.

Core Idea
Evil is defined by prohibition and harm, but God is neither forbidden nor harmed.

After refuting the Mu'tazila and Dualists, the Ahl al-Hadith theologians offer a definition of evil that centers on divine command and harm. This passage presents two versions of that definition and applies them to the case of unbelief: God's creation is not evil, but the slave's acquisition is evil. The lesson will unpack these definitions and their implications for praise and blame.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the flow from the first definition to the second. Note that the passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the completed definitions.
The passage is taken from a larger argument; the core definitions are complete.
2
Identify the two definitions of evil
Find the two definitions: 'evil is what is forbidden' and 'evil is what brings pure harm to the doer.' Notice that the second adds a harm-based element.
These are presented sequentially by 'some of them say.'
3
Apply each definition to God and the slave
For each definition, see how it applies to God's creation of unbelief and to the slave's acquisition. Result: God is not subject to prohibition or harm, so His act is not evil; the slave is forbidden and harmed, so his act is evil.
This distinction preserves God's transcendence while affirming human accountability.
4
Reflect on the implications for praise and blame
The passage mentions that blame and praise follow from these actions. Consider how these definitions lead to different assignments of praise/blame. This connects to broader debates about divine justice.
The passage ends before completing the praise discussion, but the link is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا بطل ذلك فبعد ذلك جمهور متكلمي أهل الحديث يقولون : القبيح ما نهى عنه، والله تعالى ليس بمنهي عن إيجاد الكفر، والعبد منهي عن اكتساب الكفر فكان خلقه تعالى غير قبيح، وكسب العبد قبيحا على هذا يتكلم جمهور متكلمي أهل الحديث، وبعضهم يقولون: القبيح ما يعود به على فاعله الضرر المحض، والله تعالى لا يعود عليه بفعل ما ضرر، فلا يكون خلقه الكفر قبيحًا منه لعدم عود ضرر به عليه، والكافر يعود باكتسابه الكفر عليه ضرر محض فكان اكتسابه الكفر قبيحا، ويجعل الذم والحمد من توابع هذين الفعلين فنقول: إن الفعل الذي يستحق عليه الحمد ما لا يعود به ضرر على فاعله، والذي
And if that is nullified, then after that the majority of the mutakallimun of the Ahl al-Hadith say: Evil (al-qabih) is what is forbidden, and God is not forbidden from creating unbelief, while the slave is forbidden from acquiring unbelief, so His creation of it is not evil, and the slave's acquisition (al-kasb) is evil. This is how the majority of the mutakallimun of the Ahl al-Hadith speak. And some of them say: Evil is what brings pure harm to its doer, and God is not subject to harm from any action, so His creation of unbelief is not evil from Him due to the lack of return of harm upon Him, while the disbeliever, by his acquisition of unbelief, brings pure harm upon himself, so his acquisition of unbelief is evil. And they make blame and praise consequences of these two actions. Then we say: The action that deserves praise is that which does not bring harm upon its doer, and which...
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the majority of Ahl al-Hadith mutakallimun (theologians) on the definition of evil (al-qabih) after refuting the Mu'tazila and Dualists. Two definitions are given. First: evil is what is forbidden (by God). Since God is not subject to any command or prohibition, His creating unbelief is not evil; only the slave's acquisition (kasb) of unbelief is evil, because the slave is forbidden from doing it. Second: evil is what brings pure harm to the doer. God is not harmed by any action, so His creation of unbelief is not evil; the disbeliever, however, brings harm upon himself by acquiring unbelief, so that acquisition is evil. The author notes that blame and praise follow from these two definitions. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the praise/blame distinction continues, but the core definitions are given.

Key Terms
القبيح
النهي
الكسب
الضرر
الحمد
الذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Hadith only define evil as what God forbids, ignoring harm.
Correction: They also define evil as what brings pure harm to the doer, applying harm subjectively to the doer's state, not to the act in itself.

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Hadith mutakallimun, why is God's creation of unbelief not evil?
A) Because God creates everything good.
B) Because God is not forbidden from creating it, and it does not harm Him.
C) Because unbelief is not really evil.
D) Because the action is attributed to the slave, not to God.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Hadith define evil by prohibition and harm, allowing them to affirm God's creation of unbelief as not evil while condemning the human acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your own sins, do you see them primarily as breaking God’s commands or as harming yourself? Which view makes you more likely to stop? Be honest: which have you actually felt in your heart?
?
If God creates your trials, but they are not evil because He is not harmed, how does that change the way you complain about your difficulties? Will you still grumble, or can you now accept that what seems evil to you may not be evil from God?
?
You have read that the slave is forbidden from acquiring unbelief. Have you ever excused your own small sins by saying 'God willed it' or 'it was my nature'? How does this lesson’s distinction between God’s creation and your acquisition demand that you take full responsibility today?

Definitions of Evil by Ahl al-Hadith
Imam al-Nasafi presents two definitions of qabih from Ahl al-Hadith and their implication for the shahid/ghaib analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of evil (qabih) given by the majority of Ahl al-Hadith mutakallimun and how they invalidate the analogy of the witnessed with the unseen.

Core Idea
Evil is defined by divine prohibition or by harm to the agent; both prevent applying the concept to God.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by presenting the views of the mainstream Ash'ari mutakallimun (Ahl al-Hadith) on defining evil. They propose two criteria: either what God forbids, or what brings pure harm to the agent. Both definitions are carefully constructed to exclude any possibility of evil in God's actions, while affirming it in human acts. This sets the stage for refuting the Mu'tazila and dualists who argue from human experience to God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first definition
Read the first sentence where it says 'القبيح ما نهى عنه' and note how this definition immediately distinguishes between God's act and the servant's act.
God is not forbidden from creating kufr; only the servant is forbidden from acquiring it.
2
Identify the second definition
Find the second definition: 'القبيح ما يعود به على فاعله الضرر المحض'. Notice the emphasis on pure harm returning to the doer.
Since God cannot be harmed, His act is not evil.
3
Understand the conclusion about analogy
The author then says this definition prevents applying the concept of evil to God because harm is impossible for Him, but conceivable for us. So the shahid/ghaib analogy fails.
This directly invalidates Mu'tazila and dualist arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا بطل ذلك فبعد ذلك جمهور متكلمي أهل الحديث يقولون : القبيح ما نهى عنه، والله تعالى ليس بمنهي عن إيجاد الكفر، والعبد منهي عن اكتساب الكفر فكان خلقه تعالى غير قبيح، وكسب العبد قبيحا على هذا يتكلم جمهور متكلمي أهل الحديث، وبعضهم يقولون: القبيح ما يعود به على فاعله الضرر المحض، والله تعالى لا يعود عليه بفعل ما ضرر، فلا يكون خلقه الكفر قبيحا منه لعدم عود ضرر به عليه، والكافر يعود باكتسابه الكفر عليه ضرر محض فكان اكتسابه الكفر قبيحا، ويجعل الذم والحمد من توابع هذين الفعلين فنقول: إن الفعل الذي يستحق عليه الحمد ما لا يعود به ضرر على فاعله، والذي يستحق عليه الذم ما يعود به ضرر على فاعله، وإليه ذهب أبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني فيمتنع على هذا أيضا قياس الشاهد بالغائب لما أن عود الضرر في الشاهد متصور فيتصور القبيح وفي الغائب ممتنع فيمتنع القبيح في الغائب فيبطل به جميع كلام المعتزلة والثنوية. فأما مشايخنا رحمهم الله فإنهم قالوا كل ما له عاقبة حميدة فهو وجه حكمة، وما ليست له عاقبة حميدة فهو سفه وقبيح، وما تعلقت به عاقبة وخيمة فهو سفه لوجهين أحدهما خلو نفس الفعل عن العاقبة الحميدة، والآخر : خلو تحمل ذلك المعنى الوخيم عن العاقبة والحكمة ما تعلقت به عاقبة حميدة، والكلام في بيان صحة هذا الحديث مذكور في تصنيف لي في هذه المسألة على حدة لا إلى ذكره هاهنا لما فيه من الطول وبنا غنية عن ذلك إنما حاجتنا إلى إبطال شبه الخصوم وتسويتهم بين الشاهد والغائب، وقد بينا ذلك وقد سبق القول في إبطال مذاهب الثنوية، أن الله خلق ما لا يحصى كثرة مما لا انتفاع لأحد من خلقه به ولا اطلاع لممتحن عليه من الأجزاء الكامنة في تخوم الأرضين وبواطن الجبال وقعور البحار وهو تعالى يجل عن الانتفاع بشيء، ومع ذلك لم يكن خلق ذلك عبثا، على أن الاستدلال بالشاهد محال أيضا لما أن كل فاعل في الشاهد محل الحاجات والضرورات، فإذا اشتغل بما لا نفع له به وهو محتاج إلى اجتلاب المنافع ودفع المضار فكان اشتغاله به إعراضا عما فيه جلبة منفعة له ودفع مضرة، فكان ذلك منه رضا بالنقيصة وتحمل المضرة فكان سفها، والله تعالى يجل عن ذلك فلم يكن
When that is invalidated, then after that the majority of the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith say: The evil (qabih) is what He has forbidden; and God is not forbidden from bringing about disbelief; and the servant is forbidden from acquiring disbelief; thus His creation (of disbelief) is not evil, and the servant's acquisition is evil. Upon this speak the majority of the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith. And some of them say: The evil is that which returns pure harm to its doer; and God does not suffer harm from an act, so His creation of disbelief is not evil from Him due to the absence of harm returning to Him; and the disbeliever, by his acquisition of disbelief, suffers pure harm, so his acquisition of disbelief is evil. And they make blame and praise dependent on these two types of acts. So we say: The act for which praise is deserved is that which does not return harm to its doer; and the act for which blame is deserved is that which returns harm to its doer. This is the view of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. Upon this, the analogy of the witnessed with the unseen is also impossible, because the return of harm in the witnessed is conceivable, so the evil is conceivable; and in the unseen it is impossible, so the evil in the unseen is impossible. Thus all the speech of the Mu'tazila and the dualists is invalidated. As for our masters (mashayikh) - may God have mercy on them - they said: Everything that has a praiseworthy consequence (aqiba hamida) is a aspect of wisdom (hikma); and whatever does not have a praiseworthy consequence is foolishness (safah) and evil (qabih). And whatever is attached to a harmful consequence is foolishness for two reasons: one is the absence of the praiseworthy consequence from the act itself; and the other is the absence of the burden of that harmful meaning from the consequence. And wisdom is that which is attached to a praiseworthy consequence. The discussion of the correctness of this hadith is mentioned in my own work on this question separately, and I do not need to mention it here due to its length; we are not in need of that. Our need is only to invalidate the doubts of the opponents and their equation between the witnessed and the unseen, and we have already shown that. And the statement has preceded about invalidating the schools of the dualists: that God created countless things in which no one among His creation has any benefit, nor any observation by a tested one, from the parts hidden in the depths of the earths, the interiors of mountains, and the bottoms of seas; and He is exalted above benefiting from anything; nevertheless, the creation of that was not futile (abath). Moreover, relying on the witnessed is also impossible because every agent in the witnessed is subject to needs and necessities; so if he engages in something that has no benefit for him while he is in need of acquiring benefits and repelling harms, then his engagement in it is a turning away from that which brings him benefit and repels harm, and that is from him a contentment with deficiency and a burden of harm, so it is foolishness. And God is exalted above that, so it is not.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part presentation of definitions of evil (qabih) and a final refutation of the dualists. First, the author states the view of the majority of Ahl al-Hadith mutakallimun: what God forbids is qabih. Since God is not forbidden from creating kufr, His act is not qabih, but the servant's acquisition is. A second group defines qabih as what returns pure harm to its doer; God suffers no harm, so His act is not qabih, but the servant suffers harm, so his act is. This is the view of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, and on this basis the analogy of the shahid and gha'ib fails because harm is conceivable in the shahid but impossible for God. Then the Maturidi shuyukh define qabih by consequence: acts with a praiseworthy consequence are wise; otherwise they are foolish. The author says he has already discussed the proof elsewhere. He then reiterates that the goal is to invalidate the opponents' analogies, and adds that God created countless things with no apparent benefit, yet this is not futile—showing that the shahid analogy is invalid because a human agent in need would be foolish to do useless acts, but God is not.

Key Terms
القبيح
النهي
الضرر
الشاهد
الغائب
المعتزلة
الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Ahl al-Hadith definition of evil is the same as the Mu'tazilite definition.
Correction: The Ahl al-Hadith define evil either by divine command or by harm to the agent, not by any intrinsic quality. This prevents attributing evil to God.

Checkpoint
According to the second definition of evil (pure harm to the doer), why is God's creation of disbelief not evil?
A) Because God does not forbid it.
B) Because God cannot suffer harm.
C) Because disbelief is beneficial to God.
D) Because God is not a doer.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Hadith define evil in ways that make it inapplicable to God, thereby blocking the Mu'tazilite analogy from human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship, do you ever find yourself questioning God's goodness or justice? How does this lesson's definition of evil—as that which cannot apply to God—challenge that internal assumption? Will you consciously remind yourself of this truth the next time you are tested?
?
You have learned that evil is either what God forbids or what harms the doer. God does neither. Now examine your own heart: Do you hold God to human standards of fairness? In what specific area of your life do you need to repent of judging God by your own measure?
?
The passage ends by declaring all speech of the Mu'tazila invalid. But what about subtle traces of their thinking in your own mind? When you think that God 'ought' to have done something differently, whose definition of good are you using? Will you actively replace that with the definitions from this lesson?

The Maturidi Definition and Refutation of Dualists
Imam al-Nasafi gives the Maturidi definition of evil by consequence and refutes dualists with the argument from creation without benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of evil based on consequences and how Imam al-Nasafi uses it along with the argument from purposeless creation to refute the dualists.

Core Idea
Evil is defined by lack of a praiseworthy consequence; God’s actions are always wise.

After presenting the Ahl al-Hadith definitions, Imam al-Nasafi now turns to the view of the Maturidi shuyukh (his own school). They define evil based on consequences: an act is good/wisdom if it has a praiseworthy outcome, and evil/foolishness if it lacks such an outcome. He then uses this to refute the dualists, adding a powerful argument from the myriad of created things in the earth and seas that have no apparent benefit—yet God’s creation of them is not futile. This further undermines any analogy from human needs to God’s actions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Maturidi definition
Read the definition: كل ما له عاقبة حميدة فهو وجه حكمة، وما ليست له عاقبة حميدة فهو سفه وقبيح. Note the two categories.
Wisdom (hikma) is attached to a praiseworthy consequence; foolishness (safah) is its absence.
2
Note the author's disclaimer
He says the proof for this is in his separate work, and he does not need to repeat it here. Focus on the refutation.
His goal is to invalidate the opponents' doubts.
3
See the argument from vast creation without benefit
He argues that God created countless things in the depths of the earth, mountains, and seas with no benefit for anyone. Yet this creation is not futile (abath).
This shows that the standard of benefit is not applicable to God.
4
Connect to the refutation of dualists
The dualists rely on analogy from human agents who act for benefit. But God is not needy, so His actions are not subject to benefit/harm. Therefore their analogy fails.
The passage ends with the conclusion: والله تعالى يجل عن ذلك فلم يكن

?

Does this lesson force you to reconsider whether that action was truly evil or merely lacking in worldly benefit? ?

Rational Judgments

Denial of Inherent Ugliness as Denial of Necessity
Arguing that denying the ugliness of certain things is a denial of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying the inherent ugliness of certain actions is a denial of necessary knowledge and leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Denying moral ugliness is like denying obvious beauty — it defies reason.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a concise argument against those who reject the idea that some actions are inherently ugly. He points out that such denial is not just a disagreement but a rejection of self-evident truths. Just as no rational person would deny the beauty of a perfectly formed body, no one should deny the ugliness of certain acts, because these are matters of necessary knowledge (ḍarūra). The passage shows that this logic, if broken, would even allow denial of the ugliness of disbelief itself — an impossible conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get the gist of the argument.
Pay attention to the structure: a denial, an analogy, a consequence.
2
Identify the key concepts
Note the terms 'qabīḥ' (ugly), 'ḥasan' (beautiful), 'ḍarūra' (necessity), and 'kufr' (disbelief). Understand how each is used in the argument.
The terms are central to understanding the rational judgment at play.
3
Analyze the analogy
The author compares denying the ugliness of certain things to denying the beauty of bodies. Both are denials of self-evident truths.
If one denies beauty, it's irrational; similarly, denying ugliness is irrational.
4
Trace the consequence
If denying ugliness were permissible, then one could deny the ugliness of kufr, which is false. This shows the original denial must be invalid.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the conclusion is absurd, so the premise is false.
5
Summarize the main point
Restate in your own words: the ugliness of certain acts is necessary knowledge; rejecting it leads to contradiction.
This lesson establishes a key principle in uṣūl al-dīn: moral truths are knowable by reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا وجه لإنكارهم قبح هذه الأشياء كما لا وجه لإنكار حسن كثير من الأجسام؛ لأنه إنكار الضرورة ولو جاز له هذا جاز لغيره إنكار قبح الكفر، وذلك باطل.
And there is no basis for their denial of the ugliness of these things, just as there is no basis for denying the beauty of many bodies; because it is a denial of necessity. And if this were permissible for him, it would be permissible for another to deny the ugliness of disbelief (kufr), and that is false.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of opponents who deny the inherent ugliness of certain actions. The author argues that such denial is equivalent to denying self-evident, necessary knowledge (ḍarūra). He draws an analogy: just as one cannot reasonably deny the beauty of many physical bodies, one cannot deny the ugliness of certain things. If this denial were allowed, then by the same logic one could deny the ugliness of disbelief (kufr), which is absurd. The argument establishes that some moral qualities are known necessarily and cannot be rejected without leading to contradiction.

Key Terms
قبح
حسن
ضرورة
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that moral judgments are purely subjective and that anyone can deny the ugliness of any act.
Correction: The passage argues that ugliness of certain acts is known necessarily, and denying it is like denying self-evident truths, leading to absurdities like denying the ugliness of disbelief.

Checkpoint
What does the author liken the denial of the ugliness of certain acts to?
A) Denying the beauty of bodies
B) Denying the existence of God
C) Denying the prophethood
D) Denying the resurrection

Key Takeaway
The ugliness of certain actions is a matter of necessary knowledge, and denying it leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you ignored your innate sense of right and wrong to justify something you knew was ugly? What will you do the next time you feel that tug to rationalize away moral truth?
?
If the ugliness of disbelief is so obvious to reason, why do you think people still embrace it? What hidden desires or fears in your own heart might dull your perception of necessary truths?
?
This lesson says denying moral ugliness is like denying obvious beauty. Think of a time you called something ugly beautiful. How did that deception affect your relationship with your Creator? Will you commit to aligning your judgments with the necessary knowledge He has placed within you?

Najjariyya on Aspects of Action and Kufr Analysis
Najjariyya doctrine that actions have multiple aspects, illustrated through the aspects of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Najjariyya argue that a single action can have multiple aspects with different moral values, using disbelief as a case study.

Core Idea
Actions have multiple aspects; the same action can be ugly from one aspect and not from another.

This lesson explores a distinctive doctrine of the Najjariyya theological school: that actions are not simply good or bad in themselves, but have multiple aspects (jihāt) that determine their moral status. The author illustrates this with a detailed analysis of disbelief (kufr), showing how its various attributes—being a thing, an accident, a belief, falsehood—carry different implications. Understanding this view helps clarify debates about the nature of human actions and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow, not every detail.
2
Identify the Najjariyya's main claim
The passage opens with: 'the Najjāriyya claim that the action has aspects (jihāt).' This is the core thesis. Notice how they distinguish between the act as God's act (not ugly) and as the servant's act (ugly).
3
Follow the analysis of disbelief (kufr)
The author lists several aspects of kufr: it is a thing, an accident, a belief, a proof, falsehood (and truth). For each aspect, consider whether the passage says it is ugly or not and why.
The key is to see that ugliness is not inherent in the universal categories.
4
Understand the falsehood/truth duality
Kufr as falsehood (kidhb) is ugly because it misleads, but as truth (ṣidq) it is not—it truthfully indicates the disbeliever's foolishness. This shows the same item can have opposing moral aspects.
5
Reflect on the analogy of blackness
The analogy of blackness (sawād) reinforces that general categories (thing, accident, color) do not determine specific properties. Just as not every thing is black, not every action (or belief) is ugly by virtue of being a thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم النجارية على هذا يزعمون أن الفعل له جهات، فمن حيث إنه فعل الله تعالى ليس بقبيح، ومن حيث إنه فعل العبد قبيح، ويجوز أن يكون للفعل جهات تقبح وتكون معصية ببعض الجهات ولا يكون كذلك ببعض الجهات، ألا ترى أن الكفر شيء وهو عرض وهو اعتقاد، وهو حجة الله تعالى على الكافر بالتعذيب وليس بقبيح، فمن حيث إنه شيء إذ لو كان قبيحًا من حيث إنه شيء لكان كل شيء قبيحًا فكان الإيمان قبيحًا سببًا للعقاب، وكذا كل طاعة، وكذا ليس بقبيح من حيث إنه عرض، ولا من حيث إنه اعتقاد لما يلزم أن يكون الإيمان قبيحا منهيًا عنه، وكذا الكفر كذب وهو دليل سفه الكافر، ودلالته على سفهه صدق فكان الكذب صدقا في الدلالة على سفه الكاذب وليس بقبيح ولا سفه لاستحقاق العقاب من حيث إنه صدق بل من حيث إنه كذب. وكذا السواد شيء وعرض ولون، وليس بسواد لأنه شيء ولا لأنه عرض ولا لأنه لون لوجودنا أشياء وأعراضا وألوانا ليست بسواد.
Then the Najjāriyya, upon this, claim that the action has aspects (jihāt). So by virtue of its being the act of God Almighty, it is not ugly; and by virtue of its being the act of the servant, it is ugly. And it is possible for an action to have aspects such that it is ugly and is disobedience (maʿṣiya) from some aspects, and not so from other aspects. Do you not see that disbelief (kufr) is a thing (shayʾ), and it is an accident (ʿaraḍ), and it is a belief (iʿtiqād), and it is God's proof (ḥujja) against the disbeliever for punishment, and it is not ugly? So by virtue of its being a thing: because if it were ugly by virtue of its being a thing, then every thing would be ugly, so faith would be ugly and a cause of punishment, and likewise every act of obedience. And likewise it is not ugly by virtue of its being an accident, nor by virtue of its being a belief, because that would entail that faith would be ugly and forbidden. And likewise disbelief is falsehood (kidhb), and it is evidence (dalīl) of the disbeliever's foolishness (safah), and its indication of his foolishness is truth (ṣidq); so falsehood became truth in indicating the foolishness of the liar, and it is not ugly nor foolishness (safah) [in that respect] for deserving punishment by virtue of its being truth, but rather by virtue of its being falsehood. And likewise blackness (sawād) is a thing, an accident, and a color, and it is not blackness because it is a thing, nor because it is an accident, nor because it is a color, since we find things, accidents, and colors that are not blackness.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage expounds the view of the Najjāriyya, a theological group, on the concept of 'aspects of action' (jihāt al-fiʿl). They hold that a single action can have different moral evaluations depending on the aspect considered. For example, an action as God's act is not ugly, but as the servant's act it is ugly. The author then provides a detailed analysis of disbelief (kufr) to illustrate this. Kufr has several aspects: it is a thing (shayʾ), an accident (ʿaraḍ), a belief (iʿtiqād), and also a proof (ḥujja) against the disbeliever. As a thing, it cannot be ugly because that would imply all things (including faith) are ugly. As an accident or belief, it cannot be inherently ugly for similar reasons. Moreover, kufr as falsehood (kidhb) also has an aspect of truth (ṣidq): it is truthful in indicating the disbeliever's foolishness. So the ugliness that deserves punishment comes from its aspect of falsehood, not from its aspect of truth. The author then gives an analogy: blackness (sawād) is a thing, accident, and color, but it is not blackness by virtue of being any of those general categories, because other things, accidents, and colors are not black. The purpose is to show that moral and descriptive predicates can depend on specific aspects, not on universal categories. This supports the Najjariyya's claim that actions can have multiple aspects with different moral values.

Key Terms
جهات
كفر
شيء
عرض
اعتقاد
كذب
صدق
سواد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Najjariyya consider disbelief (kufr) to be good or neutral in all respects.
Correction: They argue kufr is ugly only by virtue of its aspect of falsehood (kidhb), not by its other aspects (thing, accident, belief, proof, truth-indication).

Checkpoint
According to the Najjariyya analysis of disbelief (kufr), which aspect makes it deserving of punishment?
A) By virtue of being a thing
B) By virtue of being a belief
C) By virtue of being falsehood (kidhb)
D) By virtue of being a proof against the disbeliever

Key Takeaway
The moral evaluation of an action depends on the specific aspect under which it is considered, not on its universal category or essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a sin you have committed that you deeply regret. Can you identify an aspect of that action that was not inherently ugly—for example, a skill you used or a benefit you sought? Now, does recognizing that neutral aspect make the sin less sinful in Allah's sight? What is the aspect that truly makes it ugly, and are you owning that aspect fully?
?
The passage notes that kufr, though utterly evil, has aspects that are not ugly (e.g., it is a thing, an accident). Are there areas in your life where you are excusing a sin because you focus on its neutral or even good aspects—like a relationship that brings comfort but involves disobedience? What aspect are you ignoring that Allah will hold you accountable for?
?
If your good deeds are evaluated from multiple aspects, which aspect do you hope Allah rewards you for? Are you performing actions only for the aspect of visible piety, or for the aspect of sincere devotion that only He sees? Pledge one specific action this week where you will purify your intention to focus solely on the aspect that pleases Allah.

God's Will Cannot Be Thwarted
Nasafi presents a rational argument that if a disbeliever's will could oppose God's will, it would imply weakness in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi rational argument that if a disbeliever's will could oppose God's will, it would imply weakness in God, which is impossible.

Core Idea
No creature's will can overpower God's will.

This passage presents a crisp rational proof from the Maturidi tradition. Nasafi shows that allowing any creature's will to oppose God's will leads to an impossible consequence: it would make God weak. The argument uses a hypothetical scenario and an analogy from human kingship to drive the point home.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument builds from a condition to an unacceptable result.
The passage is dense but logical.
2
Identify the conditional scenario
Look for the 'if...then' structure: if God wills faith and the disbeliever wills disbelief, and disbelief occurs, what follows?
3
Follow the analogy
Nasafi compares God to a king. What signs of weakness would a king show? Apply that to God.
This analogy makes the abstract argument concrete.
4
Understand the refutation
The proof refutes the argument of mutual impediment (tamānu') and would validate dualism if accepted. See why that is a problem.
This connects to broader theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الأدلة العقلية لأهل الحق]: و المعقول أنه تعالى لو شاء من الكافر الإيمان (٤) والكافر شاء من نفسه الكفر، ثم كان الكفر دون الإيمان لتعطلت (۹) مشيئة الله تعالى بمشيئة الكافر، وكانت مشيئة الكافر أنفذ من مشيئة الله تعالى وكذا مشيئة إبليس لعنه الله أنفذ في سلطان الله تعالى من مشيئة الله تعالى لكون أكثر الخلق كافرين، وكذا من أدل الدلائل على ضعف الملك وعجزه أن يوجد في ملكه ما لا يشاء، ويشاء أشياء فلا تكون، ولا يشاء أشياء فتكون على كره منه، ووصف الله تعالى بذلك محال، وهذا يرد على دلالة التمانع بالإبطال ويؤدي إلى تصحيح مذهب الثنوية.
[Rational proofs of the people of truth]: And the intellectual proof is that if He Almighty willed faith from the disbeliever, and the disbeliever willed from himself disbelief, and then disbelief occurred without faith, then the will of Allah Almighty would be annulled by the will of the disbeliever, and the will of the disbeliever would be more penetrating than the will of Allah Almighty. And likewise, the will of Iblis, may Allah curse him, would be more penetrating in the dominion of Allah Almighty than the will of Allah Almighty, since most of creation are disbelievers. And likewise, among the strongest indications of the weakness of a king and his inability is that there occurs in his kingdom what he does not will, and he wills things that do not occur, and he does not will things that occur despite him. And describing Allah Almighty with that is impossible. And this refutes the proof of mutual impediment (dalālat al-tamānu') and leads to validating the dualist sect (madhhab al-thunawiyya).
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) from the Maturidi school. Nasafi constructs a conditional argument: if God wills faith from a disbeliever, and the disbeliever wills disbelief, and then disbelief actually occurs (while faith does not), this would mean that God's will is annulled or thwarted by the disbeliever's will. Consequently, the disbeliever's will would be more effective (anfadh) than God's will. The same applies to the will of Iblis, since most of humanity are disbelievers following him. Nasafi then draws an analogy: in human kingdoms, a king is considered weak if things happen in his kingdom against his will, or if what he wills does not happen, or if what he does not will happens despite him. Since such attributes cannot be attributed to God, the scenario is impossible. Therefore, the idea that a disbeliever's will could oppose God's will is invalid. This argument also refutes the proof of mutual impediment (dalīl al-tamānu'), which is used by some to argue for multiple creators, and it would even validate dualism. The proof establishes that God's will is always fulfilled.

Key Terms
مشيئة
الكفر
الإيمان
التمانع
الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof means God wills evil or that He forces disbelief.
Correction: The proof actually argues that whatever occurs is by God's will, not that He approves of evil; it shows that no creature can override God's will.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi argue would happen if a disbeliever's will could oppose God's will?
A) God would be defeated.
B) The disbeliever's will would be more effective than God's, implying weakness in God.
C) God would change His will.
D) The disbeliever would be forced to believe.

Key Takeaway
God's will is always fulfilled; no creature can oppose it without implying weakness in God, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you knowingly choose to disobey Allah, are you not acting as if your will is stronger than His? What does that reveal about the state of your tawheed? Sit with that question and repent.
?
If you truly believed that no creature's will can thwart Allah's will, would you still feel anxious about the future? Is there an area of your life where you are trying to force outcomes against what Allah has decreed? Surrender it to Him now.
?
Consider the king analogy: if a king's commands were ignored in his own kingdom, he would be weak. Do you treat Allah's commands as binding in every corner of your life, or are there 'exceptions' where you let your own will reign? Name one such exception and commit to change.

Rational Proof of the Effectiveness of Divine Will
Nasafi presents a rational argument that God's will is always effective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that God's will is always effective and how it refutes dualist claims.

Core Idea
No creature's will can override God's will; otherwise, God would be weak.

Nasafi presents a concise rational argument to demonstrate that God's will is always effective. He begins with three Qur'anic verses that assert God's absolute power over faith and disbelief, then builds a logical case: if a creature's will could defeat God's will, that creature would be more powerful—an impossible conclusion. This argument also refutes the dualist proof of mutual hindrance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qur'anic verses
Read the three verses carefully. Notice that they all state that if God had willed universal faith, it would have happened. This establishes the premise that God's will is unconditionally effective.
These verses are from Surah al-An'am, Yunus, and al-An'am respectively.
2
Understand the rational argument
Follow the logical chain: if God wills faith from a disbeliever, but the disbeliever wills disbelief, and disbelief actually occurs, then God's will is defeated. That would imply the disbeliever's will is more powerful—which is rationally impossible for God.
This reductio ad absurdum shows the impossibility of any creature's will overriding God's.
3
Examine the analogy of the king
Nasafi compares the situation to a king in whose kingdom things happen against his will. Such a king is clearly weak. Applying this to God would be absurd, so the original premise (that God's will might be ineffective) must be false.
4
See the refutation of dualism
The argument also refutes the proof of mutual hindrance (al-tamānuʿ), which dualists used to argue for two gods. By showing that God's will is always effective, the basis for that proof is invalidated, and monotheism is strengthened.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى: ﴿ فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَنكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ ﴾ (١) وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُهُمْ جَمِيعًا ﴾ (٢) وقال: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ اللهُ مَا أَشْتَرَكُوا ﴾ (٣). الأدلة العقلية لأهل الحق: والمعقول أنه تعالى لو شاء من الكافر الإيمان والكافر شاء من نفسه الكفر، ثم كان الكفر دون الإيمان لتعطلت مشيئة الله تعالى بمشيئة الكافر، وكانت مشيئة الكافر أنفذ من مشيئة الله تعالى وكذا مشيئة إبليس لعنه الله أنفذ في سلطان الله تعالى من مشيئة الله تعالى لكون أكثر الخلق كافرين، وكذا من أدل الدلائل على ضعف الملك وعجزه أن يوجد في ملكه ما لا يشاء، ويشاء أشياء فلا تكون، ولا يشاء أشياء فتكون على كره منه، ووصف الله تعالى بذلك محال، وهذا يرد على دلالة التمانع بالإبطال ويؤدي إلى تصحيح مذهب الثنوية.
He said: {If He willed, He would have guided you all together} (1). And He said: {And if your Lord willed, everyone on earth would have believed, all of them} (2). And He said: {If Allah willed, they would not have associated partners} (3). The rational proofs of the people of truth: And it is rationally understood that if He willed from the disbeliever faith, and the disbeliever willed from himself disbelief, then disbelief occurred without faith, then the will of Allah would be rendered ineffective by the will of the disbeliever, and the will of the disbeliever would be more effective than the will of Allah. Likewise, the will of Iblis (may Allah curse him) would be more effective in the dominion of Allah than the will of Allah because most of creation are disbelievers. And likewise, one of the clearest signs of a king's weakness and incapacity is that something he does not will occurs in his kingdom, and he wills things but they do not happen, and he does not will things but they happen against his will. Describing Allah with that is impossible. And this refutes the proof of mutual hindrance (al-tamānuʿ) by invalidating it and leads to the correction of the doctrine of the dualists.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument for the effectiveness of divine will. It begins by citing three Qur'anic verses that clearly state that if God had willed universal faith, He would have brought it about. The author then presents a rational argument: if God willed faith from a disbeliever, but the disbeliever willed disbelief, and disbelief occurred, then God's will would be defeated by the disbeliever's will, implying the disbeliever's will is more powerful—which is impossible. The same logic applies to Iblis, since most people are disbelievers. The author draws an analogy: a king in whose kingdom things happen against his will is rightly considered weak, but to attribute such weakness to God is absurd. Finally, the argument refutes the theological proof of mutual hindrance (dalālat al-tamānuʿ), which dualists used to argue for two gods, showing that the proper understanding of divine will leads to the invalidity of that proof and actually supports the monotheistic correction of dualist errors.

Key Terms

جبر
التمانع
الثنوية
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof claims that God compels disbelief in people.
Correction: The argument does not claim compulsion; it refutes the idea that any creature's will can override God's will, while human free will remains.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Nasafi use to illustrate the weakness of a ruler whose will is not carried out?

B) A king in whose kingdom things happen against his will
C) A parent who cannot control a child
D) A general whose army is defeated

Key Takeaway
God's will is always effective; no creature's will can override it, and this truth refutes dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your own plans or desires have overridden what God has decreed for you? How does this lesson's truth that God's will is always effective challenge that feeling? What would it mean for you to submit fully to that reality today?
?
If God's will is always effective, then your sins and shortcomings are not a sign that His will has been defeated. Instead, they are within His will—though He may command you differently. Are you using this truth as an excuse for complacency, or does it drive you to seek His will more earnestly? Ask yourself: In what area of your life are you resisting His will while pretending it doesn't matter?
?
This lesson refutes the idea that any force (like your own desires, society, or even Iblis) can ultimately thwart God's plan. Do you truly believe this in your heart, or do you sometimes act as if evil has independent power? How would your daily worries and fears change if you lived with absolute certainty that God's will is always effective?

The Mu'tazilite Objection and Definitions of Foolishness
Nasafi presents the objection and defines foolishness to refute it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite rational objection that willing foolishness makes one foolish, and how Nasafi refutes it by redefining foolishness in terms of prohibition and praiseworthy outcomes.

Core Idea
Foolishness is not absolute; it depends on prohibition or outcome.

The Mu'tazilites argued by analogy that if a human who wills foolishness is foolish, then God would be foolish if He willed evil. Nasafi responds by examining what foolishness really means. He first presents a hypothetical about impossible wills, then introduces two definitions: the Ash'ari view (foolishness = what is prohibited) and the Maturidi view (foolishness = what has no praiseworthy outcome). With Prophetic examples, he shows that apparent foolishness can be wisdom when it serves a greater good.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the first few lines where Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite argument and his initial counter about impossible wills.
Notice the hypothetical about willing something that would negate Lordship.
2
Understand the two definitions
Nasafi gives the Ash'ari definition (foolishness = what is prohibited) and then his own Maturidi definition (foolishness = what has no praiseworthy outcome). Compare them.
3
Examine the Prophetic examples
Look at the examples of the son of Adam (Cain) and Prophet Moses' prayer. How do these illustrate that a seemingly foolish will can be wise if it leads to a good outcome?
These examples prove that context and outcome matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما شبهتهم المعقولة فقولهم : إن مريد السفه سفيه اعتبارا بالشاهد. قلنا: إرادة ما لو كان لأوجب زوال ربوبيته، وإرادة أن لا يكون ما لو لم يكن لزالت ألوهيته، سفه، وقد مر أنه لو أراد إيمان فرعون فكان ولم يرد كفره فلم يكن مع أنه علم أن كفره يكون وإيمانه لا يكون لكانت إرادة تجهيل نفسه وهو سفه. ثم نقول : السفه عند الأشعري وأهل الحديث ما نهى عنه، وفي الشاهد مريد السفه منهي عنه فكان به سفيهًا، وفي الغائب لا نهي فلم يكن سفيها، وعندنا السفه ما لم يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة فلو تعلقت بإرادة السفه لما كانت سفها كما لم يكن من ابن آدم عليه السلام بقوله: ﴿ إِنِّي أُرِيدُ أَن تَبُوا بِإِثْمِي وَإِيْمَكَ ﴾ سفها وإن كانت إرادة سفه، ولم يكن من موسى عليه السلام حيث قال: (وَاشْدُدْ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ فَلَا يُؤْمِنُوا) سفها فكذا من الله تعالى، وكذا موسى عليه السلام حيث قال للسحرة (بَلْ القُوا) وقال أَلْقُوا مَا أَنتُم مُلْقُونَ أكان ذلك الإلقاء سفها أم حكمة (فإن قالوا: كان فجعلوا معارضة الرسول في المعجزة بما يأتيه الساحر من المخرقة حكمة) وهو كفر، وإن قالوا : كان الإلقاء سفهًا، قلنا: وهل أراد موسى وجود الإلقاء؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد زعموا أنه لم يرد ظهور حجته وإبطال ما يعارضون به حجته وأرادوا بقاء أمر رسالته في حيز الإشكال والتردد، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أقروا أنه أراد السفه ولم يصر به سفيها لما كان تحت إرادته ظهور حجته وغلبة دلائله، وفيما نحن فيه يكون تحت إرادة السفه تحقق علمه وخبره فيكون حكمة لا سفها. ويقال لهم: لو أن رسولاً يخبر قومه أن فلانا الكافر يجيئني فيشتمني ويقصد قتلي أيريد تحقيق ذلك ليظهر صدقة في إخباره عن الغيب فيكون معجزة له أم يريد أن لا يوجد ذلك فيظهر كذبه ولا تتحقق معجزته؟ فإن قالوا: بالأول. فقد أقروا أن مريد السفه لا يكون سفيهًا إذا كانت لذلك عاقبة حميدة، وإن قالوا بالثاني ظهر للناس تعنتهم ومكابرتهم، وما قالوا إن مريد سفه نفسه في الشاهد سفيه، فقد سبق جوابه في مسألة خلق الأفعال على أن كثيرًا من أصحابنا لا يطلقون أنه أراد شتم
As for their rational objection, it is their statement: “Indeed, the one who wills foolishness (al-sufh) is foolish, by analogy to the witnessed (shāhid).” We say: To will what, if it occurred, would necessitate the removal of His Lordship, and to will that what, if it did not occur, would remove His Divinity, is foolishness. And it has already passed that if He willed the faith of Pharaoh and it occurred, and He did not will his disbelief and it did not occur, while He knew that his disbelief would occur and his faith would not occur, that would be a will to make Himself ignorant, and that is foolishness. Then we say: Foolishness according to al-Ash‘arī and the People of Hadith is what is prohibited. In the witnessed, the one who wills what is prohibited is prohibited, so he is foolish by it; in the absent (ghā’ib), there is no prohibition, so He is not foolish. According to us, foolishness is that which is not connected to a praiseworthy outcome. So if a foolish will is connected to a praiseworthy outcome, it is not foolishness, just as it was not foolishness from the son of Adam (peace be upon him) when he said: “Indeed, I intend that you bear my sin and your sin” (5:29), even though it was a will of foolishness. And it was not foolishness from Moses (peace be upon him) when he said: “And harden their hearts so that they do not believe” (10:88). So likewise from Allah, the Most High. And likewise Moses (peace be upon him) when he said to the sorcerers: “Rather, you throw” (20:66) and said: “Throw what you are going to throw” (26:43). Was that throwing foolishness or wisdom? If they say: “It was,” then they have made opposing the Messenger in the miracle with the trickery of the sorcerer wisdom, and that is unbelief. And if they say: “The throwing was foolishness,” we say: Did Moses will the occurrence of the throwing? If they say: “No,” then they have claimed that he did not will the manifestation of his proof and the nullification of what they oppose his proof with, and they intend that his matter of prophethood remain in the realm of ambiguity and hesitation. And if they say: “Yes,” then they have affirmed that he willed foolishness and did not become foolish by it, because under his will was the manifestation of his proof and the dominance of his evidences. And in the matter under discussion, under the will of foolishness is the realization of His knowledge and His report, so it is wisdom, not foolishness. And it is said to them: If a messenger informs his people that a certain disbeliever will come to me, insult me, and intend to kill me, does he will the occurrence of that so that his truthfulness appears in his news of the unseen, thus becoming a miracle for him, or does he will that it does not occur so that his lie appears and his miracle is not realized? If they say: “The first,” then they have affirmed that the one who wills foolishness is not foolish if it has a praiseworthy outcome. And if they say: “The second,” then their obstinacy and stubbornness become apparent to the people. And what they said: “Indeed, the one who wills foolishness for himself in the witnessed is foolish” – its answer has already preceded in the issue of the creation of actions, except that many of our companions do not say that He willed abuse
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a rational Mu'tazilite objection. The objection states: in the witnessed world, anyone who wills foolishness (al-sufh) is considered foolish; therefore, if God were to will foolishness, He would be foolish, which is impossible. Consequently, God cannot will evil. Nasafi responds by examining the nature of foolishness. He first presents a hypothetical: if God willed something that would necessitate the removal of His Lordship (i.e., something impossible), that would be foolish, but that is not the case. He then discusses the Ash‘arī and Hadith people’s definition: foolishness is what is prohibited, and since in the unseen (al-ghā’ib) there is no prohibition, God is not foolish. Nasafi then gives his own definition: foolishness is that which is not connected to a praiseworthy outcome. If a seemingly foolish action leads to a good outcome, it is not truly foolish. He supports this with examples from the Qur’an: the son of Adam (Cain) saying he intends to let Abel bear his sin (willing apparent injustice but for a praiseworthy purpose?), and Prophet Moses praying for Pharaoh’s heart to be hardened (which seems to will disbelief) – these are not considered foolish because they serve a greater divine purpose. He then delves into the example of Moses telling the sorcerers to throw their staffs first. He poses a dilemma to the Mu‘tazila: either that throwing was wisdom (which would make opposing the miracle with sorcery wisdom – i.e., validating sorcery, which is kufr) or it was foolishness. If they choose the latter, he asks: did Moses will that throwing to occur? If they say no, then they claim Moses did not will the manifestation of his proof; if they say yes, then they admit Moses willed something apparently foolish but did not become foolish because it led to his proof’s victory. Similarly, God’s will for evil events serves His knowledge and truthfulness, making it wisdom. Finally, Nasafi presents an analogy: a prophet foretelling a disbeliever’s abusive behavior – does he will it to happen (to prove his truthfulness) or not? If the former, then willing evil for a good outcome is not foolish. Thus, the rational objection fails. Nasafi notes that many of his fellow scholars do not even say that God wills abuse (shatm) outright, perhaps avoiding the term.

Key Terms
سفه
شاهد
غائب
عاقبة حميدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any will for apparent evil is intrinsically foolish, regardless of context or outcome.
Correction: Nasafi shows that foolishness is defined by outcome: if a will leads to a praiseworthy end, it is not foolishness, as seen in the examples of prophets.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the definition of foolishness (al-sufh)?
A) Whatever is prohibited by revelation.
B) Whatever does not have a praiseworthy outcome.
C) Whatever is willed by a foolish person.
D) Whatever involves causing harm to others.

Key Takeaway
Foolishness is not simply willing evil; it depends on whether the action is prohibited or lacks a praiseworthy outcome.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials or see apparent evil in the world, do you ever silently charge God with foolishness? Read the Quranic examples of Cain and Moses—how does knowing that God's will always has a praiseworthy outcome change your attitude toward your own unanswered prayers or difficult circumstances? Will you today surrender the need to understand and trust His hidden wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazilites assumed God must act according to human standards of wisdom. Is there an area of your life—your career, family, or personal desires—where you are still judging God's decree as 'foolish' because it doesn't match your expectations? What would it mean for you to truly accept that God's actions are judged by their ultimate outcome, not your immediate perception?
?
The prophets' wills appeared foolish to human eyes, yet they were not. Have you ever been ashamed of a decision you made in obedience to God because others called it foolish? How does this lesson empower you to stand firm in such situations, knowing that the praiseworthy outcome is with Allah? Will you commit to one act of obedience today that might seem 'foolish' to the world?

The Dilemma of Moses and the Sorcerers
Nasafi presents the dilemma of Moses and the sorcerers and a prophetic analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the example of Moses telling the sorcerers to throw their staffs and a prophetic analogy to further refute the Mu'tazilite argument.

Core Idea
Even seemingly foolish actions become wisdom when they serve a higher purpose.

Nasafi deepens the refutation by presenting a dilemma: when Moses told the sorcerers to throw first, was that action foolish or wise? If the Mu'tazilites say it was wise, they validate sorcery as a miracle; if they say it was foolish, then they must admit that willing foolishness does not make one foolish if it leads to the manifestation of truth. Nasafi then reinforces this with an analogy of a prophet foretelling abuse to prove his truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Read the part where Nasafi discusses Moses' command to the sorcerers. Identify the two options he presents to the Mu'tazilites.
Focus on the reasoning behind each option and its consequences.
2
Understand the consequences
If they say the throwing was wisdom, they commit kufr by equating sorcery with a miracle. If they say it was foolishness, then they must admit Moses willed foolishness but was not foolish.
3
Analyze the prophetic analogy
Nasafi gives an analogy of a prophet foretelling a disbeliever's abuse. He asks whether the prophet wills it to happen or not. Work through the implications.
This analogy directly undermines the Mu'tazilite position.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the Mu'tazilites say that Moses' command to the sorcerers to throw was wisdom?
A) It would validate Moses' prophethood.
B) It would be kufr because it equates sorcery with a miracle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific situation in your life where God allowed something that seemed harmful or foolish. How does the principle in this lesson—that apparent foolishness can serve a wise purpose—challenge you to trust Him more deeply? What is one concrete step you will take this week to surrender your judgment to His wisdom?
?

?

Is there a painful situation in your life that you have resisted because it seemed foolish or unjust?
