The Timelessness of Divine Information and the Impossibility of Breach of Promise
Nasafi argues that breach of promise implies divine falsehood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that breach of promise (خلف في الوعيد) would imply that God's future reports could be false, which is impossible for God.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is timeless; breach of promise would make His report a lie.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a critical issue: can God break a promise of punishment? He argues that because God's information is not bounded by time, any future report must be true. If God promises punishment and then does not fulfill it, that report would be false, which is impossible for God. Let's follow his reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Nasafi builds his argument step by step.
2
Identify the core claim
Find where Nasafi states that reporting about the future is lying if the informant knows the opposite will happen.
He uses the example of a punishment that hasn't occurred.
3
Understand timelessness
Notice his key premise: God's information is not tied to time, so past and future are not applicable to it. This means every divine report is either true or false in an absolute sense.
4
Connect to impossibility
Conclude: if God promises a punishment and does not fulfill it, His report would be false, which is impossible. Therefore, breach of promise is impossible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا داخل فيهم والتعذيب في حقه لم يوجد فيصير إخباراً عن المخبر لا على ما هو به، وهذا كذب عند هذا القائل.
على أنا بينا أن الإخبار في المستقبل يكون كذبًا أيضا إذا علم المخبر أن المخبر لا يكون على أن إخبار الله تعالى غير معلق بالزمان فلا يتصور فيه الماضي والمستقبل على ما قررنا، فمهما كان مخبره على خلاف ما هو به كان كذبًا – تعالى الله عز وجل عن ذلك - على أن أكثر هؤلاء الذين يجوزون الخلف في الوعيد يذهبون إلى أن مغفرة الكافر جائزة في الحكمة ليست بخارجة عنها، غير أنا نعرف أن الكفر لا يغفر بالخبر . فيقال لهم: بم تعرفون ذلك ولعل الله يخلف وعيده ويغفر لهم ويدخلهم الجنة ؟ فإن قالوا: عرفنا ذلك بقول الرسول عليه السلام أو بإجماع الأمة.
فنقول: كل هذا لا يمنع الله تعالى عن الكرم والخلف في الوعيد كرم، فدل أن القول بالعموم غير مستقيم على أصول أهل السنة، وبالله التوفيق.
This is included among them, and punishment in his case has not occurred, so it becomes reporting about the object of report not as it is, and this is lying according to this speaker. Furthermore, we have shown that reporting about the future is also lying if the informant knows that the object of report will not be, and that God's information is not tied to time, so past and future are not conceivable in it according to what we established. Whenever His report is contrary to what it is, it is lying – exalted is God Almighty above that. Moreover, most of those who allow breach of promise hold that forgiveness of the disbeliever is possible in wisdom and not outside it, except that we know that disbelief is not forgiven by the report. So it is said to them: How do you know that? Perhaps God will break His promise and forgive them and admit them to Paradise. If they say: We know that by the statement of the Messenger (peace be upon him) or by the consensus of the community. Then we say: All this does not prevent God Almighty from generosity, and breach of promise is generosity. So it is indicated that the position of generality is not sound on the principles of Ahl al-Sunna, and success is with God.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who allow breach of promise (خلف في الوعيد) in God. It begins by stating that if punishment has not occurred in a given case, then a report that it will occur would be reporting contrary to reality, which is lying. The author then argues that God's knowledge is timeless, so a future report is either true or false; if God promises punishment and then does not fulfill it, the report would be false. However, it is noted that those who allow breach of promise argue that forgiveness of the disbeliever is possible in wisdom, but we know from revelation (the Prophet's statement or consensus) that disbelief is not forgiven. The author then poses a challenge: the opponents might say that God could still break His promise out of generosity. The author counters that the report from revelation does not prevent God from being generous, but this shows that the claim of absolute generality (عموم) of threat verses is not consistent with Sunni principles. The passage concludes that the view of generality is not sound on Sunni fundamentals.

Key Terms
خلف في الوعيد
الإخبار
الماضي والمستقبل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can break a promise without it being a lie because He is all-powerful.
Correction: If God's knowledge is timeless, a future promise is a report about what will happen. Not fulfilling it makes that report false. Power does not override truthfulness.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why would breach of promise (خلف في الوعيد) be impossible for God?
A) Because God is merciful and never punishes.
B) Because God's information is timeless, so a future report must be true; non-fulfillment would make it false.
C) Because the Prophet forbade breaking promises.
D) Because sinners always deserve punishment.

Key Takeaway
God's timeless information means every divine report is necessarily true; thus, breach of promise is impossible because it would imply falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's information is absolutely true and He never breaks His promise, what does that imply about the promises you make? Do you treat your own promises as binding, or do you excuse yourself with changes of circumstance? How can you align your character with the attribute of truthfulness that God has demonstrated?
?
Reflect on a time you broke a promise. Did you consider it a minor thing? Now consider that God's promises are never broken. What does that comparison teach you about your own reliability? Will you commit to being a person of your word from today?
?
God's timeless knowledge means He cannot change His report. You, however, can change your actions. Are there promises you have made to God (like prayers, repentance, commitments) that you have not kept? What does this lesson say about the seriousness of those broken promises? What will you do about it today?

Refuting the Claim that Revelation Permits Breach of Promise
Nasafi rebuts opponents who rely on revelation to permit breach of promise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the opponents who use revelation to justify God's breach of promise, and why this shows that the view of absolute generality (عموم) of threat verses is not Sunni.

Core Idea
Revelation cannot override God's truthfulness; breach of promise remains impossible.

Having established that breach of promise is impossible for God, Nasafi now addresses a counterargument: some opponents claim that God might forgive disbelievers out of generosity, citing revelation. Nasafi challenges them to prove that revelation prevents such forgiveness, and then concludes that the position of generality (عموم) of threat verses is not sound on Sunni principles. Let's examine his rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Look for the opponents' claim and Nasafi's response.
2
Identify the opponents' position
Note that they say forgiveness of the disbeliever is possible in wisdom, and they rely on the Prophet's statement or consensus to know it won't happen.
They argue that revelation tells us disbelief is not forgiven.
3
Understand Nasafi's challenge
He asks: how do you know that God won't break His promise and forgive? If you say by revelation, he counters that revelation itself does not prevent God from being generous.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that the opponents' position leads to the claim that God can break His promise out of generosity. This, he says, shows that the view of absolute generality (عموم) of threat verses is not consistent with Sunni principles.
The key: if breach of promise is permissible, then the threat verses are not universally applicable.

This is included among them, and punishment in his case has not occurred, so it becomes reporting about the object of report not as it is, and this is lying according to this speaker. Furthermore, we have shown that reporting about the future is also lying if the informant knows that the object of report will not be, and that God's information is not tied to time, so past and future are not conceivable in it according to what we established. Whenever His report is contrary to what it is, it is lying – exalted is God Almighty above that. Then we say: All this does not prevent God Almighty from generosity, and breach of promise is generosity. So it is indicated that the position of generality is not sound on the principles of Ahl al-Sunna, and success is with God.

The Implicit Rational Conditions in Verses of Promise and Threat
The author argues that conditions of dying on faith or unbelief are implicit in divine promises and threats.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi kalām argues that rational conditions are implicitly attached to general verses of divine promise and threat, even when not explicitly stated.

Core Idea
Divine promises and threats are rationally qualified even when textually absolute.

This passage presents a sophisticated dialectical argument defending the Maturidi view that the general verses of promise and threat are implicitly conditioned by rational factors, such as dying on faith or unbelief. The author anticipates objections from those who hold to textual literalism and systematically refutes them, showing that without such conditions, God's truthfulness would be compromised. By the end of this lesson, you will grasp how reason and revelation work together in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the rhetorical question at the beginning.
2
Identify the central dilemma
The opponent forces a choice: either attach conditions to promise and threat verses or leave them absolute. See why the second option leads to attributing falsehood to God.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Examine the chronology objection
The opponent suggests ignorance of revelation order to treat verses as simultaneous. The author points out the inconsistency in preferring one set of verses over another.
This shows the need for a consistent criterion.
4
Understand the rational evidence argument
Reason itself dictates that eternal punishment for believers or eternal reward for disbelievers is impossible. This rational evidence qualifies both sets of verses.
The Ash'arite challenge to this claim is noted.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how the Maturidi position harmonizes reason and revelation, and why the Ash'arite objection does not undermine it.
The passage ends with a reminder of God's mercy.

تعالى لا محالة، حيث لم يحقق الوعيد؟ فإن قال: دليل القيد قائم وهو دلالة العقل عند نزول صيغة الوعد والوعيد لما أن تخليد من آمن في النار مما يأباه العقل، وكذا تخليد من تاب عن المعصية، وكذا تخليد من ختم By God, will he be eternal in the Fire? Then necessarily yes. It was said: Similarly, everyone who committed a major sin then repented, or was a disbeliever then embraced Islam and died upon his Islam, will be eternal in Paradise and not eternal in the Fire? Then necessarily yes. It was said: Is it not that the expression is absolute in both promise and threat; no condition of dying upon faith exists in the expression of promise, nor a condition of dying upon disbelief and persistence in sin in the expression of threat? Then necessarily yes. It was said: Then you are between two matters: either you say that the condition is not attached to the verses of threat nor to the verses of promise, and every expression encompasses every individual among its individuals, and God becomes a liar by admitting the one who committed a major sin and repented or the disbeliever who embraced Islam into Paradise, and by admitting the believer who apostatized and committed a major sin into the Fire; or you say that the restriction of dying upon faith or dying before repentance is attached to the expression of promise and threat even if not explicitly connected? If you say the first, then you have disbelieved; and if you say the second, then you have abandoned your doctrine and contradicted yourself. Then every answer you have for excluding one who falls under the general expression occurring in the promise from its ruling is the same answer for excluding one who falls under the expression of promise from its ruling. And if you say that the verses of promise and verses of threat were revealed and you are ignorant of the chronology of their revelation, you make them as if they were revealed together, so some become a restriction and specification of others. It was said: Then why are you (by making the verses of threat a restriction on the verses of promise) more entitled than your opponent (by his making the verses of promise a restriction on the verses of threat), given that if you are ignorant of the chronology, then you make them as if they came as a single judgment? There is no doubt that they were revealed separately, so there is no avoiding attributing lying to God Almighty inevitably, since the threat was not fulfilled? Then he said: The evidence for the restriction stands, namely the indication of reason at the revelation of the expression of promise and threat, because eternally abiding one who believed in the Fire is repugnant to reason, and likewise eternally abiding one who repented from sin, and likewise eternally abiding one who ended with disbelief in Paradise is repugnant to reason, and likewise eternally abiding one who committed a sin, since by it he becomes an enemy of God, and eternally abiding the enemy in Paradise is impossible. The rational evidence stands at the revelation of every verse of promise and threat, so all the verses are restricted by rational evidence. We said: The Ash'arites dispute with you most vehemently about this, claiming that there is no rational evidence for the impossibility of eternally abiding obedient believers who have no sin in the Fire, nor of eternally abiding disbelievers and sinners in Paradise. Then if it were said to you: Eternally abiding one who believed and did righteous deeds and did not disbelieve but ended with affirmation – in the Fire is repugnant to reason, and likewise nullifying faith which is the utmost in goodness and many acts of worship each of which should be rewarded tenfold by God's report, due to committing something that is not utmost in [؟], and it is not rewarded except with one by God's report, along with the presence of fear of punishment, hope of mercy, trust in His generosity – and it is in itself something driven to by overpowering desire, overpowering anger, or intense zeal – this also is repugnant to reason, so it becomes a restriction on the verses of threat connected to eternal abiding. Likewise the existence of evidence that God is described with mercy, compassion, pardon, and forgiveness prevents generalizing the verses of threat as well. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument defending the Maturidi position that the general verses of divine promise (وعد) and threat (وعيد) are implicitly qualified by rational conditions, even if those conditions are not explicitly stated in the text. The author presents a series of objections from an opponent (likely a literalist or a Mu'tazilī who maintains absolute generality) and refutes them.

The argument begins with a rhetorical question: 'By God, will he be eternal in the Fire? Then necessarily yes.' This refers to the well-known case of a person who dies unrepentant of a major sin – the verse of threat applies. The author then extends the logic: if a person repents or embraces Islam, the promise of Paradise applies. The opponent then asks: Are not the expressions of promise and threat absolute, without explicit conditions of dying on faith or unbelief? The author responds that one must either (a) keep the conditions unapplied, which makes God a liar because then even repentant sinners would go to Hell and apostates to Paradise, or (b) acknowledge that the conditions of dying on faith or before repentance are attached even if not explicitly mentioned. The opponent is forced to choose the second, which is the Maturidi view.

Then the author notes that any reply the opponent gives for excluding someone from the general promise is equally valid for excluding someone from the general threat. The opponent might argue ignorance of chronology (which verses were revealed first) and treat them as simultaneous, but that would still require specification. The author asks why making the threat verses a restriction on promise verses is more valid than the reverse. Since they were revealed separately, without specification God would be lying if the threats are unfulfilled.

The opponent then appeals to rational evidence (دليل العقل) as a constant qualifier: reason dictates that it is impossible for a believer to be eternally in Hell or a repentant sinner to be eternally punished, so reason restricts both promise and threat. The author counters that the Ash'arites vehemently deny this, claiming reason alone cannot judge such matters; it is not rationally impossible for a believer to be punished eternally or a disbeliever rewarded.

The author then provides a further argument: if it were said that eternal punishment of a believer is rationally repugnant, likewise nullifying all his good deeds due to a single sin is repugnant, especially since each deed should be rewarded tenfold. So reason restricts the threat verses. Moreover, God's attributes of mercy and forgiveness also prevent the general application of threats. The passage concludes with 'والله الموفق' (And God is the granter of success).

وعد
وعيد
تخليد
قيد
صيغة عموم
دليل العقل
إحباط
الأشعرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Maturidis deny the literal generality of threat verses or ignore them.
Correction: Maturidis affirm the literal generality but hold that rational conditions implicitly qualify them, so the verses are not applied without those conditions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if one refuses to attach any condition to the verses of promise and threat?
A) The verses remain general and God's word is preserved.
B) God would be made a liar by including repentant sinners in Hell and apostates in Paradise.
C) Reason would then automatically supply the conditions anyway.
D) The Ash'arites would accept this position.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi theology holds that rational conditions implicitly qualify general scriptural promises and threats, preserving God's truthfulness and harmonizing reason with revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's threats were absolute without any rational qualification, would you live in constant fear of one sin destroying all your hope? How does the Maturidi understanding of implicit conditions affect the way you approach repentance and trust in God's mercy? Let this lesson press you to examine whether your hope is built on God's attributes or on a mechanical reading of texts.
?
When you read verses of promise and threat, do you unconsciously reason about their conditions, or do you take them at face value? This lesson claims that reason must qualify revelation. Does your heart accept that God's justice and mercy are harmonious, or do you struggle to reconcile them? What does your struggle reveal about your understanding of God's nature?
?
The Ash'arite denial of rational conditions leads to the possibility that a believer could be eternally punished. Does that thought disturb you? Why? If it does, are you relying on your own sense of justice rather than submitting to God's will? Let this question uncover whether you trust God's wisdom or your own moral intuitions.

Interpreting 'Today' in the Perfection Verse
Nasafi gives two main interpretations of the word 'today' in the verse to avoid the implication of prior imperfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key interpretations of the word 'today' in the verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' and why they avoid the implication of prior imperfection.

Core Idea
The 'today' of perfection is not a single day but the entire prophetic era.

The verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' might seem to imply that Islam was imperfect before that specific day. Nasafi, however, offers interpretations that avoid this implication. He first clarifies that 'today' is not literal, then provides two main interpretations: either it's a rhetorical opening or it refers to the Prophet's entire era. This lesson unpacks both views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi structures his argument: first a general point, then two sub-aspects, then an analogy.
Pay attention to the king analogy at the end.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Nasafi gives two main interpretations after stating that 'today' is not literal. Find them and note how they differ: one drops the temporal meaning entirely, the other extends it to the Prophet's era.
3
Reflect on the king analogy
The analogy of a king addressing his subjects helps clarify the second interpretation. Ask yourself how the king's 'today' means his entire reign, and how that applies to the verse.
This analogy shows the rhetorical use of 'today' to mean an extended period.

وجوه تأويل آية اليوم أكملت] تم تأويل الآية والله أعلم على وجوه: أحدها - أنه لا يريد باليوم يوما يشار إليه كما لا يريد بقوله: ﴿وَرَضِيتُ لَكُمُ الْإِسْلَامَ دِينًا﴾ يومًا يشار إليه بل كان الدين مرضيا عنده ثم بعد ذلك وجهان: أحدهما: إسقاط معنى اليوم وحمله على ما جرت العادة في التكلم به لافتتاح الكلام دون تحقيق الوقت. والثاني: أن يراد به عصر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إذ كان قبل ذلك فترة وقد كانت مست الحاجة إلى من يبين لهم الحق فيبين ذلك برسوله عليه السلام في ذلك العصر وهذا ظاهر في مبتذل الكلام، فإن ملكًا من الملوك لو مَنَّ على أهل مملكته فقال: إن من كان سبقكم من آبائكم كانوا في زمن من كان ملك الولاية قبلي في أنواع الفتن وصنوف من المصائب والمحن بسبب سوء مملكته وخوفه في معاملة وتعديه عن الصواب في تدابير سياسته ورسوم إمارته، وأنتم اليوم في ظل السلامة وتحت جناح الأمنة وكنف الدعة والراحة بيمن سياستي وشفافتي ورأفتي على طبقات رعيتي وأهل مملكتي لا يريد بذلك اليوم الذي يخاطبهم فيه بذلك بل جميع عصره و زمان ثبوت سلطانه و دولته فكذا هذا والله أعلم.
Aspects of the interpretation of the verse 'Today I have perfected' — the interpretation of the verse, and God knows best, is upon aspects: One of them is that He does not intend by 'today' a specific day pointed to, just as He does not intend by His statement: 'And I am pleased with Islam as a religion for you' a specific day pointed to; rather, the religion was pleasing to Him earlier. Then after that there are two aspects: One of them: dropping the meaning of 'today' and carrying it upon what is customary in speech of opening the speech without specifying time. The second: that what is meant by it is the era of the Prophet, peace be upon him, since before that there was a period of intermission (fatrat), and there was a need for someone to clarify the truth for them, so He clarified that through His Messenger, peace be upon him, in that era. And this is apparent in common speech. For if a king were to bestow favor upon the people of his kingdom and say: 'Indeed, those who preceded you from your fathers were in the time of the one who was the king of the province before me in various kinds of trials and sorts of calamities and hardships due to the evil of his kingdom and his fear in dealings and his deviation from soundness in the plans of his policy and the customs of his emirate, while you today are in the shade of safety and under the wing of security and in the embrace of ease and comfort by the blessing of my policy, my compassion, and my mercy upon the classes of my subjects and the people of my kingdom,' he does not intend by that the specific day in which he addresses them with that, but rather his entire era and the time of the establishment of his authority and his dynasty. So likewise is this. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a detailed interpretation of the verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' (Quran 5:3), aiming to explain away the apparent implication that the religion was incomplete before that specific day. Nasafi first states that the interpretation has several aspects. The first is that the word 'today' does not refer to a literal specific day, just as in the verse 'And I am pleased with Islam as a religion for you' (Quran 5:3 part), the phrase 'today' is not meant literally; rather, the religion was already pleasing to God before that. Then he splits this into two sub-aspects. The first sub-aspect is to completely drop the literal meaning of 'today' and treat it as a customary opening phrase without specifying actual time. The second sub-aspect is to understand 'today' as referring to the entire era of the Prophet (peace be upon him), because before that there was a period of intermission (fatrat) when no prophet was sent, and there was a need for a clear guide. So God sent His Messenger in that era to clarify the truth. Nasafi supports this with an analogy: a king might say to his subjects 'you today are in safety' but he means his whole reign, not just that particular day. Thus, the verse indicates that the religion was perfected over the entire prophetic era, not that it was imperfect earlier. He concludes with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that 'today' in the verse refers to a literal 24-hour day, implying that Islam was imperfect before that day.
Correction: Nasafi shows that 'today' is either a rhetorical opening or refers to the entire prophetic era, so the perfection of religion was not a sudden event but occurred over time.

Checkpoint
What is the first interpretation given by Nasafi for the word 'today' in the verse?
A) It refers to a specific day.
B) It is used as an opening expression without specifying time.
C) It refers to the era of the Prophet.
D) It refers to the Day of Judgment.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that the perfection of religion in the verse occurred over the Prophet's entire mission, not just a single day.
